THE RED RAGE.
Arthur A. 1). Bayldon writes regarding Byro
and JLionel Johnson’s review—
In spite of an obvious parade of culture and
style declamatory rather than critical, Johnson
review of Byron is excellent. In his eagerness t
be felt, however, he is narrow. “Emphatically
Byron was no poet ; not if Shakespeare and Milton
are poets. If poetry be the product of imagina
tion, Byron has certainly produced it—of which
anon.
* * . ,
Johnson has raised a swarm of critics about him
most of them puny but virulent. He is abusec
for a candid criticism which, with some deductions
to which I shall refer, will be the final verdict for
Byron. With the false Ruskinism and the pelting
of garbage to which Johnson’s critics have de
seended, I have nothing to do. A critic has
right to be heard, whatever his opinion, providec
he is competent. Many of Johnson’s critics are
incompetent. Their style is as barren as their
thoughts. What have we to do with the strangled
howl of mountain torrents, with sti earning clouds
and shadows, and all the theatrical imagery of
mountain looming in solitary grandeur above some
insignificant mime picking pebbles from its base
We want facts not hysterical fancies: plain
prose, not long lumbering sentences clipped of
riiyme. Why shouldn’t a critic rub it into
Byron if he deserves it? And even if Byron
doesn t he would get only what he gave others.
He condemned Wordsworth and reproduced him •
sharpened his intellect at Shelley’s grindstone
and kept a stolid silence as to his genius : hit off
Heats with a pungent couplet that slandered him •
poured out the vials of his distempered vanitv
over Coleridge ; and was most objectionably insin-
cere and heartless with his friends. It is very
well to read Moore’s life of him—but what of his
treatment of Hunt? But it’s not of Byron the
man, but of Byron the poet, that Johnson writes.
How is Byron s day of reckoning, and he is fear-
lessly weighed in the scales. Moreover, because a
cntic dabbles m verse he is no less a critic. And
Johnson s criticism of Byron is the subject of dis-
cussion, not an invidious comparison betwixt the
men as poets—which fact Johnson’s critics should
remember.
* * • *
Johnson says Byron could do many things, but
could not sing. This is true, but pushes nothing
against Byron as a poet. ’Tis strange what be-
trayals of ignorance about the distinctions in verse
one finds among even good critics. A singer is
not necessarily a poet, nor a poet a singer. A
man can be an excellent craftsman and be neither
poet nor singer The greates t poets have the three
faculties in perfection. Burns will ultimately be
dismissed to the choir of singers : he was really
not a poet at all, but a great humorist and satirist

In utmost leisureliness the great sun droops
towards the horizon. With ruddy opulence he
fe Slot t d ° Wn to tbe ver fe re through a cloud-

I rom the slanted beams this meadow-grass takes

the church windows send forth an intermittent
white dazzle: at intervals, also, the gracious notes
of the organ float hither on the still air. And
now the sun has gone, but the western sky is still
abrim with his mo ted beams as the worshippers
trickle forth from the stifled church into the clean
and sacred out-of-doors. Across the benign field-
paths the people straggle with an air of relief yet
each one, despite his devotions, takes
a grotesque and persistent shadow.
* * * »
Night, the stately and simple night, now passes
over the land Westward, the thrSbbing gold has
become a dead waste of pallid sky ; the pile rose-
passed from the grass; the flame in the

here To loiter down this lane, enfolded by the
friendly dark, is to be overtaken by an incom-

THE RETURN.
Now at last I am at home—
Wind abeam and flooding tide,
And the offing white with foam,
And an old friend by my side
Glad the long, green waves to ridei
Strange how we’ve been wandering
Through the crowded towns for gain,
You and I who loved the sting
Of the salt spray and the rain
And the gale across the main !
What world honors could avail
Loss of this—the slanted mast,
And the roaring round the rail,
And the sheeted spray we cast
Round us as we seaward passed ?
As the sad land sinks apace,
With it sinks each thought of
Think not now of aging face;
Question not the whitening hair :
Youth still beckons everywhere.
And the light we thought had fled
From the skydine glows there nowj
Bends the same blue overhead ;
And the waves we used to plough
Part in beryl at the bow.
Hours like this we two have known
In the old days, when we sailed
Seaward ere the night had flown,
Or the morning star had paled
Like the shy eyes love has veiled.
Round our bow the ripples purled,
swift tide onward streamed
Through a hushed and ghostly world,
Where our harbor-reaches seemed
Like a river that we dreamed.
Then we saw the black hills sway
In the water’s crinkled glass,
And the village wan and gray,
And the startled cattle pass
Through the tangled meadow-grass.
Through the glooming we have run
Straight into the gates of day,
Seen the crimson-edged sun
Bum the sea’s gray bound away—
Leap to universal sway.
Little cared we where we drove
So the wind was strong and keen.
Oh, what sun-crowned waves we clove!
What cool shadows lurked between
Those long combers pale and green ! ■
Graybeard pleasures are but toys;
Sorrow shatters them at last:
For this brief hour we are boys ;
Trim the sheet and face the blast j
Sail into the happy past!
L. F. Tooker.

Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms :
Do your facil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down ?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back?
Have you pains in head ?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued ?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch ?
Do your hands tremble ?
Do you feel shaky ?
Can you walk without halting ?
Are you melancholy ?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim?
Nervous Debility-
This affliction destroys ambi-
tion, organic strength, energy,
and hope. Following are some
of the symptoms
Do you feel weak?
Have you cold feet?
Have you backache ?
Do you shun society ?
Are you losing flesh ?
Do you sleep poorly ?
Are you low-spirited?
Are your eyes sunken?
Do you have hot flushes?
Is your memory impaired?
Have you no manly vigor l
Have you no vital energy.
Do you have sick headaches /
Is there nausea after eating?

Spring-time Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Spin of the Coin.
The Spring is warm and waking, and the wattle’s
bursting bud,
And the longing of the rover makes a fever in the
blood ;
The grass is growing swiftly in the sheltered river-
bends,
And the Bush, our old coy lover, waits to kiss us
and make friends ;
And the yearning is upon us to be somewhere
and away,
If it’s but to tilt at windmills, as a careless
Quixote may.
And since it matters little if we ride to North or
South,
To the reeling desert dust-showers or the rocking
harbor mouth,
Let’s toss our last half-sovereign and the spinning
coin shall say:
If it’s heads, we start to-morrow;' if it’s tails, we
start to-day;
And heads shall be for Sydney Heads, fair wind
and ocean tide,
And tails for tailing weaners on the Diamantina
side!
For Spring is close and coming : you can hear her
rustling wings
And her thousand-throated murmur—never music
like the Spring’s !
Her hand is in the rover’s and her lips to his are
pressed;
She is all a-fire and eager, and she will not let us
rest
Till our hand’s upon the bridle and our foot’s upon
the bar.
And our face is to the freedom of the storm-wind
and the star 1
Long luck to every rover—to the west or Sydney
Side,
With the blue Sea for a lover or the brown Bush
for a bride.
If they mount with merry laughter, may they
never taste of woe !
If they take the track in sorrow, may they gladden
as they go !
There's a free lance down the Lachlan with their
roving ranks will join
At the bidding of the Springtide and the spinning
of a coin !
N.S.W.
WILL. H. OGILVIE.
Your Hand in Mine.
Melody mingled of shadow and sheen
Over the fields of our fairyland rung,
Laughter came liquidly welling between
Converse of love as we wandered and sung,
Crowned with the crown of a dalliance divine,
Heart of my heart —with your hand in mine !
Murmur of mountainous thunders afar
Broke in a peal o’er our path as it rose ;
Steep was the stair of the hills to our star,
Keen was the blast as it blew from the snows ;
What did we reck—we were drunk with Love’s
wine,
Heart of my heart—with your hand in mine !
Even comes now in her garment of grey,
Tenderly greets us descending again.
O’er the brown hills we have battled our way
Down to the verge of the Ultimate Main ;
Hark to yon call in the breaking of brine 1
Heart of my heart —is your hand in mine ?
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Foot o’ Velvet.
Foot o'velvet, foot o'velvet, foot o’ velvet comes the
Spring /
By the running creeks and, rivers, where the
mountain-waters sing
And the dandelions sleeping dream awhile of
blossoming,
Foot o’ velvet, foot o’ velvet, foot o' velvet comes the
Spring /
Foot o’ velvet, foot o’ velvet, foot o’ velvet comes
the Spring
As a monarch to a kingdom with his heralds
trumpeting;
For across the hills and valleys new-fledged choirs
a-carolling
Gladly to the heart of Nature in their sweetest
love-songs sing—
“ Foot o’ velvet, foot o’ velvet, fool o’ velvet comes the
Spring l ”
Ah, the tale is growing hoary
In the book of Father Time !
But each year with verdant glory
Sings the Spring his youthful rhyme}
And we know that where he goeth
Down the pathway of the old,
All our better nature showeth
Through an aureole of gold ;
Still the thoughts of youth and maiden
To the old, old story cling,
And its message, perfume laden —
Spring is Love and Love is Spring /
Foot ■o’ velvet, foot o’ velvet, foot o’ velvet comes
the Spring!
As a maiden greets her lover when, in youth’s
awakening,
Floods of passion tints of ruby to her firm white
bosoms bring—
so the trees, aglow with glory, all their young
breasts blossoming,
. Vhisper to the yearning sunlight—“ Foot o' velvet
comes the Spring ! ”
Foot o’ velvet, foot o’ velvet, foot o’ velvet comes the
Spring 1

mountain-waters sing
A.ni the dandelions sleeping dream awhile of
blossoming,

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to yon every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
Whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Blaclc and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
W estralia and iffnor Hand.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Inter provincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage. England £l, with
6s. 6d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

Owing to the imposition of l\d. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage — l jot. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight — now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
SATURDAY, AUGUST 27, 1898.
Australia versus Reid.
Colonel Crockett —the man the coon came
down for—had a hustings story of a plough-
man whose master instructed him to “ plough
across the field to the red cow.” He began
to plough, and she began to walk ; and he
ploughed all morning after her. When the
farmer came to see how the work was getting
on, he swore at the man for going so crooked.
“ Well,” said the man “ I ploughed straight
for the red cow ; but she always kept moving !”
And that’s precisely what’s the matter with
Australian Federation. We have all of us
•—New South Wales, Victoria, South Aus-
tralia, Westralia, Tasmania ploughed
straight after the Reid cow ; but she always
keeps moving !
• * * »
So the Federal track is like a corkscrew in
convulsions. Premier Reid has decided at
different times that there ought to be no
amalgamation of debts and railways, that
debts and railways should certainly be amal-
gamated, that debts and railways ought only
to be partly amalgamated, with numerous
drawbacks and conditions. Having changed
from an open enemy of Federation to a
lukewarm friend, he now announces himself
a life-and-death ally, and proves his good
faith by attaching himself to the self-styled
“ arch-destroying angel of Federation,” and
refusing to let go on any account. Having
declared that “this great question” must
be approached in the true spirit of concilia-
tion, he translates the “true spirit” into
assertions that if New South Wales doesn’t
get her particular wants satisfied she can't
federate, and into friendly declarations that
the delegate of another Federating province is
an old fool, with other nice, amicable remarks
of the kind. Approving “ simple majority
rule,” he defies the 5000 majority which New
South Wales gave at the polls; and, in
order to carry out “ the will of the greater
number,” he proceeds to alter the Bill to
suit the supposed wishes of the minority.
Because the Convention Bill was unsatisfac-
tory he voted for it, and now that he has
voted for it he finds it bad beyond redemp-
tion. Because but it is needless to con-
tinue : who can put faith in such an
obviously insincere trickster ?
* * * *
The mischief of it is that to this trickster
we are tied. Federation or confederation
without New South Wales is practically im-
possible ; and, while the vote of the whole
province would have unquestionably returned
Barton to lead the federal cause in New
South Wales Parliament, the vote of King
electorate returned Reid. There never
was a better illustration of Hare’s
thesis that the small - electorate system
means the triumph of small minds
and mean motives, the glorification of
the huckster-politician at the expense of the
patriot. And the small-electorate system is
responsible for the continuance of the Reid
Government in office, though there was cast
against them a large majority of votes.
Clearly New South Wales Assembly does
not adequately represent the opinions'of the
electors. Nevertheless, Premier Reid, as the
representative of the minority, will—thanks
to the splitting of Opposition votes in the
small electorates—usurp the functions of a
majority-representative ; and in his nominal
headship his actual position at the tail of the
elections will be forgotten. And this is
Representative Government !
* * * 9
There is nothing but to make the best of
it. Likely enough Premier Reid will im-
prove his position as Parliament continues.
At his command he has the ..immense force
of Ministerial patronage—and a trickier
diplomacy than even Parkes’s. For while
Parkes occasionally forgot, in pride in him-
self, or in contempt for others, or in the fas-
cination of a great idea, the petty arts neces-
sary to conserve and increase a majority,
Premier Reid never forgets. Like the ser-
pent of Eden, on his belly he goes all the
days of his life—flattering here, bribing
there, never making an enemy, never mis-
sing an opportunity of. turning an enemy
into a friend. The Labor-party claims to
exact “support in return for concessions,”
though all the concessions it has gained
from Premier Reid are Dead Sea fruit—an
inadequate Factories Act, an emasculated
Navigation Act, a reform of the nominee
Chamber which began with “ practical aboli-
tion,” and is whittled away to unimportant
alterations of office-tenure. Premier Reid
is only too pleased to give such “conces-
sions ”in return for support. Were he com-
pelled, he would go further, and give real con-
cessions, if he could in no other way maintain
himself in office. But he recognises that
there is no use in waking sleeping dogs when
the Labor-party is satisfied with the pretence
of it. Having achieved office, to stay in
office is the end to which Premier Reid
wriggles and crawls and subordinates every-
thing. At the bottom of his mind he has
something which he calls “my princi-
ples,” but they are so vague and
elastic, and defer so accommodatingly to
circumstances, that they might as well be
cold pudding. .Understanding that, with
one-man-one-vote in force, the trend of
policy must be Liberal rather than Conser-
vative, Premier Reid is a judicious Liberal;
but he has learnt that, like the Labor-party,
the people may be fed on ten parts of pro-
fession and theory to one of practice.
Really, the only important result of Premier
Reid's four years’ tenure of office is that
Premier Reid is still in office. For, though
he veer like a weathercock or the Yicar of
Bray, he is absolutely determined that
“ still I will Premier stay, sir ! ”
* * * »
And upon this “rock”—this quicksand,
this quagmire, this thin crust of ice labelled
“Jolly and Fat—the Australian people is
asked to build its Federal church. On
Premier Reid everything hinges. . Having
wrecked the Convention Bill in New South
Wales apparently through jealousy of
Barton, to whom he played second fiddle at
the Convention election and in the Conven-
tion ; for if Reid had no personal motive
his contradictions are incompatible with
sanity Reid proposes to re-create the
measure his own hand destroyed. (There
can be no doubt that, had Premier Reid
spoken as he voted—had he even spoken to
the New South Wales electors as he spoke to
the Convention—the majority-vote would
have reached the figure prescribed by Par-
liament. But Premier Reid made assurance
doubly sure : he drowned “ Barton’s Bill ”
by agreeing to the 80,000 minimum ; and he
knocked it on the head at the polls). So
that we have the singular spectacle of this
eye-glassed trickster—this dignified Premier
who raves “You are a lot of skunks ! ” at
his opponents —this representative of a mass-
electoral minority— defying the majority-vote
of all five provinces, and proposing, instead
of the majority-Bill, to ram down the throat
of Australia a Bill with his own image and
superscription !
* * * *
Premier Reid is the man who killed the
Commonwealth Bill approved by a majority
of electors in six provinces. In its stead he
is conceiving—what ? He did not tell the
New South Wales electors ; he did not tell
Parliament in the Governor’s speech ; but
his amendments to the Commonwealth Bill
are to be so obviously just, are so obviously
to improve upon the wisdom of the Conven-
tion, that the Government of every province
will have no hesitation in quashing the
majority-vote at the Referendum and sub-
mitting the amended measure to the elec-
tors. And the Parliaments which ap-
proved another Bill are to approve this
one; and the electors who voted for
another Bill are to vote for this
one ; and the “ just rights ” of New
South Wales, as understood by Premier
Reid and a minority of New South Wales
electors, are to be admitted by every other
province and bya New South Wales majority-
vote without quarrel or dissent. It is not
to be wondered at if this marvel has cost
Premier Reid some pangs. The birth is
fixed for the end of the debate on the
Address-in-Reply—and The Bulletin, in
going to press on Tuesday, August 23,
awaits the event with hopeful despair.
The A.J.C. and Pony-Racing.
The A. J.C. has been in existence over 70 years—
it was established 1828. Its ruling spirits have
always been prominent among the Tory Cab-
bage-Tree mob. Until actually compelled, such
a body will not try to move with the times, and
then the attempt naturally ends in ludicrous
failure. At the Club’s recent annual meeting,
regret was expressed that “ the character of
the sport it was established to uphold ” had
deteriorated, and the blame was put down to
the proprietary clubs—with “ two honorable
exceptions,” about which there has never been
anything exceptional, as the S.M. Herald,
The Bulletin and other papers have already
shown. He who lets the rill trickle past his
feet must not attempt to dam the navigable
river. With foresight the deadly upas-tree
which cumbereth the ground may be nipped
in the bud. All these proprietary clubs, when
they have been recognised at all, have grown
up with the sanction, not the encouragement,
of the A.J.C., which, in now condemning their
shortcomings, merely testifies to its own folly
in the past.
* * * *
Pony-racing, denounced as the- source from
which all the turf villainy of the present day
has sprung, was virtually unknown in Sydney
10 years ago. Sydney Driving Park was pro-
fessedly more devoted to trotting than to any
other branch of racing. Kensington, Rose-
bery Park, Lillie Bridge, and Brighton clubs
were then undreamt of. What is more, they
have never been under A.J.C. rules, nor have
they ever wished to be. Why do they now
attract the reforming zeal and virtuous indig-
nation of the boss club? Everyone admits that
there are too many race-meetings and that
the multiplication of clubs has aggravated the
evil. But. is it not a fact that the A. J.C. itself,
instead of curtailing, has been the chief sinner
by adding to the number of its own racing
dates ? It is suggestive that not until “ money
had been lost on the ‘ off’ meetings” and “ the
club’s meetings on the whole had been carried
on at a loss during the year,” did the senior
body rise against the rival caterers for gate-
money sport and denounce their iniquity. This
railing against the “ sport of spielers,” and “a
degraded form of racing ” now smacks of
jealousy and disappointment. Having vainly
reached out for some of the spoil without which
pony-racing would not last a month, the A. J.C.
cries “ sour grapes ! ”
* * * *
Whatever its motives, the A.J.C.’s proposed
methods must prove abortive. Finding itself
impotent to act directly against the pony-
racing clubs, it announces its intention of get-
ting at them through the trainer and the,
bookmaker, thus crediting these hitherto
despised classes with being the real props on
which rests the sport of kings—and other
thieves. Briefly, the new rules say : “(1) -Amy
trainer of a pony shall be disqualified from
running under A. J.C. auspices. (2) No book-
maker, under pain of disqualification, shall bet
at a pony meeting.” With the experience of
the land laws and licensing Acts before them, a
set of schoolboys, not to speak of men who
must possess more than an average know-
ledge of life’s seamy side, should see the
futility of such regulations. Let them
be enforced, and the present partnership
between the “noble owner” and the
astute trainer will be instantly dissolved. The
“noble owner” will become trainer of his
own “ horse,” while the acute trainer will
own ponies and give them undivided atten-
tion. The Bulletin’s friend, Ikey Mo,
will resolve into his component parts— lkey
attending to horses and Mo to ponies—
taking care, however, that the gains in one de-
partment of the partnership shall counter-
balance the losses of the other. The regulation
extends to bookmakers’ clerks—presumably to
prevent Mo, Ikey’s clerk at Randwick, from
taking the bag and giving Ikey the pencil—
directly they cross over to Kensington. But
clerical labor is the cheapest of all in the
market, and this limitation will have little re-
sult.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
No More Minimum.
Premier Reid declares, first, that there must
be another Referendum on Federation; and,
second, that at the Referendum the issue will
be decided by a simple majority—there will be
no 80,000 minimum majority next time. So
that, if Premier Reid can formulate his altera-
tions in the Convention Bill—and it is very un-
certain whether he can, seeing he does not know
his own Yes-No mind for two weeks together ;
and N.S.W. Parliament accepts them; and
IE the other Parliaments decide to override
the popular vote, and deal with them, and
accept them; and IF, by a miracle, N.S.
W ales electors are put before a new
Convention Bill in the same way as they were
put before the old one, and with the same
powers, and the same understanding with the
other provinces;—then Premier Reid announces
that if one vote more is given for the new Bill
than is given against it, the Bill will be carried,
and N.S.W. will federate gladly, while stout
little angels with stubby noses sleep all over
the premises in token of heartfelt con-
gratulation. At the original Referendum
71,000 electors voted for the Bill and 66,000
against it; and Premier Reid and Sydney
Telegraph declared with a loud voice that the
Bill had been hopelessly damned by popular
disapproval. At next Referendum, if 66,001
electors vote for the new Bill and 66,000 against
it—or if 100,001 vote for and 100,000 against—
or if only 50,001 vote for and 50,000 against—
Premier Reid and the Telegraph will fall
upon the neck of N.S.W. and declare the Bill
triumphantly carried by the vote of the Demo-
cratic majority. The fact that the Democratic
majority clearly signified their wishes last time,
regarding another Bill, and in the way which
Premier Reid now approves, will not be taken
into consideration. Jim Crow has jumped, and
everybody else must jump along with him. It
is true that this is only Premier Reid’s this-
week attitude, and his next-week attitude will
probably be something altogether different.
But, till further notice, that is the beautifully
Democratic position where ’e are. :
An Upper-House Poet.
Many a man who subtends a large angle in
the public vision and enjoys an easy frequency
on the public tongue, only needs to put pen to
•paper to make a holy show of himself.
“ Brains, has he? ” says the experienced editor.
“Urn!—let me handle his screed!” When
N.S.W. legislator W. T. Trickett (M.L.C.)
set out, the other day, on a S.S. Islands
pleasure-trip, and was, in virtue of his bulk
and “dignity,” elected Grand-Panjandrum of
the company of 120 “ nice ” people. He
“went-down all right” as long as lie
stuck to his accustomed field of talkee-
talkee. But pride goeth before destruction ;
in the intoxication of success the wretched
man took unto him a loaded pen, and—in the
guileless phrase of Sydney D.T.’s “ special
correspondent ” “ composed the attached
poetry ” :
Since the melodious Trickett, however, is a
member of the Upper House, it is perhaps un-
reasonable to expect figs from thistles ; and, in
herewith redeeming this noble effort from local
obscurity, The Bulletin places it on record,
not only as a sample of the Colossal intellect
that marks the auguster end of the legislative
machine, but also as yet another example of
what may happen to a man who doesn’t “ lay
to his book ! ” That W.J.T. lamentably lacks
verse-skill is nothing—many an ass can rhyme
and tinkle, but that a maker of this “ great
country’s ” laws should be willing thus to
parade his penury of ideas is a matter for the
prompt interference of his friends—if, after
such a metrical outburst, he happens to have
any left.
4.
Marshall-Hall Resigns.
There is little doubt that Marshall-Hall’s
resignation of his professorship of music at
Melb. University was practically forced on him;
and, if so, the University Council does not
come creditably out of the broil. Marshall-
Hall’s offence was an error of taste, an error
in judgment; but it was a venial offence, and
might well have been condoned and forgotten.
The accusation of “impiety” and. “inde-
cency ” displayed a bigot’s ignorance, and was
levelled with a bigot’s characteristic malice.
Marshall-Hall has a right to hold what
opinions he chooses ; and there was nothing in
the manner of expression of his opinions which
could gravely offend any literate or fair-minded
person. The sole issue was whether his Uni-
versity professorship should have restrained his
hand; and on this point Marshall-Hall
argues plausibly that the theoretical University
should be the greatest champion of freedom of
thought. Very likely ; but it is the practical
University which is in question, and the prac-
tical logic of things as they are. Given even
that all Marshall-Hall’s opinions are solid
and valid, they are a generation ahead of the
mass of opinions in Victoria ; and if Melb.
University goes ahead too fast it will probably
after all make less progress than by going
slowly. To many Victorians Marshall-Hall
appears a hare-brained, harum-scarum kind of
professor. Would Melb. University profit if
brought into perfect conformity with the
ideas of Marshall-Hall, and recognised
as a hare-brained, harum-scarum kind of
University ? Would Victoria profit ? The
Bulletin does not think so. This paper is of
opinion that, to reach its maximum of useful-
ness, a national educational institution like a
University should give their due to both
Orthodox and Heterodox ; and, while honoring
freedom of thought, should restrict its own
freedom of action so as not violently to ruv
When our good ship’s far at sea,
And her strong prow cleaves the main,
Three hundred miles each day we go,
And think of friends and home ;
We think of the lands we’re going to,
And the dear old lands we’ve lost,
And we think of “ terra firma ”
When we get a little tossed.
Our captain is a master hand,
In skill and manners too—
A wise and careful pastor of
A brave and able crew.
Waikare is as good a craft
As ever ploughed the sea,
Although she rolls a bit too much
For landsmen such as me.
We’ve lawyers and we’ve doctors,
And men of each degree,
Yet never has a passenger
To fork them out a fee.
Our tables have a generous spread
Whene’er we go below ;
Our “Beveridge ” is the very best
Upon the Union Co.
Among our list of gentlemen
Are seen much more than one
Of monster men that ever yet
The sun did shine upon.
Our pretty girls they gambol
As sweetly on we go ;
There never were such darlings yet
Upon the Union Co.
Then here’s to ship and captain,
Our officers and crew,
And here, my lady passengers,
A double praise to you
Who cheered us on by smile and song
When we were not “ so-so” ;
We’ll ne’er forget what you have done
Upon the Union Co.


counter to the ideas or prejudices of the people
whom it aims to benefit. Quite possibly
Marshall-Hall could establish a better case
for another kind of University which would be
a pioneer of thought—not a translator, accumu-
lator, and conservator ; but it would not be the
University we know, it could not be the Uni-
versity of Melbourne.
Tlie New Guinea Steal.
Premiers Reijo, Byrnes, and Turner, in con-
ference in Sydney, have decided to protest once
more against the New Guinea steal. Chamber-
lain now cables that the steal is consummated,
and it is too late for disallowance ! Since the
Premiers have already used their thunder and
failed to move the man from Brummagem, pro-
bably a more effective protest would have been
to pass a condemnatory address to the Queen
through four Parliaments, and cable the joint
resolution. '1 hat course would so impress the
English newspapers that Mr. Chamberlain
might be persuaded to bend his stiff
neck. The admonition of the Premiers
has nob quite Parliamentary force, and
may be disregarded. It certainly will be dis-
regarded if there is the least excuse for doing
so. The Influence behind the grab is appa-
rently the biggest Influence in Britain, bar one.
Chamberlain’s wishes were conveyed to Gov.
Macgregor almost in the form of a command.
Lowles and Somers Vine had Australian vice-
regal backing ; and Nelson was talked to in
London. Some amount of information he
could hardly avoid giving to his colleagues ;
but he gave them as little as he could. Ex-
Premier Nelson’s whole conduct in the matter
was the shady conduct which, since the
Q.N. Bank scandal, the public have learnt
to expect from him. And the matter
still rests in suspense. Whatever the issue,
the whole question of the relation of New r
Guinea to Australia needs to be considered.
The present control —under which the contri-
buting provinces pay that foreign syndicates
may have the privilege of looting the country
at the expense of Australian pioneers—is radi-
cally unsatisfactory. Premier Byrnes suggests
that New Guinea should be handed over to
Queensland. Unluckily the present Q. Govt,
does not inspire confidence ; and its planter-
friends are firmly wedded to the idea of swamp-
ing Queensland with black New-Guinea labor.
New Guinea may profit at the expense of
Queensland. Besides, Queensland is over-big
already. If she cannot satisfactorily rule the
North, how can she rule a country farther away
than the North ? The alternative, that New
Guinea should become a Crown colony, seems
even less desirable. Pending Federation, pro-
bably the best thing to do is to continue the
present control, with alterations that will
make it thoroughly effective. After Federa-
tion, could not New Guinea become a Com-
monwealth colony ?
Britain and Europe.
The British Empire, it would appear, is near-
ing the zenith of the hyperbolic curve of its
destiny—the keystone of its welkin, so to
speak. Then must follow the downward,
earthward, spent-force collapse. Empires can’t
overcome the law of gravitation, or the limit of
vitality anj r more than can individuals. . The
events of the last few' years point unmistak-
ably to a hostile concert against the mother
country. The “ dogged, cold, exclusive temper
which has accomplished so much has at the
same time estranged the friendship of her
neighbors. She is the bond-holder of the
W’orld, the centre of a bowelless commer-
cialism, which has neither body to be
kicked nor soul to be damned. Her
world-wide interests, the very strength and
extent of her imperial dominion, impede her
action. It is the corpulence of pursy pro-
sperity. And round her are waiting, hungrily
watching for spoil, the mailed fists and pre-
datory eagles of Europe. Englishmen laugh
at the idea of a hostile combination. They
point to the “silver streak,” they insist on
the hopelessness of reconciling the race-hatreds
and traditional feuds of the continental powers.
But no thoughtful observer can be blind to the
fact that if England is not involved in the
family jars of Europe it is because she has
come to be looked upon as outside the family,
as at best “ a bad European,” a greedy inter-
loper, a disturber of the balance of power,
against whose unwieldly bulk may well be em-
ployed the swarming battalions of Europe,
finding as rich a prey as was found in Spain by
the mediaeval buccaneers. It has been pointed
out that the history of the last few years shows
a succession of hostilities against England from
all sides and in all quarters, none the less
aggressive, none the less dangerous, than if
they had been conducted in actual war-
fare. Look at the intrigues of France
in Egypt, in Siam, in Nigeria, in Mada-
gascar, or the Nile; look at the en-
croachments of Russia in Persia, Afghanis-
tan, the Pamirs ; look at the charaeterically
prudent and cautious, but none the less real,
hostility of Germany in the Transvaal, in Zan-
zibar, in Samoa j look to-day at the unanimous
exultation of the Continent at the blows which
are being struck at the prestige and commercial
interests of England in the East. Everything
seems to show that England must either make
ignoble submission to a European aggressive-
ness which seeks to bleed her by fair means or
foul, or take the risk of financial ruin, of possi-
ble effacement as a first-class Pow r er, in a
desperate contest with the monster armies of
her neighbors, to whom she appears as the one
great obstacle to their designs. It is a choice
between swindling and highway robbery.
Beak-made Law.
The report of N.S.W. Inspector-General of
Police on the licensing business transacted in
the metropolitan area during the year ’97 show s
that while the number of publicans’ licenses
remained stationary there was a large increase
in native-wine licenses—a very healthy sign
from a temperance as well as asocial and econo-
mic standpoint. At the close of the year the
publicans’ licenses in existence totalled 789 —
the same as in 1896 ; colonial wine licenses,
292—an increase of 26. Instead of rejoicing,
the powers-that-be are doing all they can to
restrain this healthy change in public taste
the Sydney Licensing Benches have decreed
that no more new Australian wine licenses
shall be issued except to grocers and res-
taurant-keepers! This move seems to have
been made for the purpose of protecting
the British race from the competition of tlie
“ dirty foreigner ” who sells fruit and con-
fectionery, and, acting according to his tradi-
tions, wants to sell wine too. Dozens of
applications have been refused during the past
year, and hundreds, probably, have been
deterred from making application at all by ie
knowledge that to do so would be useless. As
Thu Bulletin has already noted, Brick,
M.L.A. and solicitor, in his legal capacity,
pointed out, last April, that the object of tne
Legislature was clearly to offer all reasonable
facilities for—nay, to actually encourage—the
consumption of locally-grown wine, and lie then
promised to do his best, in his leg's a IV ®
capacity, to make the intention of Parliament
more clearly understood. Meanwhile, Parlia-
ment has proposed, and the beak disposes.

The Vic. Mine Tributor.
There are nine-and-sixty ways of solving the
Vic. mine-tributing problem, and every one of
them is wrong. The \ ic. Parliament incor-
porated about half of the nine-ana-sixty in the
Mines Act of last year, and some people com-
pute that already the number of unemployed
ex-tributors at Bendigo alone exceeds bOO. the
boss body of the Miners’ Association, looking at
the matter theoretically, decided some time
back to approve the Act, and to thank those
who had secured its passage ; yet now the Ben-
digo branch of the Miners’ Association can pi in-
duce only two men to speak in its favoi. Bo
sooner have you got your solution ready than,
no matter how finished an appearance it has,
some poor, forgotten fossicker in the recesses of
the Poverty Extended feebly complains that
you will surely flatten him out ; or the
Poverty Extended complains on its own account.
Tributers are of all sorts ; and so are tributois.
The brainiest of miners, and the most experi-
enced and most active and most vigorous of
them, work this w'ay ; and so do the most
foolish and inexperienced and slovenly and
lazy and broken-winded. The latter work on
tribute simply because they can't get wages-
work to do ; the others work that way because
wages are not enough for them. So also some
tributes are let because accidentally a fraction
may be left to the company which knows very
well that the ground cannot be profitably
worked on wages ; while others are let because
it seems the cheapest w'ay to get intelligent
prospecting done. But at all Limes the tributor
is a speculator ; for the time he takes over part
of the risks and part of whatever prospective
profits there are from the shareholders. As to
these shareholders the same problem which is
now worrying the tributors long ago cropped
up ; and the State, while not professing to solve
it, threw them a sop to end their clamour.
That sop is the Prospecting Vote. The
State will not, however, go so far in its rela-
tions with the tributor ; but in Victoria it says
that, when the tributor gets very poor results,
he shall be entitled to all the gold he wins up
to <£l 2s. 6d. a week. “ With the prospecting
vote,” it says, in effect, “we help you when
you, our lessee, get in low water ; now you
help your sub-lessee (for the tributor is in that
position) when he gels in low-water. It seems
a fair arrangement so far, anyhow. But, then,
the lessee has to incur actual expense foi
hauling and so on—through the employment of
the tributor ; while the State is not put to any
expense to speak of through the working of a
leasehold. Besides, before a grant is made
from the prospecting vote the claim is inspected
by a Govt, expert and its prospects are con-
sidered ; if they are not good enough, the
money is not “advanced.” At least, that
is the theory of the thing. With tributes,
though, the lessee goes to no such trouble;
all that he satisfies himself about mostly
is that the particular block will not pay
him to work on his own account. The tributor
takes the block either because he thinks he
knows more about it than the lessee does, or
because he must get some sort of a living some-
how. If the lessee had to certify that more
than £1 2s. 6d. per man per week could be
made (and that is about what the sustenance-
wage provision means, unless he is prepared to
do the hauling, &c., for nothing), he would do
two things : first pick his men, and then pick
his ground. This is just what he is doing.
The provision in practice amounts to one fixing
a minimum wage ; and that tributors are specu-
lators, who hope for reward far exceeding the
average wage, is a pretty fair argument against
the application of a minimum wage in their
particular case. After considering all this, and
a good deal more, The Bulletin then comes
to the conclusion that there isn’t any solution
to the problem at all, chiefly because it isn’t
one problem, but about as many problems as
there are tribute “ pitches” in all the mines or
blocks that might, in certain conceivable circum-
stances, become tribute “ pitches.
Bias problems suggested by the Marshall
Hall case :—Should Murray Smith, a trustee
for the Argus proprietary, sit in Univ. Coun-
cil in judgment on a man whom the Argus has
so truculently abused that the failure of its
attack might mean a great financial injury to the
paper ? Is a functionary who has been mor-
tally offended with a teacher for an explana-
tion of a school-exercise given to the function-
ary’s daughter, and whose nephew has written
a bitter letter to the press denouncing said
teacher for another, but distinctly analogous,
matter, competent to sit in judgment at any
stage of proceedings in reference to the last-
mentioned matter 5 Is a University council, m
which church colleges are over-represented,
and in which Bishop Goe has a seat, competent
to decide whether certain verses in a volume
are immodest, when a large part of the volume
is devoted to trenchant criticism and contemp-
tuous pasquinading of Christianity ? I s a
council, which professedthat it couldnt consider
the question of dismissing a professor for alleged
long-standing drunkenness, competent to dis-
miss a professor for alleged impiety ?
The more the Vic. Intestates Widows Relief
Act is studied the more sterling a piece of un-
pretentious but radical legislation it appears to
be. It is, indeed, probably the porch to the
temple of free justice. Under it public officers
aive all the advice necessary without fee or
reward ; ample directions are given by them as
to the proper manner of filling up the necessary
forms, which forms are provided by the
State ; the applicant herself or any
who can write legibly may fill up t e
forms, and, finally, probate or letters of ad-
ministration are issued to applicant without
the intervention of any variety of shark at any
stage of the business. The only fees payable
are the duty on the estate, the cost of swearing
the necessary affidavits, and, if the applicant
doesn’t do the engrossing of the probate per-
sonally, a small charge for that purpose. An
estate valued at £3OO will go through for about
j6l 11s 6<1.; whereas under the old system it
would be somewhere about £22. Is the private
lawyer necessary in any non-contentious legal
matters ? W ould not the State stop an enorm-
ous waste of hard-earned money by undertaking
all such business ilself?

“ Lignum” writes re the unemployed :
The present N.S.W. Government takes credit to it-
self that its era of Jolly and Fat has much reduced the
number of the unemployed so far as they appear in
latest report of the Labor Bureau. Having just returned
from a lengthy trip through the North and N. W. dis-
tricts, writer has had many chances of gauging the feel-
ino’ of employes and employers towards the Bureau. I he
former, especially bush-workers, regard the Bureau
stamp as “ pauper, scab and loafer hence the fact that
the country branches show comparatively few le-
gistrations, and certainly none from local unem-
ployed. Employers are down on Bureau labor as
being incompetent, and station managers “ pass it
on sight indeed, at two large stations are to
be seen notices —“ No Bureau men wanted. _ Taking
these facts into consideration, the Bureau statistics, as
indicators of the state of the labor-market, are worthless.
An advt. in a daily paper for labor is a better criterion
any day. The Govt. Labor Bureau, started by George
Dibbs as a means of bringing employer and employe
together, without the intervention of rapacious agents,
has altogether failed to benefit either party, and labor-
agencies thrive as of old. The only real service rendered
by it appears to be to country members, who occasionally
find it useful as a means of shunting impecunious con-
stituents back to their electorates on the fossicking
ticket. Another reason why the number of applications
at the Bureau is bound to diminish is that the man who
is helped out into the wilderness by that institution is
not eligible for further aid until he has repaid his railway
fare. If he hasn’t managed to do that then he has to
stay away, and thus help to reduce the number of appli-
cants, and to show how N.S.W. is prospering.
A Bill to make the ipse dixit of ignorant
“ honorarium ” justices the law of the land in
the majority of civil matters under £5 is being
introduced into Vic. Assembly by M‘Cay and
Gaik, each of whom ought to know better, and
by Outtrim. The intention is, of course, good
—viz., to prevent the unnecessary piling up of
costs in small matters not involving substantial
points of law or questions of jurisdiction. But
this end could be readily reached, without in-
justice, by limiting costs in such matters, both
at the hearing and on appeal, to an amount not
exceeding the sum recovered. (As a matter of
fact, the costs in all appeals by way of order to
review are disgracefully large, being seldom
less than £2O). But the cure proposed by this
Bill is worse than the disease. The number of
dishonest and utterly incompetent J’sP. is so
large that it is utterly unsafe to allow them
an unappealable discretion even in paltry
questions of fact. The right of interfering
with their decisions in matters of evidence is
seldom exercised ; but the whip is there, and
it prevents discretionary outrages. Take it
away, as this Bill proposes, and justice will
only be got in small civil matters when it
happens to coincide with the prejudices, busi-
ness interests, or social predilections of this
class of justices.
Should the Chinese embroglio culminate in
war, and Russia choose to make Chowdom the
seat of strife, what are England’s chances?
Russia has merely to pour troops through
Siberia and (provided China remains com-
plaisant) let an army of vast numerical strength
await the coming of Britain’s handful, already
thinned by the necessity for looking after
India and fifty other places. Siberia is now
one of the world’s great wheat-producing
tracts, and there would be little need for Russian
oversea transport. To fight in China is almost
out of the question ; Russia is a compact empire
without distant possessions to save ; and the
only alternative is direct attack upon her
shores. But she is steeled by constant inter-
national bickering, and is not likely to have let
her vaunted Kronstadt fall into decay—and
Bull’s only hope of successful attack from the
north would rest with Kaiser Wilhelm the
Mad. The late cheerful Bismarck measured
the value of that hope when he said that it
wasn’t Germany’s business to take a hand if
England was squashed between France and
Russia: she could afford to lie back and
“ watch the stiff-shirted lord howl for help to his
God.” And to the south the “sickman” would,
probably, reverse the old order, and Bull might
this time have to fight his way into the Black
Sea. And, even then, he would have no
greater hold upon the coveted Celestial empire.
Also the time will come when Britain, after
profiting more than once by international al-
liances, must herself fall victim to combination.

What a document might be made of a Go-
vernor’s Speech if a capable Premier chose !
Conceive the stir of a Declaration of Independ-
ence every three years —a.recital of endeavor,
a trumpet-call to action, a manifesto ablaze
with earnest, honest zeal for progress ! And
from such an ideal —to drop to the timid little
time-serving plaster issued by Premier Reid
last week ! An assurance that the Ministry
will strive for Federation—discounted by the
qualification that Federation is to be “on fair
and just lines,” which means anytliiug, every-
thing, nothing ; the reformation of the Marine
Board —which is obviously necessary and popu-
lar ; the milk-and-water draught for the Legisla-
tive' Council—which needs prussic acid ; the legal
practice Bill—which already the lawyers are
jeering at (though that may be a recommenda-
tion) ; the old local-government stalking-horse
and a new land-law stalking-horse ; Savings
Bank amalgamation (George Dibbs, in a pre-
carious billet as boss of one Savings Bank,
js Chicngned if he can see Reid's majority);
relief to aged poor, compulsory arbitration in
industrial disputes, and some stock measures ;
—total, the biggest sop to the constituencies
which circumstances permit of, soaked in Labor-
party gravy.

The position of N.S.W. Agent-General in Lon-
don is practically vacant—piteous accounts are
printed of the physical decrepitude of locum
tenens Cooper —and there is some talk of send-
ing to fill it one of. the discredited ex-Ministers
who lost their Parliamentary seats at the
elections. If Premier Reid attempts this it
will be a disgraceful job. The two ex-poli-
ticians mentioned— Smith and Gould —are
Dull and Insignificant, and, having lost
all political credit for themselves, could
not possibly gain any for the Agent-
General’s position. Awaiting the happy
day when all the Agent-Generalships will be
rolled into one, and efficiency increased while
money is saved, the representative of a pro-
vince in London should be a shrewd, cultured
business man of good position—not a mere
party hanger-on needing solatium for his woes.
Let Govt, study the interests of the country,
and leave the ex-Ministerial hacks to the ob-
scurity to which they have not been consigned
one moment too soon.

One wealthy defeated Sydney Parliamentary
candidate is, The Bulletin hears, taking his
wipe-out very badly. Whilst he was a member
he travelled on his charity, and, in refusing a
subscription the other day, he referred the
applicant to the new member. The F.lectoral
Act bribery-provisions might well be extended
to prevent members from subscribing otherwise
than anonymously to any local clubs or chari-
ties ; the length” to which this is now carried
amounts to a public scandal, and defeats the
object of Payment of Members. A wealthy
member cares nothing for the ,£3OO a year ; it
is the etceteras that he wants, and by judici-
ously expending £4 or £5 a week on charities
as insurance premiums on his seat, lie can make
a big charitable splash at next election ; whereas
the poor member, if he succeeds in wresting the
seat, has to expend most of his parliamentary
pay in this way, and battle for a living other-
wise as best he can.
N.S.W. Divorce- Judge Simpson is reported to
have said lately that any woman who succeeds
with her petition for divorce will, if she asks
for it in court, be granted reasonable alimony
while her husband lives. This, on the face of
it, would be monstrous ; for a woman fre-
quently gets a divorce to marry again, and that
No. 2 should be kept on the alimony supplied
by No. 1 was surely never contemplated by
Parliament. Divorce—which takes away a
husband’s rights —should take away also his
responsibilities. Probably Judge Simpson re-
ferred only to cases where the husband was
badly in the wrong, and the wife had to pro-
vide for children. But, even so, the knife
should cut both ways. If a wrongdoing hus-
band should support his divorced wife, then a
wrongdoing wife should support her divorced
husband.
The Powers which originally joined to finance
British Papua were Great Britain, Fiji, Victoria,
N.S. Wales, Queensland, S. Australia and West
Australia. Of these Great Britain, New South
Whiles, Queensland, Victoria and W 7 estralia still
stand in. Westralia, whose contribution is
only £lOO, has tendered a formal concur-
rence with whatever rules and regulations
for the management of the territory the eastern
Provinces may draw up. These eastern Pro-
vinces may be called on to pay up to £5OOO
per annum. Great Britain pays £3,500 per
annum and maintains the steamer Merrie
England, or, say, another £lOOO a year. If
England dropped out and S. Australia and Tas-
mania came in, Federated Australia could take
over Papua at very little additional outlay. In
fact a gradually diminishing grant might be
started forthwith, for the sum of £30,000, re-
presenting accumulated savings, already stands
to Papua’s credit, and under a Federation com-
mercial expansion would be a certainty.

AGAINST THE DEATH TRAPS,
OOMMEROIAL MEN ASK FO r REGULATION OP
A ll SAILING VESSELS,
At the regular meeting of the directors of the Chamber
0 { Commerce yesterday the matter of enacting a law for
securing the safety of sailing vessels was discussed. A
short time ago the Board of Supervisors of this city came
to the conclusion that there should be some law by which
all saiHng vessels should be inspected by Federal officers,
and any found un tit for the transportation of freight and
passengers could be condemned. To this end a commit-
tee was appointed, consisting of Supervisors Clinton
Morton and Lackmann, to confer with the members of
the chamber of Commerce and the Collector of the Port,
The conference was held at the meeting yesterday, and
as a result the following resolution was passed, which
secretary Scott was directed to telegraph to the Pacific
ooast Repi . esen tatives at Washington
whereas, Tiiere does not appear to be any law,
national or State, covering the inspection and supervision
of sailing vessels engaged in our coast-trade, applicable
either to the construction of the vessel, manner of loading
or ability to carry passengers ; and whereas, owinsr to the
deficiency, we believe many sailing vessels have left Our
ports during the past season which were unseaworthy or
dangerously loaded, particularly those destined for Alas-
kan"ports/ notably the bark Helen H . Almy and the
ac hooner Jane Gray, which were lost and many lives
sacrificed. Be it therefore
Resolved, That we earnestly request and respectfully
urere upon our California Delegation in Washington to
take immediate steps to induce Congress to pass such a
j aw or j aW s as will give to the proper United States
officers full control of all sailing vessels carrying passen*
gers out of our ports.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Royal “One of the Best.”
Her Majesty’s “ Dorothy.”

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.

Theatre Royal “ Round the World in 80 Days.”
Opera House Rickards Variety Co.
Bijou “ Fun on the Bristol.”
At Her Majesty’s that doughty body of
red legs, “The Yeomen of the Guard,” stepped
into the “ Gondoliers ’ ” shoes on Saturday. The
pensive, low-toned gravity of this comic opera
leads to a belief that Ye Merrie Gilbert and
Sullivan wrote it after Xmas pudding. This
scribe maintains that it was either conceived in
the melancholy that follows boiled plum-duff, or
before dinner one Sunday after church, and he
inclines to the former because he wouldn’t like to
charge Gilbert and Sullivan with church, and
because the plums in the play are so large and
solid. Everything at this opera goes against the
regulations of other opera coniiques. The right
people don’t get their deserts, the funny man dies
of a broken heart, the love affairs that entangle
themselves in the middle of the play as furiously
as all well-conducted love themes should don’t
come at all right at the end, and for these reasons
“The Yeomen of the Guard,” with its lovely
music and fetching costumes, has never gripped
the public’s heart "with the wild, fevered clutch of
the “Mikado” or “Gondoliers.”
* * * *
The present cast isn’t the Samson of the
old days. Too many knock-kneed people wander
in and out, and neither Miss Yane nor Miss
Darley has that subtle supremacy of charm that
covers a multitude of discrepancies in the rest of
the co. Mr. Kenningham takes the longest strides
towards improvement as Colonel Fairfax. The
sweet, light voice is supported with some spirited
bursts of acting, and there was one time when he
got the whole of the cast on their legs again after
a dreary downfall into spiritless action. The big-
gest bit of fat in the play falls to Howard Vernon
as Wilfred, the jolly jailer with a face like a back-
door that has been out in the sun. Lauri in
Elton’s old part as Jack Point, the jester who died
for the love of a lady, hung fire occasionally, but
bobbed up serenely in “ I have a song to sing, O.”
Dorothy Vane also seemed to have a weight on
her chest, playing prettily, but without the sparkle
that was expected of her. Mabel Darley, as Elsie
Maynard, was two shades lighter and brighter
than this scribe had expected, and moved so grace-
fully that you could almost forgive her for aiming
at top notes out of her reach. Mrs. Bracy put
more spirit than voice into her creation of Dame
Carruthers, and the various others completed or
incompleted the cast, as the case might be.
* * * *■
The latest shocker at the Lyceum seeketh
to unravel “The River Mystery,” which mystery
is further intensified by a cunning sub-title, “The
Unknown.” Everything that ever happens in a
shocker gets fair play in this one. Beginning with
two wicked trustees, a beautiful young heiress, and
a young man who has letters in his possession to
prove that he is somebody else, we come to a plot
to put a man who isn’t mad into a lunatic asylum.
So far, excellent. Then we arrive at the man who
is thrown in a river as dead, and who is picked up
and taken to a hospital, and isn’t dead at all, but
only mad. And, of course, there is the poison that
is to put an end to the heiress and gets replaced
by an innocuous narcotic just in time to save her
life. And, more than all else, there is a vault
in which the unpoisoned heiress is entrapped
later on, and the infamous attempt of one of the
wicked trustees to marry her for her money while
her mind is suffering from the narcotic that might
have been deadly poison. It is hardly necessary
to speak of the scene where the man, who wasn’t
mad when they tried to put him into an asylum,
and who fell into the river, and who didn’t die, but
had a lapse of memory, recovers his reason, and
the man who knew certain things that could put
everything right turns up with them ready-made
on his tongue, and certain other inevitables called
“Lost Papers ” duly make their appearance, and
the heiress who was saved from Poison and incar-
cerated in a Vault marries the only man she has
ever loved. These things are because they have
to. Otherwise you couldn’t have a shocker.
♦ * * *
Harry Diver is particularly effective in the
mad frenzies that follow after the hit on the
head—did we forget to mention the hit on the
head ? Harry Shine is cast as an Irishman, and
keeps things at bubbling point when the chill of
all the awful calamities is trying to freeze them.
Pretty Miss Helena Burdett is a picturesque
heroine, and the two wicked trustees are inflicted
upon Messrs. Johnston Weir and Phil Welsh.

* * *
Brisbane notices spoke very warmly of
Miss Lilian Wheeler’s performance of Lady Clan-
oarty (a fine part) and the more ordinary melo-
drama heroines in George Rignold’s shows.
Rignold himself considers his handsome leading
lady the cleverest pupil he has ever tutored, and
thinks that with good luck she is bound to achieve
fame in England one day. Miss Wheeler’s voice,
always strong and sympathetic, is much rounder
and fuller than when she spoke her first stage
lines, some 18 months or two years ago. The
tricks of acting come natural to her: her grip of a
new character and intuitive knowledge of drama-
tic effect are said to be remarkable. Given an
opportunity to let herself go as Paula Tanqueray
the gifted novice would probably surprise her
friends, and show the Australian public the dif-
ference between an actress who is born and *ne
who has been laboriously made.
#* * *
Some Melb. sassiety people interested in
working up the Shakespeare ball passed scornful
comments on J. C. Williamson’s want of en-
thusiasm for the good cause. Evidently they
were surprised that the manager of the Princess’s
didn’t devote all his available theatrical resources
to the assistance of the rival show. Their surprise
was quite unreasonable. One way and another,
J.C. W. does a good deal for the Distressed Actors’
l und ; and if he didn’t exert himself violently in
aid of this particular entertainment, it was because
the money spent in tickets and fancy costuming
would necessarily represent a considerable loss to
the Princess’s treasury. In the purpose of pro-
ducing the maximum of benefit to mummer chari-
ties at the minimum of cost to theatre-managers, a
Shakespeare ball seems less effective than a
dramatic fete or a co-operative matinee-perform-
ance, either of which is, of course, more or less
damaging to ordinary show-business at the time.
Mr. Justice WALKER, the new N.S.W. Supreme
Court Judge .
Photo. , Freeman & Co.
Melbourne shows will remain few and far
between until Saturday, when the Royal and the
Bijou are to reopen, for the benefit of the festive
agriculturist, who brings fat live-stock to town
at this season of the year, thus tempering the first
breath of Spring with an odor of prize pig. Mean-
while, “Harbour Lights” gets a whiff or two of
sea-breeze into the Princess’s, and business at the
Firm’s emporium seems brisk enough to go on
with. “Harbour Lights” is not the best melo-
drama in the world, by a good many, but it
has an old - established name, a large east,
and eleven changes of scenery to its credit!
Also, it introduces one or two human in-
cidents, in a casual sort of way. The
fashionable villain gets a telegram announc-
ing that the horse he put his patent-
leather boots upon was beaten by a beastly
outsidah, and when he appeals to sundry
johnnies for some temporary assistance they all,
with one accord, proceed to clear out of his ances-
tral mansion in violent haste. There’s a touch of
nature about that. Also, the girl who was'
betrayed before the curtain went up manages to re-
turn to her dear old village home and dodge around
the place where she was born without once falling
against her father’s chest. Any drama that
doesn’t bring an erring daughter and her aged
parent together in the second or third act, has
claims to respect on the score of unusualness.
Julius Knight and Miss Hughes are somewhat
more chirpy than at first, likewise Miss Florence
Ferriss is a more reasonable Peggy, and the per-
formance shows other improvements here and
there. Miss Mortyne deserves a word of thanks
for her vigorous attack on the girl who drove
Mark to drink by not marrying him, although it
is quite probable that Mark would have taken to
liquor in any case.
** * *
The Royal is to resume business for nine
weeks under the management of Bateman and
Maurice, the latter appearing as leading man in a
new version of “Round the World in 80 Days.”
The old theatre has been cleaned and touched up
for the occasion. “ Round the World ”is a drama
with vast possibilities in it, and the list of things
that are said to be going to happen to hero
Maurice is quite appalling. If the show proves to
be only half as expensive as rumor alleges, and the
two ballets come up to prediction, the new ven-
ture should flourish.
** * *
Fun on the Bristol,” by Cosgrove co.,
is to break out again at the Bijou on Saturday.
Two or three weeks back there was a Cosgrove co.
at the same house, and one morning the man-
agement discontinued business owing to bad
health, or something. Cosgrove, in this case, is
brother William of that ilk, who gives a very
acceptable imitation of Sheridan’s Widdy O’Brien,
but the name of the responsible manager who will
engineer the show and pay the salaries was not
announced at time of writing. The rumor that
Charles Holloway and Rignold would run the
Bijou for a time turns out to have been false.
** * *
The hugely li bellous and utterly groundless
suggestion that Biondi, now with the Rickards co.,
is not his real original self, seems to have arisen
from a confusion of the two Italian identities—
Biondi and. Fregoli. It is generally understood
that Biondi studied his business with Fregoli in
their own country, but in London and elsewhere
he was the original quick-change artist, Fregoli
coining some time later. Fregoli, now appearing
m London, has doubtless picked up something
irom Biondi, in his turn. As to which is the
cleverer artist of the pair, opinions differ. Some
people prefer Biondi’s work, others give the palm
to Iregoli Anyway, Melbourne has had good
evidence of Biondi’s originality in his impersona-
tion of the local Zelman, which is one of the most
humorous pictures in his gallery of musical com-
posers.
* * * •
Biondi’s striking imitation of young Mas-
cagni explains why the creator of “Cavalleria
Rusticana ” has not followed up his one great suc-
cess. Mascagni, as presented at the Princess’s, is
a nervous, self-conscious, spoilt darling of the
multitude. Such a. man would seem naturally
lacking in grit, and incapable of fighting against
the failures of his later works. When the sun of
celebrity had ceased to shine upon him Mascagni
gave way to despair. Attempted to commit sui-
cide, didn’t he ?

At Poverty Point.
‘Mummer” : Was with a company in Wanganui, M.L.,
district, a part much infected by Johnnies who do rouse-
about work cheap and call it “ eadetting.” Met one in a
pub. Short drab coat and cap ; bony, vacuous face :
cords preposterously big in the breech and skintight
where the calf ought to have been.. His thin shanks ran
into policeman’s feet like a hat-pin stuck into a plug of
tobacco. Wheu he smiled his face wore, to quote Louis
Becke, “ the expression of a child's coffin.” I expected
to hear him say, “Naice weathaw. Wang’ui deuced
slew.” Got a shock when he began : “ Ah’m thenkin’
ye’ll note dae verra weel in the toun. We hae say mony
guid pairforinances wi' th’ amachewers. We hae list
pieeyed the ‘ Youman o’ the Gaird.” “Yes. What did
yuu play ? ” “Ah pled Wulfrid, an’ we’re gaun to hae a
coansairt next week for a banefut.” “ Are you in that,
too?” “Aye. Ah’m daein’ some o’ Awlburt Shuvaleer’s
coaster tur-rns.” “How do people like ‘coster turns’
with a Scotch accent?” “ Mon ! Ah hae nae Scoatch
aksent; Ah’ve bin daein’thae turrns fur ’ears, an’ nae-
body but yersel’ iver kenn’d ah was Scoatch.”
Wybert Reeve, lessee of Adelaide Royal, took to the
boards again the other evening. Must be about 75 years
old. Reeve claims to have helped Henry Irving up the
ladder over 40 years ago. His two great parts in his best
days were Count Fosco and the Crushed Tragedian. He
looks the latter in the street, with his pompous manner
and erect carriage.
“S.” : Painful to hear of handsome Harrie Ireland
crowded off the stage to pose as high priestess of the
groaning board, and deal out sandwiches and tea to Ade-
laide mashers. Harrie cuts a finer figure on the stage
than any lady now acting in Australia, and if as a com-
forting, ruddy-haired picture alone should always be
gratefully received by Australian playgoers. There are
many actresses who cannot hold a light to her now in
decent places in the profession here, but they are im-
ported. Another injustice to Ireland !

“ Thorold ” : Be critic Stansbury and the violets worn
by Melba when singing. I have never heard of any other
vocalist who would dare to wear violets (of all flowers)
when about to sing in public. Their strong perfume has
a most baleful effect upon the voice. But Melba!—she
cycles against orders, doesn’t diet herself after the
fashion of other stars, and in a few years’ time she will
either have exploded the idea of coddling the voice, or
afford melancholy proof that the people who shook their
heads were wiseacres in their generation.

suit happened outside the Princess stage-door every night
last week, when the now-devoted Ada Reeve could be
seen chatting with her fellow-mummers and awaiting her
loving hubby.
Lemmone is so disgusted with the financial failure of
his last musical venture that he spent last week in cabling
to certain stars he had been negotiating with for the
future, “Stay where you are.” Adela Verne, with the
generosity so eharacteric of the professional class, pro-
poses to get up a benefit-concert for Mr. Lemmone in
Sydney. The suggestion is being very well received, as
the great flautist is highly popular. Miss Verne will play
three concertos with orchestra at this concert, one of
which Mr. Eric Morley introduced here by the scherzo
movement.
“Theorbo”: Flautist Lemmone, as proprietor of the
Alva-Verne concern, fired a parting-shot in the Melb.
press, before leaving. The monetary failure was, of
course, accountable. No electric star. Alva did not put
her best leg foremost the first night, writes one. She did
not come down with the smash and crash of a Hambourg,
though no humbug, but a fine, sound artist. And she
must have been ready to pull the ears of little Verne.

S.S. : Biondi’s caricatures of the musical masters, old
and otherwise, are remarkable and wonderfully clever.
The operator uses wigs and whiskers, and, for several of
the characters, a false nose, but the rest is all Biondi.
We must take the accuracy of the work for granted in
most cases, but the characterisation would be sufficient if
he called his subjects Brown, Jones and Robinson. The
keynote of each character is placid and fatuous conceit,
ana sleek, smug*, dolly Sullivan is one of the most
affectedly egotistical. Biondi’s musicians are an oily
glittering, simpering, ogling band preening themselves in
the light of women’s eyes with a grotesque vanity that is
deliciously humorous in the Biondi creation, without
suggesting a hint of too much colour. But Biondi’s first
turn, “ The Music Lesson,” is flat, stale and unprofitable,
and would not be borne from a local man. Ugo’s choice in
whiskers is wretched, their falsity is as obvious as a hay-
stack, and his men are like the tawdry, 50-year-old carica-
tures of British tourists in the cheap Italian comic
journals.
Once upon a time, as the story goes, the proud author
of a ghastly stage work invited a certain musical artist to
come and gaze upon the G.S.W. aforesaid from the
private box. The victim, with his wife, accepted the in-
vitation, and had to sit out the excruciating entertain-
ment whilst his host, the author, stood behind the chair
and prompted him to admiration with such remarks as
“This is a funny bit!” “ Now we shall have a pure
comedy scene.” “ What did you think of that for a
smart line?’’ and so on. There was no escape for the
tortured visitor, for the author kept guard over him all
the evening, and wrung from him many assurances of the
“ pleasure” he was deriving from the piece. Well, next
morning, after a troubled slumber, that polite man re-
ceived a visit from the author, who first read aloud to him
a gushful letter purporting to express his (the sufferer’s)
opinion of the previous night’s performance, and then
asked him to kindly sign it for publication in the news-
papers. This was most embarrassing. The musical artist
—he was running a show of his own at the time—pro-
tested that it would never do for him to fly in the face of
the adverse press notices which his young friend’s effort
had provoked. But he was fluttering in his excuses when
a happy thought struck him. “ I’m quite sure,” said he,
“ that my agent wouldn’t sanction it.” It is at such
moments that a showman feels the necessity for travelling
with a hard business agent.
Cockaigne asks of The Bulletin : “ Why oo you turn
into a draper George Lewis, the eminent attorney, spouse
of Kate Terry ?” Cockaigne is very mixed. Kate Terry
married George Lewis (member of'the firm of Lewis and
Allenby) years before attorney Sir George Lewis was
heard of. That Lewis married a Miss Eberstadt.
“ S.S." : Re Johnnie Coleman and the scarecrow-dance,
it is, so far, merely a matter of assertion against asser-
tion. There are several people in Melbourne who persist
in the declaration that they have seen Ashcroft, the
Solid Man, do most of Coleman’s business in the “ M‘Gee”
song, “Scarecrow” dance included. The writer of the
first par. was inaccurate in saying that Ashcroft was
dead, but the latter’s death, under tragic circumstances,
was reported in England. Ashcroft did Irish business,
and may he in Dublin now, but he ran a music-hall in
Liverpool, where Coleman hails from, when Johnnie was
a comparative youngster.
“ Old Par” : Re the disposition of actors and actresses
to be deeply “ impressed ” by any play that they are
viewing from the front of the house—are they not wilfully
impressed ? It has always seemed to me that the purfesh
conspires to over-rate histrionic effects. A very moderate
actor will make a point of enthusing over another fellow’s
moderate performance, provided that the other fellow is
not in the same line of stage business as himself, or is,
anyway, not a dangerous rival. The purfesh is a Mutual
Admiration—not less than a Mutual Jealousy—Society,
and it refuses to recognise the fact that 19 mummers out
of every 20 have less of the dramatic instinct than the
average successful city man.
The Salvation Army placards a Sunday performance, in
Melbourne, of the cinematographe and grammaphone,
thinly veijed as a service, with silver-coin admission.
Rickards does not dare to introduce a Sunday concert at
the Opera House. Sauce for the Herbert Booth goose is
not sauce for the Harry Rickards gander. But, then,
Acting-Gov. Madden doesn’t take the chair at Rickards'
temple.
Alleged programme of an entertainment given at Cue
(W.A.) in the early roaring days, about five years ago:
Grand Entertainment
in aid of the
SUPERANNUATED DRY BLASTERS’ RELIEF
ASSOCIATION.
Part I.
A Snorter, by Handelson, in Three Spasms and One
Squeal, by Signor Squalini. Scenery bv the celebrated
Dutch artist Van Daub. As performed before all the bald-
heads of Europe, Abraham, Isaac, and America. Pyro-
technics by Professor Rocketi.
Spasm I. —The blood-stained windlass. Finding the
corpse at the bottom of the shaft. Did it fall, or was it
pushed ?
Spasm 11. —The inquest. Arrest of the culprit. Guilty
or not guilty. “ I’ll prove an albino, yer honor.”
Spasm lll.—Murder of the warden back of the Cue pub.
Theft of the church. The sacred utensils dollied. A
craven deed. Panned off in the holy water.
Squeal IV.—Justice avenged. Red tape in excelsis.
To conclude with a transfiguration scene representing
the Sergeant of Police being crowned with straw envel-
opes, and presentation of the Inspector of Nuisances with
a Jay Peaship.
Interval of five drinks.
Part 11.
Barmaid Show. —The prettiest barmaid in town to be
chosen by ballot. Prize to be awarded is the choice of
any dryblower in the room as a husband, on the instal-
ment plan, on a month’s approval, or for keeps. The
double or straight out.
Interval of Ten Drinks.
To conclude with a dance and supper. Donahs, free ;
gentlemen, sdwts. each.

Doors open at 7.30. Agony starts at 8 o’clock sharp.
