The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The cold blast of retrenchment is blowing over
Westralia this budget. Premier Forrest reckons that, in
spite of all the loan money spent, he has accumulated a
revenue deficit of £186,000 ; and the wild cat is to be
whipped to square things. There is to be an excise of 10
per cent, on beer and stout, calculated to raise £lO,OOO ;
the duty on wearing-apparel is raised from 10 to 15 per
cent. —which is moderate, considering that Q., S.A., and
M.L. charge 25, and Vic. goes as high as 35 per cent. ; 5
per cent, is put on machinery (formerly free) ; 10 per cent,
on timber (formerly 5) ; and several articles are changed
from the ad-valorem to the specific list—cottage pianos,
for example, being charged £5 each instead of 15 per
cent. On the whole, Treas. Forrest estimates that he
will get £1,685,000 from miscellaneous sources and
£1,220,000 from railways, and show on the expenditure
side only £2,900,000 including the deficit ; so that if all
goes well he will come out a few thousands to the good.
If it doesn’t In the meantime municipal subsidies
come down from 20s. to 15s. in the £ ; expenditure on
public works is being reduced, and public servants are
being dismissed by the score ; and there is a great noise
in the land where the sandgroper busteth.
* • * •
S.A. helps Westralia to shadow the budget pic-
tures. Treas. Kingston’s was a speech of regrets. Revenue
fell £61,000 below previous year’s, and it was small com-
fort that expenditure was decreased by £16,000. He
starts with a £28,000 debit-balance ; leaves taxation as it
is; and estimates this year’s revenue at £2,649,000;
expenditure at £30,000 less. With a good wheat harvest,
Kingston thinks he can about square accounts. But his
general air of gloom is depressing.
* * * *
Q. Treasurer Philp provides a budget which
looks well on casual inspection. His year’s revenue, as
already chronicled, exceeded the estimate by £41,000;
and he is convinced that the province is well on the up-
grade. Nevertheless expenditure also exceeded estimate
by £47,000 ; being less than revenue by £20,724. And, as
is the habit of the good-year Treasurer, Philp is going to
“ let her go ” and increase current year’s expenditure by
£119,000 —which the expanding revenue is asked to kindly
cover.
• * • •
Maoriland goes one better, with an estimated
surplus of £166,800 ; and Treas. Seddon thinks that this
year will give him £450,000 to play with. He proposes to
set apart £105,000 for old-age pensions, and keep the
M.L. reputation for advanced Democracy well in front.
Despite this glowing balance-sheet, Oapt. Russell says
he is dissatisfied, and a fierce want-of-confidence debate is
proceeding.
* • * •
The Q.N. Bank half-yearly meeting was dis-
tinguished by the directors’ attempt to cancel the rule by
which one of their number steps off the board every two
years. Chairman Munro pointed out that after two years
a director was just beginning to learn his business, and
he might have added that it was a shocking thing that his
fees should be taken away just when he was getting used to
them. The meeting, however, with the memory of
directorial hanky-panky still in mind, considered that the
advantage of a little new blood on the board now and
then was worth keeping; and the directors failed
to get the necessary three-fourths majority for
alteration of the rule. Premier Byrnes’ expressed
disapproval of their little plan made the directors’ bluff
seem steeper than ever ; and even Brisbane Courier
doesn’t consider that the Board is far enough from the
Smell to do without its two-yearly disinfectant. The
balance-sheet was adopted after some obstreperous share-
holders had commented on the expense-rate, and asked
why the Q.N. wanted ten directors to manage it when
other and bigger banks get along with eight.
• * * *
The Port Jackson Co-operative S.S. co. has
just paid its first div. since 1892. It is now on a
thoroughly-sound footing, and is prospering under the
direction of a level-headed manager. The year’s business,
after maintaining the co.’s steamers in good working
order, and providing over £4BOO for depreciation, leaves
a balance at credit of profit and loss of £3961 odd, of
which the div. (10 per cent, to shareholders and 5 per
cent, to debenture-holders) absorbs £2968, leaving £993 to
be carried forward. The old management of the P. J.S.S.
co. was probably the most pig-headed aggregation of
donkey-natured persons ever entrusted with the control
of an obviously good thing since the world began. In-
stead of catering for the general public, they stuck out
for monstrously high fares to please a little coterie of
shabby-genteel people who condescended to live at
Manly ; and in the result they had to sweat tears of blood
—and gold. Talk about paying for experience !

According to a private letter, the finders of the
mysterious “ Sacred Slug,” at Kanowna, Westralia, are
two miners from Oassilis (Gippsland)—Brodie and Millane.
These two good Romans have also a 240 z. hunk to their
credit. The story also goes that the big slug is now at
Melb. Mint ; and that it wasn’t found in the locality indi-
cated by Father Long.
» • • •
Hill End, Broad Arrow, is one of the few good
things which the French have got hold of in W.A. Since
the erection of a mill a year ago the stone treated has
returned £5 10s. per ton. Expenses figure at £llos., and
it is alleged that there is enough in sight to keep the mill
jigging for years. Mt. Colac, in same locality, is a show
on which Adelaide people spent a lot of money before
finally abandoning. A local syndicate has recently started
crushing the ore-dumps, and out of two cleans-up has
paid a div. of £2 per share. Two ounces was the tale the
battery told.
« * * *
If all that Konemann says about his own pro-
cess of treating tailings and slimes is true, the Great
Boulder co. did well in making the much-abused contract
with him. He talks of dealing with the Boulder tailings,
which contain about 60 per cent, slime, because of grind-
ing in pans, at 4s. 6d. a ton, and saving a minimum of 93
per cent, and alludes to the heap of money to be made by
the G.B. co. in other directions by its share interest when
the process gets going elsewhere. Alas ! the same story
has been told of every other process under the sun.

Cabled that a knight and two others connected
with the Coolgardie Mint and Iron King mines (Hannan’s)
have been charged in London with conspiracy and false
pretences at the instance of the liquidator. In this co. it
was found last year that bogus scrip for thousands ot
shares had been issued, in re which proceeding the chair-
man of directors said in London a few weeks back that
the guilty persons were not worth powder and shot, and
therefore it was not proposed to prosecute. Curiously
enough, no allusion whatever has been made to this
matter by the big Australian dailies in explanation of the
cable. m
• * * •
Published that the Westralian Govt, has offered
£50,000 for the patent rights of the cyanide process, and
that the Australian Gold Recovery co. is asking £70,000.
If this is correct no doubt a deal will be made, and a lot of
litigation saved. No co. using the process in W.A. is pay-
ing royalty, which is being ignored in other parts of Aus-
tralia, and, whatever its rights, the A.G.R. co. is very
dilatory in enforcing them. Though controlling the
greatest metallurgical invention of the century, it has had
a chequered career. While losing a lot of money as the
owner of the process, many fortunes have been made by
the use of it. Up to two or three years ago it could have
bought, or got under offer, nine-tenths of the t’fijhuhngs-
heaps of Australasia. To-day nine-tenths of those left
untreated by cyanide are not worth treating by any pro-
cess. These worthless heaps are largely in Bendigo and
Ballarat. At Charters Towers alone there are over 40
cyanide plants at work, some of them treating tailings
washed miles away from where they were made. What-
ever the real cause the A.G.R. co. has had a very bad time.

Lawyer Bright, chairman of the Gen. Gordon
co., has gone over from Adelaide to inspect the property.
Speaking at Kalgoorlie of the prospects of the show, Mr.
Bright casually mentioned that, on the present manager
taking charge, there was no ore in sight, but that a small
body of pay-stone had since been met. What has become
of the famous 40ft. lode, of which the world heaid so
much a couple of years ago ? Five men are at present
employed on the Gordon. Manager Simpson, late of
Bulong, has charge.

The end of the Hit or Miss, Mulgarrie, W.A.,
appears to have arrived (writes a Bulletin correspondent).
The 20-head battery was auctioned t’other day, and the
silence of desolation reigns supreme over the abandoned
township. About the most interesting feature of Mul-
garrie nowadays is the great stack of empty bottles on the
site of Jerry Collins’s old pub.

• * * *
The neighborhood of the old Plunderderry has
now its mica claim, owned by a £24,000 Melb. co. The
mineral, for which the demand is consistent, occurs in a
felspar lode formation running to 12ft. wide. Said that
where cut at 70ft. deep the stuff is still good. But the
result of similar claims in half-a-dozen widely-separated
places in Australia makes one shudder ; it takes such a
pile of the clean-cut stuff to make a ton, and invariably
the pile of rejected stuff has been disproportionately
large.
• * * *
Excerpts from a Vic. miner’s letter dated from
Kalgoorlie, W.A. “ Have put in for over 20 contracts
during the last five weeks, and never got a look-in. Con-
tracts are let before tenders are called, and go to men
willing to earn 30s. a week, which is all the average con-
tract is worth here. . . . Two men were wanted at the
mine yesterday. Turned up with a crowd includ-
ing two reduced johnnies in black suits. A manager
sporting an eye-glass went over to the bateh j —good
miners, mainly—and selected the johnnies. . . . I predict
a deadly time for underground men here in the summer.
At the Great Boulder, the Lake View Consols, and the
Australia, thej are running their cyanided tailings below
to fill up the stopes. The tailings are wet and the stopes
hot, so that the former fairly reek cyanide poison. The
man who can stand much of it must have the constitution
of a crocodile. Twelve men had to come up out of the
Lake View the other day for this reason. They tell me
they drop at their work, and the fumes leave them too
weak to climb the ladders. . . . They brought men and
machinery all from Germany to run the Brown Hill.
This d d country is stamped in red, ‘ Made in Ger-
many.’ ”

* * • *
Be Lyell slump. It is rumored that much of
one big man’s scrip is in the market. Argued, therefore,
that the slump must be a fake, as no director would rush
his paper on to the market during troublous times.
Other knowing ones say that whatever of that director’s
scrip is on sale is gift stuff that has been hoarded up by
promoters’ friends since the early days of the float.
Delprat, now on his way out from England to
take charge (under Stewart) of the B.H. Prop, mine, is
said to be one of the most reputable mining men in Eng-
land. Head-quartered in London, he supervised and
“ ran ” many of the big South American properties, acting
as something between a concentrated board of directors
and general manager.
B.H. South directors succeed in having their
fees raised to £6OO per annum ; and, though the prospects
of the co. have improved, and perhaps justify the rise, it
would have looked rather better if a mere shareholder had
proposed it. Melb. shareholders in the co. begin to com-
plain that its interests are being sacrificed to those of
Block 14, which has the same gen. manager; and the
relations will, anyway, be worth observing.

B.H. Consols has lately yielded some more huge
slugs- of almost pure silver, and, from a shipment lately
made, a div. will be paid.
0 m * *
B.H. Prop., at Pt. Pirie, is now relying largely
on sea-waier, refusing to pay the 6d. per 1000 gallons de-
manded for the water from the Govt. bore. The latter’s
yield of 50,000 gals. daily is consequently flowing into the
sea.
* * • *
The owners of the Paddy Lackey mine
(N.S.W.) have their new main shaft down 500 ft. This
wiil give them over 100 ft. of ground to stope out, but, to
put the mine in good shape, this shaft should go another
200 ft., so that two or three levels could be worked simul-
taneously It is, and has been from the very first, a rat-
tling good mine, and why it was not bought by one or
other of the several London representatives who have
monkeyed with it, has always been a puzzle to the writer.
There is a Paddy Lackey Deep Levels ! When does if
expect to strike the reef ?

Prospectus of the Birthday United G.M. Co.
(shares already allotted) appears in this issue. The pro-
perty is a 20-acre lease at Berringa (Vic.), and three
shafts—one down 80ft.—have given encouragement to
work the show vigorously. Up to now 67 tons have
crushed for 370 z. of good quality gold ; also, the stone
seems to improve as it goes deeper. The new co. takes
the stone at grass, and a crushing will be put through
shortly Capital, 25,000 shares of 6s. each. 20,000 paid-
up to 18d. were offered to the publio. The vendors take
6000 fully paid-ups, and leave £5OO to credit of co.
Bendigo’s resuscitated wonder, the Garden
Gully, has got into its old swing again. For the last week
in July it crushed 1248£0z. gold from 250 tons, of which
200 tons (a second crushing) came from the new reef.
This is Bendigo’s best yield since ’B9, when Hercules and
Energetic milled 20440 z. from 46 tons, wiped off an £lBOO
debt, and paid a ss. div. G.G. shares, down into the
single shillings the other week, dance merrily now m the
neighborhood of £4. Lansell’s luck again.
New Moon—one of the'big, low-grade, div.-
paying mines of Bendigo—has had a special meeting of
directors to deal with an offer to place it on the English
market. Determined that any arrangement would be on
the basis of £60,000 cash and one-half interest in the new
co., and a deposit of £5OOO put up to secure the option of
purchase. The usual thing, when any attempt is made to
buy a producing gold-mine. New Moons are selling at
40s. The cash asked would give 50s. a share, and each
share would carry one or more shares in the new co.,
which would also have to put up the £12,000 admitted to
be necessary for sinking a new main shaft and increasing
the battery capacity.
* * • *
Adelaide mining men are thinking hard over a
new hammer just modelled there. The idea simply is a
revolving wheel on which several hammers are swung.
In the ordinary jargon of the engineer, “ if the speed (of
the wheel) is 300 revolutions per minute each hammer
will travel 90ft. per second and give five blows of 270 cwt.,
or 54 tons, upon the material operated upon.” A modest
claim of six times greater efficiency than the stamper-
battery is made on the new idea’s behalf —which is much
less than has been made in respect of many other ideas
that have afterwards been converted into very good scrap
iron. And yet no one ever can feel that the old stamp
quite fills the bill.

Doubtless Q. Geologist Jack says the right word
about Chillagoe—“ the mines amply justify the expendi-
ture of the necessary capitalin constructing a railway,
erecting smelting works, and an electrolytic refinery.”
That “ amply ” measures the difference between a pro-
mising spec, and a poor one. Just how rich the Chillagoe
field will be nobody knows ; but that it is likely to be rich
and permanent is now obvious ; that it will be rich for a
time is almost a certainty.
* • * »
Schlapp denies that anyone had authority for
attributing to him the published statements re the great-
ness of Chillagoe and contradicting Stewart as to the
character of the ore bodies and lodes. Who was it put
this great yarn into his mouth? Schlapp is sa?d to have
no doubt as to the future of the country when the railway
gets into it and the lodes and formations have been better
developed; but that will take time and big money.
Smith, who accompanied Stewart to Chillagoe, will take
the active management of Chillagoe Proprietary mines at
the end of the year. Stated in Melb. last week that
Stewart himself would take the general management.
That seems to be the understanding, but he has no inten-
tion of leaving Broken Hill for some time to come, and
will, meanwhile, act more as consulting and supervising
engineer.
No doubt a lot of work is wanted at Chillagoe
before the presence of Stewart is needed. The money
for the railway, too, has yet to be put up. The crowd
handling this business have no such word as failure in
their lexicon, none the less they are passing through a bad
time with Chillagoe. It gave premature birth to a boom.
Under the belief that the shares were going to at least
£5OOO each, persons borrowed money to get in at £lBOO,
and when it was generally realised that some years must
elapse before the mines could possibly become big pro-
ducers they had to get out anyhow. To-day it is very
much easier to buy Chillagoes than to sell them. The
slump in Lyells and throughout that over-boomed camp,
and the awful, complete knock-out in Lake George, have
given many persons the copper-pip; also the scallawags
who madfe for Chillagoe, and who stood not on the order
of pegging and lying but pegged anywhere and lied on
everything, have been a curse to the .field. Boomsters
and persons stuck with Chillagoes at a big figure try to
blame experts who have simply told the truth of Chilla-
goe, and Stewart is one of the truth-tellers.

Dear Bulletin. — Be two Chillagoe pars, in
Bulletin of July 30. I can assure “ C.H.J.” that Line-
dale West Chillagoe £lO shares have changed hands at
£lOO. The co. has been reconstructed into 3300 shares of
£5 each. Latest quotation, £26 buyers at Brisbane.
“ S.Y.K.” gives damnatory faint praise to Chillagoe, and
then talks of the Keelbottom in a boomful style. The
Keelbottom is the richest and therefore very naturally
the smallest and most elusive copper-show in Queensland.
Big, isolated patches of metallic copper occur, and then
great blanks. Also, there is no proof that the copper
goes down or up or sideways, because, as I have
said before, its metallic contents are isolated and
elusive. This is the show which has registered—
not necessarily floated—in London with a £500,000 capi-
tal, and its promoters propose that besides mining and
smelting the co. should make copper-wire and sheets and
tubes on the mine. The only thing they haven’t arranged
for is to build and copper ships on the mine and float
them down to the sea per canal to Townsville. Like that
“ Old Miner” who wrote you a few weeks back that Mt.
Garnet was a low-grade show, “S.Y.K.” knows nothing
about Chillagoe, or he has no Chillagoe interest but does
hold a fat cut in the Keelbottom. —Yours more in sorrow,
&c., Randolph Bedford.

Mount Perry (old Teningering—what barbarian
changed the mellifluous name ?) is now nursing a hope
that it will be allowed to share in the crumbs that may
fall from the table of Q. copper-boom. Said that the cost
of smelting by present methods is as low as one-third of
what it was when the Teningering copper-mines had to be
shut down.
• * * *
Someone has been telling the Cowra (N.S.W.)
people of how he, a master-mariner, was, 34 years ago, in
charge of a vessel, with £BOO,OOO of treasure aboard, off
the west coast of S. America, when it blew up and went
down in Bor 10 fathoms; that all hands, barring said
master and another (the latter died of his injuries a few
days later), being lost. They set such store on this
romantic tale at Cowra that they are subscribing a £4OOO
co. (£2OOO in fully paid-up shares to go to the ex-captain
for his interest and assistance), with a view to disinterring
the long-buried treasure. The man who goes into this
spec, is just as likely to make money as the fellow who
goes into many other specs.

“ What in the captain’s but a choleric word.”
Earl de la Warr and Mr. Broadley were concerned in an
offer of money to wrecked financier Hooley to alter his
damaging evidence. The Earl, it is cabled, was found
guilty of an indiscretion ; the plain Mister was found
guilty of subornation of perjury. Britain is still the
Country which Crawls.
• * * •
Revelations by Hooley still going on in London.
Hess, editor of the Critic, who made Labouchere sit up
recently, seems to have had much to do in bringing about
this exposure. In his issue of July 2 he named the date
when Hooley would give the names of persons and papers
that had blackmailed him, and advised the Attorney-
General to be ready to watch proceedings.
Hooley in the heyday of his glory:—“I have
always pone for big: things. Half-way up the ladder you
will find it crowded with competitors. If you go to the
top you practically have a field to yourself. There are
plenty men ready to deal in thousands, but they get
frightened when you talk of millions. Making money is a
matter of will and self-sacrifice."
* * • •
Rand gold-production for June —365,0910 z.,
bringing the half-year’s total to 2,067,0680 z., at which
rate the year’s production should be worth about 14J
millions. Less than 10 years ago it did not reach one
million. Big men went in with big money ; and with big
machinery, and nigger labor, working on fairly large and
wonderfully long and consistent reefs, the world’s
greatest production (the output from this camp alone ex-
ceeds that of Australia altogether) is the result. Be it re-
membered that the average from ore and tailings treat-
ment is a little over Joz. per ton in all. Some day the one
or two big low-grade places in N.S.W., small by compari-
son, will be tackled in the same way, but probably not by
N.S.W. mining men—the shock would be too great.
• • * •
The cash-value of impudence has been once
more demonstrated in an Australian mining town, where
a plausible new-comer, having established himself as
“ financial agent," did good business in the way of 25 per
cent, deposits upon scrip which he bought by wire from
confiding metropolitan agents—to arrive attached to
drafts. But ere the first batch of drafts arrived the
agent had evaporated, as deeply regretted by most of
the local tradespeople as by his “ clients.”
* * * *
Carl Harden, who suicided in Rockhampton
t’other day, was, before his appointment as Receiver in In-
solvency, the terror of the embarrassed business men in
West Queensland. When bills accumulated, creditors
sent Harden out to find how matters stood. His methods
of accountancy were rapid, drastic, and, in most cases,
final. His motto was: “ You can’t pay 20s. in the £;
then go.”
• • * •
A clumsy attempt at smuggling rooted grape-
vines, under the false bottoms of cases—an old, old ruse—
containing plants, from a N.S.W. nursery into Queens-
land simply courted discovery. The plants had, under
the recent Q. Diseases in Plants Act, to be forwarded to
the Department of Agriculture to be fumigated, and
failure to discover the smuggle was almost impossible.
The importation of grape-vines into Q. has been prohibited
for 20 years.

* * * m
“ Bargo ” : N.S.W. G.P.O. authorities are
astutely thus circularising postmasters : “ Owing to one
or two unsatisfactory reports regarding tampering with
registered letters, postmasters and assistants at the office
of origin and offices through which registered letters pass
are particularly requested to place the office date-stamp
on the back of the letter, so that one portion of the im-
print may be on the flap and the other on the letter.”
Holy smoke ! The P.M. at the office of origin can first
open the letter, if so inclined, and put flap imprint on
afterwards. (The flap-stamping isn’t done before sender’s
face.) The P.O. Sherlock Holmeses evidently think that
if a letter is tampered with after stamp imprint the tam-
perer will not be able to place flap so as to give the im-
print its original look. Registration merely affords a
means of tracing a letter. Tampering, unless done under
the eyes of postal detectives, or with trap-letters, cannot
be sheeted home in the case of a letter which has been
sorted through five or six different offices. British regis-
tration envelopes close with an end flap on the front, and
postage-stamp is then placed one half on flap and other on
letter, making tampering a little harder after stamp
obliteration. There is a fortune for the inventor of an
envelope which cannot be tampered with without detec-
tion.
* * * *
Sydney G.P.O. authorities are in a quandary
over the demonetisation of the Id., 2d., and 2jd. stamps
produced previous to the “ Record Reign issue." These
stamps were not to be recognised after the 30th June last
and the “Postal Guide” for the last two quarters con-
tained a notice—“ New Id., 2d., and 21 d. stamps were in-
troduced on 22nd June, 1897 ; stamps of these denomina-
tions issued prior to that date may be used for the pre-
payment of postage up to the 30th of June next.” But
this notice has appeared in the July, ’9B, “ Guide,” thus
giving the stamps another 12 months to run. Nathless,
postmasters, acting in accordance with instructions
of the two previous “ Guides,” have been charging
the letters bearing the supposed obsolete stamps 2d.
deficiency and 2d. fine, so that the letter is actually
charged 6d. The Department is now instructing p.m.’s
to allow the stamps the advantage of their unintended
new lease of currency. Stupid piece of officialism, in the
first place, not to allow the old issue to run out by effluxion.
Few people in the country see the “ Postal Guide,” and a
far-back cocky who has received £1 worth of stamps 10
years ago for, say, a produce-remit, and has been using
same at the rate of two or three a year ever since, is in
blissful ignorance of the fact that what remain with him
are so much waste-paper. Besides, it’s public robbery.
The P.O. gets cash for its stamps, and should return value
—now or 50 years hence.
* * • •
“ Bingara,” to The Bulletin : All woolgrowers
must resent the attempt to establish—or uphold—a mono-
poly, now illustrated by the boycott of the N.S.W. Co-
operative Wool and Produce co. And, if all “free”
woolgrowers who have hitherto realised in Sydney market
will gently, but firmly, intimate to their brokers their
intention of shipping to London, if the unholy combine
effects its object, it will go far to squash the trouble. If
the Brokers’ Association had used the machinery of their
combination to abolish touting, bonuses, and circulars,
and shared the resultant savings with their clients, they
might have looked to us to support their organisation.
But, as a one-eyed combine to maintain rates and smash
competition, they deserve our execration. Let the
growers’ ultimatum be : An open market or shipment!

“ Koora ” : Be lost loading. On 10th Jan. last
three teams loaded on two railway trucks at a N.S.W.
station 177 bags of wheat, and up to date no trace of
same has since been seen. The wheat belonged to a
widow, and is a heavy loss. Just about the time it was
trucked a change of station-masters was made—a very
unbusiness-like proceeding—when the wheat was being
sent away at the rate of 30 trucks a day, with only one
man to do the whole of the work, including telegraph
and postal.

SOCIETY
Mr. A. H. Griffith, M.L.A., has moved for a
Royal Commission on the Sulfield case. If
thoroughly in earnest he can get it. When a
Royal Commission solemnly holds the balance
between Levien and Sleath—when a Royal Com-
mission sits to hear charges against a Senior-
Constable, and several hundred pounds are
spent in printing 280 pages of evidence —then can
a Royal Commission sit to enquire whether or
not the eight-years’-imprisoned Thomas Suffield
is the victim of monstrous injustice.
* * * *
Gov. Buxton has definitely decided to flit
for England at the end of Sept. It is pretty
certain that none of his family will return, and
the return of His XXX. with his pious staff and
tracts and dreary speeches is also problematical.
Adelaide society is now anxiously anticipating a
social burst under the Acting-Governorship of
Chief Justice and Mrs. Way.
* * * *
Gov. Buxton has put up an Australian
vice-regal record. During his term he has per-
sistently ignored the theatres, with the solitary
exception of a one-horse amateur show, and people
who noticed the conscience-stricken expression on
His Excellency’s countenance as he painfully sat
out that worldly performance will never forget it.
H.E. has made many friends in S.A., but a person
more unfitted for the position of a Governor could
hardly be imagined.

In a kindred spirit did the English people welcome the

VAIN REGRETS.
SHE : “ Before we were married you used to say you could eat me. ”
H E: “Did I! Well, I’m darned sorry I didn’t.”
party at Warlies last Saturday, and spent a delightfully
restful and memorable Sunday at his Excellency’s beauti-
ful Essex home. Warlies is now in the full glory of its
early summer verdure, and, much as Dr. and Mrs. Cock-
burn had heard of its fine park and perfectly-kept lawns
and gardens, the reality far surpassed their anticipations.
They were specially interested in the hot-houses, and
amused to find the tomato —so common with you - treated
as quite an aristocratic vegetable, and hanging from the
glass roofs like grapes. The Agent-General was pro-
foundly impressed with the beneficent influence which an
establishment like Warlies exercises on the whole country-
side. Hay-making was in progress on Saturday, and Mr.
Buxton had some 30 extra handsjin addition to his own
numerous employes at work. It was a lesson in the best
sort of good manners to see the kindness and considera-
tion with which the family treated everyone. You could
easily tell that working for others was the natural rule of
their lives. On Sunday afternoon the haymakers of the
entire neighborhood were hospitably entertained to tea
on the lawn at Warlies. They enjoyed an ample meal,
which was presided over by Mrs. Buxton, and then the
gathering spontaneously moved off in the direction of the
schoolroom, which, if I am not mistaken, was one of Lady
Victoria’s many benefactions to the village. Here Mr.
Victor Buxton gave out a familiar hymn, and, after a
short but impressive service, conducted by a rev. gentle-
man stopping at Warlies, Mr. Fraser, a wealthy stock-
broker with serious proclivities, delivered an earnest ad-
dress. During his stay at Sir Fowell Buxton’s, Dr. Cock-
burn, I need hardly say, went the round of the mansion,
missing none of the numerous pictures, relics, and curios
to which noble traditions attach. Altogether, it proved
a most interesting visit.
Even the Buxtonian vegetables were aristocratic !

The Bulletin continues to receive from
Norfolk Island bitter complaints of the condition
of things under the new administration, and
severe, but mutually corroborative, aspersions
upon local officialdom. “If the N.S.W. Govern-
ment,” writes one correspondent, “cannot send
us better representatives, they had better keep
them at home until they are reformed ! ” All the
same, this paper doesn’t believe that even an arch-
angel placed in authority in so paltry-minded a
community as that of Norfolk Island would escape
slander.
* * * *
The stories of the moral frailties of the
Norfolk Islanders, who are among the most tee-
total people on earth, fall in with brave Father
Ullathorne’s reason for part of the debasement of
the convicts when imprisoned on the same island.
Dr. Ullathorne, onetime Vicar Apostolic of N.S W.
and afterwards R.O. Bishop of Birmingham (Eng.),
attributed much of the wickedness of the convicts
of the island “to the absence of all stimulants.”
Ullathorne was himself a practical abstainer dur-
ing his time in Australia, but held that sobriety-
by-compulsion was, in many cases, adverse to
virtue.
* * * *
The “crimson thread” is strained
muchly. Sydney G.P.O. authorities have got
word from England that “ franked ” letters from
N.S.W. cannot be forwarded “Home”—said frank
stamp only being usable for the colonies. The
“franked” correspondence from N.S.W. is
mainly from Govt. House.

A POLITE REMINDER.
“I have called on yon, Miss —, to tell you that you should he more careful how you talk
about other people. Remember you were young yourself once.”
While Sydney Municipal Council quibbles
over reforms there is at least one—which does
meet much of the public—that might be at once
attended to. Scores of Chinese market-gardeners
bring loads of vegetable to Belmore markets three
times a week. Do they go back with empty
carts? No ; nearly every one returns with a load
of stable-manure and other foetid matters given
him in the city for the “taking away.” In a
word, the cart which brings in “ succulent ” vege-
tables takes away filth—with a Chinaman sitting
on top of it!

Maoriland leads the way with a law to give
power to set aside portion of a deceased man’s
estate—-no matter how otherwise willed-for his
widow and family. The Crotty case is bearing
fruit.
* * * *
Mayor Harris, in cutting down Sydney
Town Hall expenses in the way of feeding and
entertaining distinguished and other people, is
paving the way to a reduction of the Mayoral
allowance. What he saves he proposes to devote
to some object in the general citizens’ interest.
A loyal lady with a lovely lawn pressirigly
entreated N.S.W. Govt, to give or sell her the
carcase of the lately-deceased Soudan donkey.
Her purpose was to have it stuffed and exhibited
under glass on her beautiful grounds, as a
memento of the past and an exhortation to future
loyalty. Brunker would probably have yielded
if Sydney Museum had not collared the hide an
hour after the donkey’s death.
* * * *
When the Greek Gipsies reached Ballarat,
Mayor Shioppee had arranged to have the whole
tribe carted to St. Kilda in furniture-vans, and
dumped down on Premier George Turner’s door-
mat. Shoppee is one of those massive, municipal,
Tory magnates who hold a Radical Government
responsible for every evil that happens along,
from measles to drought. This time his scheme
was frustrated by a showman who opened negotia-
tions with the Gipsies, fenced them in, and
charged the curious populace fid. each to gaze upon
the frowsy strangers.
* * * *
Simons v. Simons has been the divorce-
case of the month in Melbourne. There was no
co. in this case, and respondent was all that a wife
should be, save for the fact that she refused to
accept petitioner for a husband. After bearing
several children to her lord and master, she took
violently to spiritualism, and suddenly concluded
that poor S. was not her particular affinity. An-
other illustration of the truth of the theory that
the woman with a fad can always content herself
without a fancy.

Dear Bulletin.— To raise funds for carry-
ing out the late Bismarck Commemoration at Syd-
ney Centenary Hall, a subscription-list headed
by Mr. Kempermann (German Consul-General),
with was sent to the leading German Club in
Sydney ; the members subscribed freely from =£l
to <£s, according to their means, and they were
rewarded with grey cards of invitation signed in
print—“P. Kempermann, President.” When
these members, their wives, and friends entered
the building, they learned to their astonishment
that the committee had sent out something like 800
red cards of invitation to people usually found at
Govt. House levees, for whom the committee had
reserved the whole body of the hall up to the
gallery, and who were received by Mr. Kemper-
mann and his satellites at a kind of levee, with a
private secretary calling out the names of the
comers, while those who contributed to pay the
piper (the holders of the grey tickets aforesaid)
were relegated to seats in the very back of the
hall, behind a barrier specially erected for the pur-
pose, as if they were outcasts and unfit to asso-
ciate with the high-toned invited guests ! Such
snobbish conduct may be the proper thing in
Germany, but surely not in Democratic Australia.
—An Australian by Choice.
* * * *
Editor Bulletin.— l understand that a
letter has been sent you for publication from some
disaffected party in re the arrangements with re-
gard to the Bismarck Commemoration at Sydney,
and I desire to explain to you that that letter, so
far as I am cognisant of its contents, is based upon
some misunderstanding. First of all, the guests for
whom the front seats in the body of the hall
were reserved were not those of the committee,
but those of “the Germans of Sydney,” and com-
prised only persons considered official, and talo n
from Mr. Critchett Walker’s list. The private
“ TOO EARLY.”—AN INFLUENZA-PNEU-

guests of the members of committee were treated
exactly the same as those of other subscribers.
For the subscribers were reserved the back rows in
the body of the hall and the whole of the first gallery
(the best places in the building). The barrier was
only made for the purpose of reserving them their
numbered seats against the inxited guests whose
seats were not numbered and the number of whose
attendance could not be gauged beforehand, the
invitations far exceeding the accommodation pro-
vided for them. The principal subscribers
would also have been admitted among the
guests if they chose to take the chance of getting
a seat, as some of them did. At all events
it would be wrong to blame Consul Kempermann
for any faulty arrangements, as neither he nor
myself had anything to do with the official invita-
tions, which were issued by a special sub-commit-
tee.—'Yours truly, Oscar Schultze.

Probably the most com-
fortable death that any man can
die was that which struck John
O’Shannassy a few days ago at
Deer Park (Vic.). O’Shannassy
worked at the big explosives
factory there—where they make
dynamite, gelignite, lithofrac -
teur and the like. The factory
consists of about 50 little build-
ings each with a high earthen
rampart round it, so that if one
happens to pop off it may have
a chance to go by itself and
not take the others with it.
O’Shannassy had one of these
little buildings all to himself,
and there he daily took his. life
in both hands for a small salary,
washing nitroglycerine. And
one day it went off so suddenly
that nothing was afterwards
found of O’Shannassy except
2'bs. of flesh and one half-boot.
All that could be recovered was
put in a small box—a good-sized
cigar-box would have held it all
- and taken to the morgue.
That is the ideal death—no
pain, no warning, no undertaker
calling round and leaving his
soiled card at the door in ad-
vance, no dreary acquaintances
dropping in to recommend the
faith-cure process.
The free and independent
showed up at a newly-returned
Sydney M.P’s. office, the other
morning, and this took place.
The elector was a West Indian
negro. Opening the door he
said: “Are you the membah we
elected down our way?” “Yes.”
“ Wall, I want you to introduce
me to the Premiah.” Slowly
the newly-anointed said, “ But
I don’t know the gentleman.
What, may I ask, do you wish to
be introduced for?” There
was a tragic-pathetic pause,
when the man from Jamaica
burst forth, “I want the Pre-
miah to get my sick wife into the
hospital.” The sad degradation
of suffering ignorance.
Some back-gate moralisings, for the benefit
of tired tradesmen :
The Chow vegetable-vendor (writes “Yowi” to The
Bulletin) is the refuge and asylum for ugly servant-girls.
The butcher and baker shy with true Australian rudeness
at this amorous and unfortunate female, but the Asiatic
squints loving compliments from his almond eyes, and
intones admiration in his catalogue of prices. The agility
with which Emma, our servant, deserts the life-story of
Lord Marchmont and the Lady Eleanor for the pigeon-
English of John is, at first, a marvel and a mystery to the
male mind. If a woman cannot get a man to love her,
she must have something that pretends. The ugly ser-
vant has the Chinaman, the old maid her cat. The house-
hold dog greets the other tradesmen in a boisterous
manner, but his bearing towards the Celestial is one of
chilling indifference ; it is different, however, with the
cat, who develops a lasting friendship with him. The
first time John came she nestled her head against the
Celestial shin, and purred ; next time, she was rewarded
with a piece of meat from the yellow man’s pocket. This
so impressed the servant-girl that we have had bad vege-
tables ever since.
* • * *
“Chow-Chow,” to The Bulletin, from
China: —
Woman must have association of some kind with males
of her species from time to time. In the larger ports in
China the ordinary run of white girl is fairly all right,
because she has plenty of men of her own rank and color
to flirt with. But the poor governess in the bigports and
the girl in the ports where whites are few and far between
have but a slack time. Then the quiet, cat-like, purring
houseboy, who is always about and who recognises
the right moment, has his innings. From one large
port in China four governesses have been hurriedly
packed off home within the last six months. And there
is now in another port, awaiting the arrival of a little
stranger, of whom the Corean houseboy—a married man
—is joint author, the very pretty daughter of a pro-
minent official.
Why don't we encourage the Chows to come to
Australia? They do make such splendid house-
servants.

One of the most deplorable sequelae of the
excessive use of intoxicating drinks is that a
person who breaks with the habit is liable ever
after to attacks of brain-twitch, which cause him
to write letters to the papers, explaining how he
was cured. In most cases, this brain-twitch is
more difficult to cure than was the original drink-
habit.
* * * *
'The Sydney magistrate who, under the
the penal clauses of N.S.W. Education Act,
ordered a man to pay a fine, or be imprisoned till
the rising of the court (a few minutes), for not
sending his child duly to school, was a mere
minnow compared with the Bench that tried the
Dubbo boiling-down offenders, in 1893. These,
for technical breaches of the slaughtering law,
were fined =£16,870 on Dec. 5, and <£67,480 the
week following (the fines being so much per head
of sheep and cattle slaughtered), or imprisoned
till thG rising of thG court. The defendants
elected to “take it out,” and left the court a
minute after with the law-and-order Bench.

During last N.S.W. divorce-sittings a
junior barrister was taking more time over his
case than pleased Judge G. B. bimpson. His
Honor could not shut up the persistent junior.
At 11.30 Mr. (say) Jones, the counsel engaged m
the case next on the list, came into court, lhe
Judge seized his chance : “Ah ! Mr. Jones, said
he with a sigh, “ I think I can state that I won t
take your case till 2 o’clock. ’ At 2 o clock Bar-
rister Jones came in and found the unabashed
iunior still on the floor. His Honor again. 1
am really very sorry, Mr. Jones, to have brought
you away from chambers where, I am sure, you
are busy, and to keep you waiting while the time
of the court is being wasted as it is now—say
3 o’clock.” At 3 o’clock the same ceremony was
gone through, only the Judge was still more pro-
fuse in his apologies to Jones, ending with “ Well,
say 10 o’clock to-morrow. ”
Writes Mr. L. Brack, the Sydney medical
agent and publisher :
lie a recent Bulletin paragraph stating that “ the right
to the possession of a medical prescription threatens to
be soon tested in a Sydney court,” allow me to inform
you that a prescription belongs to the patient, although in
America there have been efforts to make it belong to the
writer. I doubt whether any chemist would have the
audacity to claim a prescription as his property. In most
countries the chemist is bound by law to copy each pre-
scription, but in Australia it is quite a common practice
of chemists to annex the prescription and thereby escape
making a copy of it. If a patient desires a copy of a
prescription from his medical attendant the latter is en-
titled to his usual fee for giving one. lam not aware of
any legal decision ever having been given upon the points
raised, but I fail to see how so plain an issue would lead
to litigation.

At a Sydney police-court, lately, the In-
spector of Nuisances prosecuted a man for allow-
ing some cattle to stray in the streets. That was
right enough ; but such prosecutions should be
conducted fairly. Instead of charging the man
witli one offence, as he could have done, the in-
spector had the gall to issue four summonses, one
for each animal. Result to defendant: four con-
victions, four fines, and the costs of four sum-
monses. The alternatives came to six days’ im-
prisonment, and defendant had to go to gaol!
Monstrous !

“Melb.”: Then is doomsday near. One
of our best-known pawnshops is just shut, and to
let. Such a thing has not occurred in the memory
of the oldest inhabitant. The goodwill of a
pawnery has been like that of a pubbery. Another
Boom on hand ?
* • * *
On the bakery question :
Dear Bulletin. —Luckily for themselves, the people
who wrote to you re bakers’ shops don’t know as much
about the matter as I do, or they might give the staff of
life a long rest. I was, a few years back, on the Melb.
Board of Works surveys and went through every house
and building in a certain suburb. Within the boundaries
were about ten bakeries, all of which I visited, and at
nearly all of which I learned something I didn’t want to
know. Out of the ten only three were conducted with as
much cleanliness and care as is possible in a bake-house —
and that is much less than the outside public believe
the rest being a mixture of stable, fowl-house, lavatory
and bake-house. The stench from the stable happily
overcame all the rest, for if its volume was greater its
pungency was less than the others. The lavatory 7, how-
ever, obtruded itself when you reached the corner of the
inixing-vats that abutted upon it, though the workmen
who ever and again came from the stable would remedy
this by bringing back a stinking, viscous mass of mud on
their boots. This mud in course of time would fall on
the floor and unite with the loose flour which was ever
and again shovelled up into the vats. Meanwhile the
fowls gleefully fattened on all the loose dough they could
steal, and the only 7 break in the monotony was when a
foolhardy rooster would fall into the vats and have the
surplus dough scraped from him with a piece of hoop-
iron which I believe was generally used for cleaning out
the choked stable-drain. Altogether, it was a pleasant
job measuring up those bake-houses, and the average
surveyor was glad when he got free away to a tenth-rate
slaughter-house, or an ill-kept “ sanitary ” yard.—M‘G.

* * * *
“ The frequency of suicides among
Chinese prisoners” (writes the travelled “Von
Kotze ” to The Bulletin) “is a remarkable
feature all over the Eastern world; loneliness, as
‘ Timi ’ suggests, has little to do with it. The
Chinaman will hang himself on the slightest pro-
vocation; his indifference to death makes him a
ready victim to epidemics in unwholesome
climates, as, for instance, the E. coast of Sumatra.
Like the kanaka, he gives in at once ; but, unlike
Tommy Tanna, he doesn’t care a rap. When the
Chartered co. in German Papua was in full blast,
about 10 years ago, a batch of Chinese laborers,
who had deserted from the plantations at the
head-station, Einschhafen, were sentenced to 10
lashes each (Sumatra planters’ style). They were
to receive their punishment in the morning, and
over-night were imprisoned on a hulk in the har-
bor —seven of them. Next day they had mysteri-
ously disappeared. Swimming was out of ques-
tion on account of constitutional hydrophobia and
ground-sharks. Everybody was dumbfounded.
Then a boat entered the harbor, and the skipper
asked in horror : ‘ What have those fellows been
doing, strung up on the hulk there ? ’ And subse-
quent investigation revealed on the seaward side
of the hulk the spectacle of the seven vanished
defaulters suspended by the neck, ropes tied to
the bulwark rail, feet near the water-line, swaying
mournfully to and fro with the movement of the
hulk. After that, with Chinese cases, punish-
ment followed delinquency without unnecessary
adjournments.”

Ben Tillett, to Melb. Tocsin :
Mrs. B ’s night-shirt is alright—a bit long, though.
(I mean the shirt Mrs. B made for me.)
This is the embroidered night-gown publicly pre-
sented to him at Spencer-street railway station on
his departure. By the way, The Bulletin had
imagined that only Wesleyan parsons, Sunday-
school superintendents, &c., clung to the archaic
night-shirt. To them pyjamas are distinctly Im-
moral, a snare of the Evil One.

The bill to amend the marriage laws, now
before Yic. parliament, knocks the bottom out of
the private parson nuptial-industry by forbidding
marriage-celebration by anybody not an officiating
minister of a denomination of which ministers are
already registered, or of a denomination the
Governor in Council may gazette as such for the
purpose of the Act ; and a requisition must be
further first submitted signed by the Victorian
denominational head, or by two registered minis-
ters of any denomination. The moral of all which
is simply that the State should recognise no
religious marriage ivithout marriage by registrar,
and any religious marriage with it.

A recent Sydney daily paper advt. for
board and lodging in respectable family elicited 187
replies (in one day). Suggests a way by which
the imaginary-surplus Govt, could increase its
postal revenue. Cook might daily pop in a
few advts. for board and lodging for Christian
young gentlemen ; an average of 180 replies would
mean a 15s. outlay in stamps.
THE BUSH POET SPEAKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tell me not in future numbers
That our thought becomes inane,
That our metre halts and lumbers,
When the Wattle blooms again.
Time may change this loyal jernal
From religious to profane ;
But a rhythmic law eternal
Makes the Wattle bloom again.
Trust no Flossie, howe’er pleasant;
Sweeps are treacherous ; totes are vain ;
Banks and scrip are evanescent —
But the Wattle blooms again.
Cultivate no fair ideal;
Own no country seat in Spain ;
All these things must go to Sheol,
Whilst the Wattle blooms again.
Thus, you see, austere and lonely,
Sailing o’er life’s solemn main,
One great fact is certain only—
That the Wattle blooms again.
TOM COLLINS.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 23, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
The Madden matrimonial glare doesn’t quite
obscure our own weddingof the week, that of Miss
Virginia Constance Martin, the late Chief Justice
Martin’s sixth daughter, with Mr. E. E. D. Arden,
of Bucks, Eng. Daileys, Darleys, Inneses, Salo-
mons, Mannings, Wentworths, Macarthurs, Pol-
locks, Allens, I aithfulls—all the old and new legal
families of the country were represented at the
festivity. Sedate but chic gowning marked the
wedding for its own, and white satin and veil
played their old, old parts.
Women in Parliament! Here are a few samples
of what happens when they are let in for one hour.
No. 1. Usher: “Those seats are reserved,
madam. ” Madam You don’t know who I am. ”
“Those seats are reserved for the Judges.” “I
am Mrs. Dash. It’s not often I come to a thing
of this kind.” “Will you kindly move from those
seats, madam?” “O, ’old your tongue! Cissy,
come and sit over alongside me, there’s plenty of
room. If the Judges do come they won’t turn us
out.” “But I must insist, madam.” “And I
know the Judges, and I know they would be
only too glad to let me ’n Cissy have their
seats. My goodness, it’s not often I come
to a thing of this kind.” “Great goodness,
I hope not.” But there was not much hope in his
voice. No. 2—“ Pardon me, madam, those seats
are reserved. No answer. “ Excuse me, madam
those seats are reserved.” No answer. “Madam
will you kindly move from those seats : they are
reserved for the Consuls.” No answer. “Will
you get out of those seats, please.” A whisper:
Don t take any notice of liim. ” “Do you ladies
hear me ask you to give up the Consuls’ seats.”
Another whisper: “ Don’t look at him—he’ll stop
m a minute.” . . . If women get into Parliament,
men will have to write their names large upon
their pews.
~ A wild crowd of early spring millinery shed
itsell m all possible and impossible places at Wed-
nesday s ceremony. Wherever you looked there
were women. “The youngest bishop in the colo-
nies had a giddy time in the row where he was the
only cleric. Mrs. Reid, in a navy coat and skirt
and a big black hat, came slipping modestly into
her seat, and sat like a mouse under a heavy fire
of eyes feminine. A quaint remark struck the ear
That’s Mrs. Reid, isn’t she slim!
Well to be her with all her worries.” This novel
bit of envy came from a pretty, plump actress who
nnds things theatrical going so smoothly just now
that she grows plumper and plumper every day
One curious bevy of Eves stormed the Speaker’s
private apartments, peering into his bath, looking
at themselves m his looking-glass, and making
themselves generally at home and obnoxious,
oome people must be glad that Parliament does
not open every week.
Art’s spring quickening has been agitating local
artists all the month. Mr. Sid. Long, Society of
Artists President, has worked his poetic imagin-
ings into a big canvas called “Pan,” an idyll in
green rushes and blue waters that would cool the
cast-iron wall of a bush building in mid-summer.
George Lambert has a Circe, with sullen lips and
rebellious eyes. Miss Meston has been teaching
so , a the year that she has nothing ready for
exhibition. Mr.. Moir’s private view last week
showed a portrait of Dr. Maclaren in a purple tie
and several water-color landscapes. Tom Roberts
is keeping all his good things to himself this year,
iL !lth< T society being privileged to exhibit them!
c T rexhibit a water-color portrait
of little Miss Burdekin, and Mary Stoddart is
finishing the likeness of a big white cow.
Mr. Gordon Coutts’ largest canvas, “The Cloud
that Comes to All, is all about an old man and
fiis daughter m a nice green garden, and a black-
edged letter that has just arrived. His vivid
xr\Ti ? lc^ r ? of “ our ’arbor,” viewed from
M Mahon s I omt, smites the eye cheerfully, and
various little bush studies keep up the Aus-
tra lan spirit carefully. Prank Maliony secedes
from Society of Artists this year, and is sending
his work to the rival camp.
Our artists have also been plying their brushes
. r Florence Schmidts benefit concert—result
window cards extraordinary. Tom Roberts has
achieved a fetching back view of the singer in the
act of a song. A leaf-green gown makes nice relief
tor her warm hair, and a huge audience is artfully
suggested. The concert comes off next Saturday
and to honor what many good people think the
finest voice that has ever gone out of Australia, a
bumper house ought to arise that it may call itself
blessed m the years to come.
By the way, Florence Schmidt’s one and only
public vocalising off her concert platform will be
made at Ethel Turner’s at-home, which takes
plane in the Society of Artists Exhibition Rooms
I riday, September 2nd.
Httle grumble in German is travelling about
the city this week. Listening, one gathers that
the artless German mind has not been able to
withstand the soiled and ragged conventions of
Sydney Society. It seems that tickets for the
local Bismarck celebrations were sent out in two
colors—pmk and grey. The pink ’uns were let in
by the front door, announced in a loud voice and
received by a body of the very best Germans.
Grey meant the back of the house, behind a speci-
ally-erected barrier, or up in the regions of the
hang-overs. The pmk people amounted to 800
were frescoed and flesh-potty, and could find Govt’
House m the dark. When the hall was filled a
musical evening began, decorated with a big white
Bismarck and flowers and funereal draperies
Well, but to raise money for these adornments,
ord^ ar 7 , ca, l Germans had subscribed heartily,
and had duly received from the very best Germans
cards for the party. Presenting these on the fatal
night, they were hustled away upstairs, or to the
back of the house. Their cards were grey—that
was final. And the eight hundred pinks, who had
paid, nothing, were invited, announced, received,
put into the best seats, and kept carefully away
from the goats—l mean the greys. This little
premature rehearsal of the world-to-come is still
sending spasms through the ordinary German heart
•of the city.
Apart from these dark deeds, the evening was
delightful. Truly German in its spirit, the music
might have charmed a block of old red-sandstone.
A spirited little orchestra under Sydney Moss’s
guidance played some of Beethoven’s Symphonie
Eroica with a fine feeling for the music that in-
spired Browning’s Prospice. Herr Staedgen’s
round nch voice rolled out an emotional cry to
Be True Till Death, ” and the House chafed
severely under the printed command, “No
applause. One isolated clap from an over-
wrought ,grey brought down a hurricane of
hushes, and the agonies of the encore-fiend that
night must have been ghastly. His only happy
moment came at the close of the winding pane-
gyric _ on Bizzy, done in German-English first,
then m pure German.
Later on in the week the decorations merged
themselves into a big sheet for Mr. Clement
Wragges lectures. Over the sheet storms and
cyclones and solar quarrels and pictures of Kos-
ciusko showed up in a limelight glare. Mr.
Wragge surprised me by being a lean, lonely,
weather-beaten man with a sandy beard, a pre-
occupied eye, and a dry and tuneless voice.
1 eople known only by rumor so seldom turn out
m one s.dream-mould. Mr. Ingleby, the assistant,
who chirped in towards the end of the lecture,
might have also happened in a dream, after lob-
sters. Long-haired, clad in a tight blue suit but-
toned to the chin, and capped all the time with a
squiffy tani-o’-shanter that couldn’t rise if he had
asked it to, which he didn’t, while he was telling
his little tale about Kosciusko, a portrait flared
out prematurely on the sheet and brought him to a
dead stop. Mr. Wragge then came forward, and
explained that that there photograph was Mr.
Ingleby, on which a picture of Mr. Wragge fol-
lowed artlessly, and Clement himself had to retire
behind a limelight shadow.
Many interesting things flitted in and out of the
talky-talky. For it wasn’t exactly a lecture, and
it wasn’t in the least like a sermon. Dry chuckles
came from the Queensland meteor’s throat as he
discoursed of storms that defied the boundaries of
unfederated States, and the word humbug was
thrown in lightly. Absence of frill and quaintness
9* expression marked all the speaker’s comments.
God was an “infinite dynamo,” and the storms
were “he’s” and “she’s.” As for Kosciusko, if
Sydney doesn’t migrate to its summits next scorch,
the Queenslander can’t blame his faithful and en-
ticing description of life up there. In fact, a lady
who would inflate an ordinary-sized tent began in-
quiries as soon as the lecture was over.
Nasty shock for some people to find the “ Yeo-
men of the Guard,” at Her Majesty’s, had left St.
Pauli behind. Like all saints, he had to be gone
before you fixed the maximum of his value. Any-
way, the golden-fair rushed homewards from the
‘Yeomen” with loud expressions of disappoint-
ment at the play and the acting, and the draught,
and the man who sat behind, and the woman who
sat m front, and finally thought it was a pity that
Mr. Pauli was not playing. Ah ! but it is the
casual finally that tells.
In this show the men have the gaudiest gowns.
Puffy scarlet garbs, pinched in here and there
with black velvet, and crowned with a flower-
trimmed belltopper, point to the frisky lives of
wiese good old Beefeaters. George Lauri, as
Jack Point, made the most of his chance to wear
two coats at once, and completed his oddity with
variegated shoes and socks. Little Dolly Vane—
in short skirts again—had a white overskirt draped
back from a miniature quilted petticoat, and the
dash of red velvet in her corselet repeated itself
somewhere in her babyish little bonnet. Mabelle
Darley was more pleasing to the eye than in her
Gondolier gown; a vivid cap looked happy on her
fair hair, and a pretty fawn frock with black velvet
corselet, spangled emerald back drape, and rain-
bow ribbons, brightened things up considerably.
Fanny Liddiard and Nellie Stewart used to play
Phoebe Mcrril and Elsie Maynard 5 and, curiously,
Miss Vane, in Fanny’s part, has a strong facial
resemblance to her predecessor, while Miss Dar-
Ly s arrangement of her golden locks suggested
our Nellie m a loud whisper.
University Chancellors are deteriorating since
the good old days” of Judge Manning. Dr.
Maclaunn has never become too popular with the
students, and since he told them to stay away from
their own Commemoration of ’96, their animosity
has only been smothered, not extinguished. The
last straw has now come in the shape of a curt
refusal from the Doctor to allow the women
students the use of their Common Room for an
evening “At Home.” The anti-Federalist is
afraid the students and their friends might dance
in it as they did once before. “And if they did
dance,. what ? the sweet girls are indignantly
enquiring, J

wifl give the University a little of the shaking-up
it so badly needs. 8 1
How “shoppiness” does cling! When the
Chinatown co. was given a holiday on the occasion
of poor Sadie Macdonald’s death, the members of it
spent the evening witnessing “Henry V.” Having
nl rph t warbling and piano-pounding in
the 1 own Hall, the Lemmone Concert co. turned
up in full force at the same vast hall to hear the
Amateur Orchestral Concert. And the Toynbee
Ouild which has been lately established in Sydney,
and is going in just now for a vigorous crusade in
tavor of the Early-Closing Act, met with an un-
looked-for check at the very hands of those they
wish to benefit. Some of Anthony Hordern’s em-
ployes, who are now released at 1 p.m. on Satur-

the afternoon at Mark Foy’s !
Unitarians in Sydney have been stirred to the
dregs lately by the wild doings of George Joseph -
of-Canaan Walters. This cleric, rebelling against
a salary of threepenny bits, the number of
same being limited to half the numbers of his
average congregations, has burst the bonds twixt
him and his churchmen, and starts off on a line of
his own. Other rumors include stories of dissen-
sions between organist and parson. But was
there ever an organist that did not consider him-
self of more importance than the parson? The
oldest, stalest bone of eontention in church
i. 1S ® rs^.^e choir, and second the man who
sets the harmonium wheezing—or woman, in which
actions multiply abnormally. A feature
of Mr. Walters’ church that used to attract people
with feelings, was his unusual type of hymn.
Poems by Emerson, Browning, Tennyson and
other poet-thinkers came like a drink in the desert
after the fevered drivel of “ Alleluia-’tis-done-I-
believe -m - the - Son, ” and “ Who-went-up-in-the-
hery - chariot - safe - to - the - Promised - Land? ”
But, possibly, just here is where a “yorganist”
would differ. The joy of setting iris instru-
ment rolling round unlimited alleluias and
hosannas could have no compensation in accom-
panying words whose beauty and meaning, would
eclipse their musical setting. Anyhow, Mr.
Walters has taken himself and his hymn-book to a
new church, which is to be opened shortly by Rev.
Dr. Strong, of Melbourne.

** Of course women went mad over the Madden-
Vallange wedding. For hours before time, the
female population of ten miles round was massing
itself about the Cathedral. Knowing ones were
praying in the holy place betimes, for though
admission to the ceremony was “ by ticket only,”
officials couldn t well refuse benefit of sanctuary
to nine o clock religieuses. A mourner in widow’s
weeds knelt in a picked spot, and there “ took
on so violently under her new crape veil and so
ostentatiously lamented the departed that, as no
one had nerve to toss the wounded dove out of the
Ark, she took her trial to the proper place in
prayer, at her leisure, and only jumped off her
roost at the end of the show.
Strange the curiosity re marriages displayed by
battered frumps who, judging by appearances,
have themselves come such horrible croppers in
matrimony.

cheerful poem, ‘ The Vacant Chair.’ At the gift-

, , . youthful Duke of Manchester, after
looking m at the Madden wedding, turned up at

prev lously, is utterly unlike his aged progenitor
save m the respect that both showed an utter lack
of the indescribable something which is supposed
i ' oorent'ate the aristocracy from the common
plebs the old duke looked like a superannuated
larm laborer, while his descendant would pass for

vdlam in one scene remarking that he has had to

that the pictures must follow them. ‘But, master,’

sell the pictures: they’ve been in the family 300
years. ‘ All the more reason they should travel
and see the world a bit, ’ responds the callous
villain, forgetful of the duke in the audience. A
loyal manager like J. C. Williamson shouldn’t
allow such mistakes. Whenever a duke or other
exalted personage is to be present the book should
be carefully exj3urgated of anything like person-
alities.
Recent election ot Melb. Benev. Asylum com-

Suburban afternoon trains and trams reap a
good harvest just now. Most women are darting
eagerly about from one drapery to another in-
specting Spring shows. The things are all much
of a muchness : we are all going to be very spotty
and very wrinkly, apparently. Same old accordeon-
pleatmg effects on all sides, and even ostrich
feathers are peppered over with little black fluffs.
V eils are going out, it is rumored, as face-covers;
now they are tied up over the hats instead, which
still have a squashed appearance. Quills which
pertly cock up on one side of many chapeaux are
dotted with black spangles. This spotty notion
must originate in Smoketown surely, because a
smut more or less doesn’t count now. Parasols
wrapped up look untidy rag bundles, but unfurled
are misty masses of muslin which will cruelly wilt
m the summer sun, and must be kept in a water-
proof tent or under the bed when it rains, as one
drop will reduce them to pulp; but they fulfil
their mission by being very pretty and uncommon
dear, blurts are altered—an “apron front” is the
ne Tl st ut .k lous es are still the same, baggy
and betnmmed with beads, or bits of glass or tin-
smith s cuttings snipped into tinsel, or anything
shiny that comes handy.
Yours affectionately,

CURffD BY WARNER’S SAEE CURE !
Air. Jack Lyons, the well known butcher, of
North Terrace, Adelaide, in a letter, Feb. 14, ’9B,
says : —“ I had been a sufferer for years with liver
and kidney complaint, and could never get any
real relief until about 12 months ago a friend
recommended me to use Warner’s Safe Cure. I
had tried nearly eserything else, and thought I
might as well give it a trial. After the first bottle
I began to feel a little better, and after taking five
bottles I was completely cured. I can conscien-
tiously recommend it to anyone suffering from
liver or kidney disease, and they will find it a
God-send to man.” *

Pastor Puckle, Church of England, died
at Essendon, Melb., last week, in his 99th year.
The oldest Anglican clergyman on record except
one.
The Reid Government has appointed four
Judges—two Simpsons, Cohen, and Walker—to
N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench, at the rate of one
per year.
F. B. Suttor, the elect of Bathurst, has
held office under Parkes, Jennings, and Dibbs at
stages when each was the political enemy of the
other two.

During the last royal progress through
Toowoomba (Q.) of Premier Byrnes, someone
called for “ three cheers for Mrs. Byrnes.’’ When
the crowd was informed that Tommy is still a
bachelor, an old German yelled “ Ach, den, nefer
mindt, poor poy, I soon findt you a Missus Bre-
mier allrighdt! ”
The new N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge,
Gregory Walker, Q.C., leader of the Equity
Bar, is the 32nd appointed. The first, Elias
Bent, arrived with Gov. Macquarie in 1809,
being the outcome of the Bligh - Johnston-
M‘Arthur troubles. Bent is buried in the St.
Thomas cemetery, North Sydney, in the same
tomb as Capt. Ovens.
The 83rd anniversary of the arrival in
Sydney of Rev. Samuel Leigh, the first Wesleyan
minister to land in Australia, recalls the fact that
Gov, Macquarie strenuously resisted the intrusion
of Wesleyans into his community. Macquarie
assumed authority in things spiritual as well as
temporal, and didn’t see “what good Methodists
could be in this settlement.”
Charles Counsell, who polled three votes
for Alma, at the recent N.S.W. general election,
returned thanks for those three, from a Broken
Hill balcony, a few nights’ later. “ I am disap-
pointed at the result,” he wailed ; “ sadly disap-
pointed. I expected quite 10 votes.” Counsell,
by the way, is unexcelled as a penman in, say,
Australia—and there his abilities end.
J. H. Angas, South Australia’s gilded
cattle-breeder, who has now reached his 75th year,
has had his philanthropy still further spurred by
the death of his only surviving sister, Mrs. Evans.
He has made over the Belair Inebriates’ Retreat
(of which he was mortgagee) to the trustees, and
is willing to go most of the way in establishing a
girls’ college at Adelaide under Nonconformist
auspices.
On Oct. i, N.S.W, Chief Justice Darley
will enter on his 69th year. In the following
month he will have completed his 12th year on the
Bench. In January he will have been 46 years in
the legal profession. His uncle, W. E. Darley,
Q. 0., is still the “ father ”of the Irish bar. He
was called in Michaelmas term, 1830, in which
term, actual date Sept. 30, the N.S.W. Chief
Justice was born.

In a certain N.S.W. test-appeal, plaintiff’s
counsel asked the Chief Justice not to give costs
against his client, because the case was the first of
its kind. The C. J. refused, adding that the Crown
would, as usual, deal fairly with the applicant.
Counsel again pressed, but ineffectually. Where-
upon Salomons, Q.C. (for Crown): “I will see
that your Honor's intentions are carried out.”
Then, turning to departmental solicitor, with a
playful wink : “ Don’t charge Dodds anything in
this matter ! —increase my fees !

over Terah Hooley’s Scriptural handicap. Yet
the name of Ebenezer Syme is not altogether un-
familiar in Melbourne journalism.

One of the few remaining pioneers of
Port Phillip passed over last week. Mr. Charles
Stone arrived from Van Diemen’s Land m too 7,
and resided at Brighton for 61 years. He was 78
at his death.
In N.S.W. Leg. Council are but two men
’ whose fathers had seats in the same Chamber
Jago Smith and Septimus Stephen. The fathers
of “ Jack ” Want and W. H. Suttor sat in the old
“ Mixed ” Legislature.
Carl Harden, who lately shot himself at
Rockhampton, had been a continuous resident of
Sheolville for 39 years. He came to Australia in
’52. Well-to-do, seemingly, and of good repute,
his successful spasm of suicide is puzzling the
post-mortem critics.

Thomas Broughton, born at Windsor
(N.S.W.) in 1810, and Mayor of Sydney in 1847,
entered upon his 89th year on Sunday last, and
on same day celebrated his diamond wedding.
Mr. Broughton was atone time a member of the
Leg. Assembly, but for some years has been out of
politics.

public per medium of a column-long letter in
Northern Miner that to allude to Kaiser Wil-
helm as “ Sibv Billy” is lese Majeste ; such out-

Lord Arthur Hill has retired from the
House of Commons, where he was Tory whip.
One of his alleged reasons for pique was that he
had, in his own opinion, “ some claims to a
Colonial Governorship” which Chamberlain re-
fused to recognise. 1 here you have an example
of how Governors are foisted on to “ the kaw-
linies.” Hill had been a useful party hack ; there-
fore he had “claims” to be appointed to a good
billet with a good salary, paid by “loyal sub-
jects ”on the Hobson’s choice principle. Hill is a
fair specimen of the sort of men who become
Governors. He is good looking, ambitious, and
not brainy ; his wife writes drawing-room songs of
some merit, and is likewise ambitious. And
because a billet is not found for Arthur he retires
in a huff. Some colony has had an escape from
one incubus, but only to be saddled with another.

Speakers Abbott and Coles, of N.S.W.
and S.A. respectively, decline to treat members of
Assembly to the time-honored sessional dinners.
The N.S.W. Speaker knocked the treat off when
he took the chair, eight years ago ; Cole followed
the example three years back.

“ Old Jack “ Sabretasche ” omits many
names of old-time Victorian police cadets. M°s-
tyn, afterwards sporting editor on bydney JiELL s
Life and Town and Country, belonged to the
corps. “ Orion ” Horne was of them, and oin-
clair, who succeeded to a Scottish title and com-
fortable estate. As for the morality of the blue
and silver boys, it was as sweet as that of a crack
cavalry regiment. “ Sabretasche ” lumps them all
together as “ unsavory,” and says they borrowed
money from their subordinates. Well, 1 have
known two-thirds of the officers of a frigate bor-
row regularly from the ship’s tailor. Indefensible,
doubtless, but why unsavory ?
Died in the West, little George Stevens-
like so many other Australian newspaper men, a
graduate from one of the Adelaide dailies. He
served the Age syndicate during the silver-boom,
married a music-hall artist, got under the fringe
of the “profesh,” but finally drifted back to news-
paper-hacking. Stevens it was who, when there
was much hanky-panky at B.H. Junction, and
when the mine was closed to reporters, accom-
panied another pressman on a series of daring
surreptitious visits. So well did they plan and
work that they baffled an exasperated manage-
ment which was understood to be waiting along-
side the shaft most of the time with a gun.
At last ex-Wesleyan parson and ex- Vic.
M.L.A. Carter’s torpedo looks as though it were
going to have a chance to kill somebody. The
Melb. co. which financed the inventor has agreed
to sell to an English co. which he has been organ-
ising for many weary months. Carter is a curious
blend of enthusiasm, determination, and the
capacity for making enemies. When, resigning
his pulpit in S. A. on account of the latter virtues,
he saw an opening in Vic. politics, he set out to
fill it on a credit balance of Is. 7d., or so, and
thereupon sailed in to a short-lived membership
for Port Melb.; and, when Pt. Melb. fired him out,
he sat down and incubated this new Kerristian
contrivance.

Dr. Wolfe, the eminent Melb. oculist, a
violent critic of the anti-toxin theory, points out
that Prof. Marshall-Hall wrote those giddy sap-
phics while just recovering from severe diphtheria
coupled with the inoculation of anti-toxin—on
which general diphtheritic paralysis supervened.
Dr. Wolfe urged that the matter be regarded in
its pathological aspect, and that the University
give the Professor a month or two’s holiday “ to
allow of the remainder of the horse, or ass juice,
being eliminated from his system.”
BUSINESS ITEM.—THE IMPORTANCE OF
BEING HENRY ARTHUR.
GLADYS : “ Please, mister, if yer gives me a bag of
peanuts I’ll let yer kiss Henry Arthur !”
A certain titled personage now touring Aus-
tralia caused much talk on the voyage out by hi*
generous attitude on the liquor question. More-
over, he announced that he intended to teach Aus-
tralians how to bet—a decision which will be wel-
come news to Moses and co., who have had a rocky
time of late. One other aristocrat who struck Aus-
tralia (and S.A. particularly) with similar views,
two years ago, went back a poorer but wiser
youth.
John MTnfcyre, barrister-at-law, Middle
Temple, 1875, and a member of Tasmania’s lead-
ing non-political firm of lawyers, is to be appointed
third Judge in the Land of Shadows. Solicitor-
General Dobson, brother of the deceased Chief
Justice, was looked upon as a certainty, by his
clan, but brother Henry is not now Premier, and
Premier Braddon seems to have decided that
Tasmania is just about full-up of the judicial
reign of Dobson.
Recently died, Edward Bevan, surveyor,
water-color artist, journalist, story-writer, in-
ventor, tenor-singer, orchardist, and general all-
round good fellow. Some three years ago he was
attacked by an incurable disease, and it may be
safely said that no man under sentence of death
ever bore up more bravely. His sunny and genial
disposition never changed to the last. Originally
a surveyor, always a dabbler in art and journal-
ism, he left the former for the latter alternately ;
and even combined them both in the editorship of
the defunct Illustrated Sydney News. Lat-
terly he devoted himself to an orchard in the
Dural district.
Dear Bulletin. —The only epigrammatic
fragment of Marshall Hall’s for which you can find
a word of praise is that about pious and pro-
miscuous King David ; but surely this is merely a
very ancient chestnut in a new shell. See
Granger's “King of Yvetot ” :
Such crowds of pretty girls he found
Occasion to admire,
It gave his subjects double ground
For greeting him as Sire !
Of the Ormond poet, I should say he might be
forgiven for being a great deal worse if he were
much better. —S.S.
A choice story is attached by N.S.W. Par-
liamentarians to a Ministerialist nonentity who
bruited the fact about that he was going to make
a scathing attack on “ Barney ” Wise, then acting
as freelance in support of the fallen Parkes.
Someone gave the hint to Wise, who sat himself
immediately opposite his antagonist just after
that individual had begun to spatter mud. The
keen stare of his butt entirely disconcerted
the orator, who stopped in the middle of a sen-
tence, turned a sickly yellow, and, after apologis-
ing to the Speaker on the score that he bad sud-
denly taken ill, hurried out of the Chamber.
Since then he has always been one Of the mildest-
mannered men in Parliament.
An obituary. There died the other day,
at Hawthorn (Vic.), Alfred Massina, one of
the firm of Massina and Sons, printers and
publishers, Melbourne. He was a man, for
whose memory the many hungry haphazards
who, as S. V. Winter elegantly puts it, “live on
the skirts of the press,” must have generous
thought. His guinea always seemed fresh from
the mint, and worth far more than ordinary coin.
In short, he was one of those who, without being
brilliant, make an impression which brilliant men
often fail to accomplish, and which was a reflec-
tion of a frank and honest nature. He went over
at 44 years of age, which was harder luck for his
friends, perhaps, than for himself.^-G.B.
The pious editor of a southern religious
monthly is still quaking over his recent narrow-
escape. A fellow-laborer in the vineyard was to
supply some copy about the virtue of resignation ;
and, after proofs had been finally revised and put
aside for the printer, a rev. visitor picked up one
“ pull ” in the office, began to read, and nearly
fell down dead when he struck : “ Sickness is not
without many consolations; but a good deal
depends upon the venus one has chosen ” ! The
printer was clamoring for his stuff, and there was
just time to ring-up the contributor for an explana-
tion. But if, as the holy man averred, he had
written “ venue,” the devil must have tampered
with the MS., which showed “Venus” plain
enough.

muscular, and rather good-looking fellow for an

Hard-pressed by long drought, an out-
back squatter sold 8000 sheep at a ruinously low
rate to be delivered on the Lachlan, 60 miles off.
Took charge himself. No grass, and little water,
and weak sheep perishing in hundreds. As
he rode despondingly along he was suddenly
accosted by a new-chum (remarkable for a fond-
ness for pets) who was the “ generally-useless ” of
the run over which the sheep were crawling,
“ You look very down-hearted this morning, Mr.
0.,” remarked the jackaroo. “Well, yes, I
reckon I have as good cause to be down in the
mouth as ever any man had.” “Indeed?” says
jackaroo. “Ah, well, I can sympathise with
you; I m in bitter trouble myself. I’ve ”—his
vmce broke with emotion—‘ I’ve lost my butcher-
bird! , , ,
