Grabbit : Concerning cats as destroyers
of rabbits and the statement that they will enter
warrens to prey. A few years ago, when droughts
were perennial, the rabbits were driven into
Broken Hill in thousands. The cats did eat some,
it is true ; but they often became very friendly—
used to occupy the same apartments under the
houses, which are mostly built on raised wooden
bio, 8> Rabbits are not used as food in Broken
Hill now.

“Jim”: Be household cat and rabbiu.
My old cat often followed the dogs when out
rabbiting, and, although not able to catch bunny
on the run, was splendid amongst the bushes.
Used to sneak up and grip them at the back of
the neck, and I have never known one to g. 6
away from her.
• * • *
“ Brolga ” :
He kangaroo’s use of tail— experimentum feci. Bode
out to a stretch of snow-clad country, muzzled a stag-
hound, and started a mob of “ roos ” in about 6in. of snow,
then dismounted and found that the track of each left
three furrows in the snow, all penetrating to the ground.
Followed them into marshy ground with about J4in. of
snow, and the mark of the tail was depressed Biin. Tn the
ground. As to effect of tail-loss, I had two pet half-
grown “roos,” one of which had had its tail so mutilated
in a trap that I had cut it off. The animals were running
in a small paddock into which I used sometimes to turn
a couple of muzzled dogs, when the tailless “ roo ” would
make instantly for a dam, and the other straight for the
fence and take it at one bound, as the tailless one had
frequently tried in vain to do.
* * * *
“Lurcher”: Be tailless kangaroos. I
started a stumpy-tail on the top of a ridge, and
although it “ shifted the scenery ” at a great rate,
it was only to view it upside down, for every 3rd
jump or so it went heels over head. It proved an
easy capture because the kangaroo, having such a
“ rake ” for’ard, needs its heavy tail as a counter-
poise when going down hill. It is correct also
that kangaroos don’t tail the ground when travel-
ling ; but, when feeding, the old man on watch
does thump the ground with his tail as an alarm
signal. In a dry time one may watch kangaroos
at one’s ease by taking up a position close to a
tank or water-trough. Should a whiff of tobacco
float down to the old man, he will sit-up suddenly
with working nostrils and ears, then take a few
hops towards the main body and bring his tail
down like a flail upon a bag of corn-cobs. The
signal makes the lot sit-up, till, when the old man
has located the danger, he gives another “ whack ! ”
and away they all go. A kangaroo’s leg is broken
as easily as a clay-pipe stem. I have fired at one
and missed—yet the animal had a leg broken by
its sudden start. Once saw a kangaroo sitting up
and cocking his ears about, and, as I had no
rifle with me, I rode up to him till I began to
think he was a strayed pet ; but when, as I got
within a few feet, he turned and dashed against a
tree, and then cannoned on to my horse and sent
him adrift, I guessed what was the matter, and
eventually caught him by standing still and allow-
ing him to mix himself up with my legs. His
eye 3 were scratched out, probably in a battle for
another ’roo’s gin.
* • * *
“Jinda”: Was once out after swan and
pelican on Narran Lake, above Brewarrina
(N.S.W.), when the boat capsized about a mile
from shore. After waiting till nearly dark we
started to swim in, but after going a quarter-mile,
one of us was dead to the world and we other two
had to help him along. After another 100yds.,
however, we had to let him slip from our grasp.
He gave a despairing cry and sank—up to his
shoulders ! Our language was picturesque as we
started to walk back to the boat ! Big as the
lake is, and heavy as its great area makes its
waves, it is extremely shallow everywhere except
in an occasional gutter in its bed.
“ Wi Piko I was em-
ployed on a N.Q. station a few
years ago, owned by two
brothers, the elder of whom,
Walter, went to Sydney and
got married. On his return,
late in the evening, he arranged
to have all hands mustered
next morning to be presented
to the bride, and as among
others there were about 20
blacks, clad chiefly in the “ al-
together,” he ordered these to
be each supplied with a pair of
pants. But Bob, the younger
brother, had notions of his
own; and, being also store-
keeper, was able to carry them
out. Next morning Mrs.
Walter took her seat on the
verandah, and when we whites
had been duly presented the
blacks were called for. Bob
fave the signal, and round the
ouse they came with a rush
and drew up in line in front of
the verandah—each man fully
clothed in one white cotton
sock ! The lady fled the scene,
hubby swore horribly, but the
imperturbable Bob explained—
“lo pair socks 55.; 20 pair
pants <£lo. She’s got to get
used to it, anyhow; so the
sooner the better ! ”

Bush Mormonism ! Not
long ago, a laborer, journeying
southward from Glen Innes
(N.S.W.), astonisheda Guyra
resident with a request for “ a
camp for me and the mis-
suses.” The traveller’s harem,
however, turned out to be
limited to only two “ better
halves.” Accommodation
being limited, the itinerant
band of domestic bliss was
obliged to “ move on.”
LONG AND SHORT.—A FREQUENT LONDON SIGHT.
“S.S.”: Gippsland packers, Omeo dis-
trict, who bullock-bell their horses, say a bell can
be heard locally over a distance of five miles in
frosty weather, and that a whip-crack will travel
further in the hills. The best-sounding bells
known in this district, are made by the packers
and bullockies out of old saw-blades—crosscuts or
circulars. Beasts fitted with these bells, running
loose on Sunday, make the ranges musical for
miles, and- give the most ungodly diggers that re-
ligious feeling.
* * * *
“K.M.L.”: Ever happen up against a
member of the “New Church sort of cross be-
tween Col. Olcott, Confucius, and Swedenborg,
as explained out-back to me by a follower who
turned the local letter-mill and had whips of time
to spare. Just before Bill and I had cut out the
little job we were at, Bill got a bad attack of Sal-
vation Army, and was rent asunder between fear
of hell-fire and desire to “blew the bit of paper.”
The postmaster seized us as likely - looking
strangers, and started to inject New Church into
us. He had the bump of language, and presently
Bill’s face began to brighten. “ What! ”he broke
out, joyfully. “ Ain’t there no hell ? No devil ?
Ain’t nobody to be damned ? Come on ! —let’s
make a night of it 1 ” And we made three nights
and two days I
• * • *
Writer while sojourning in W.Q. often
used to wonder why cattle didn’t have a 10ft.
tail to keep off flies both front and rear. Was,
therefore, highly interested to read that a bullock
at Vindex (N.Q.) had grown a tail 18in. long out
of the top of his shoulder. Just see what a
bullock can do when he tries !
* * * *
“S.S.S.” : Once saw a wonderful mirage.
Our horses, although hobbled, had been away for
a couple of days, and on the third morning whilst
out looking for them I saw them clearly depicted
in the sky, close to what looked like an old sheep-
yard. The peculiar action of a hobbled horse was
plainly recognisable. On enquiry found that the
nearest sheep-yards were 18 miles away. Went
there next day and found the horses quietly
feeding.

nana,” &c., all very earnestly, with a strong

Paddy B. kept a backblox shanty, and,
although a “ marksman,” gave tick and kept his
accounts with accuracy by the following method :
Each customer was represented by a potato, duly
marked, to distinguish the individual, and for
each tick-drink Paddy cut a small nick on the
spud. These murphies were kept under Paddy’s
bed ; but the secret was let out by the pet pig,
“Dinny.” One day, when a debtor called to
square up, “ Hould on a minit,’’ said Paddy,
going to the bedroom, “an’ I’ll tell yez.” Pre-
sently came a sound of fumbling and cursing, and
then a yell from Paddy for a light. “Saints
alive, man ! ” Biddy asked, as she brought a
candle, “ what in the divil’s the matter wid ye ? ”
“Hell to me sowl! ” roared Paddy, “if that
baste of a Dinny isn’t afther atin’ me ledger!"

The recent
escape from
Cobar (N.S.
Wales) gaol
was the means of getting many a poor

shanty or hut, and to pose with a beautiful trust-

* * • •
At Dandenong (Vic.), the other day, an
eagle swooped down upon a cattle-dog and bore
the astounded animal a short distance towards
Heaven. Then Tobias, pulling himself together,
grabbed the bird by the bony part of the wing, and
they both fell to the ground. A heroic small boy
killed the eagle with a big stick, after which the
local policeman took charge of the feathered
corpse and made a funeral procession to Dande-
nong Advertiser office, where the editor measured
the eagle (6ft. 6in. across the wings) and weighed
the dog (251 b. 20z.). The dimensions of the boy
and the policeman are not recorded. Said to be
the first authenticated dog-and-eagle struggle in
Australian history.

We.stralia is the only province which pos-
sesses an aboriginal woman as post-and-telegraph
mistress. She is a pupil of the local Spanish
Benedictine monks, and gives entire satisfaction.

The Sydney Rabbi.
The chief Sydney Rabbi, the Rev. A. B. Davis,
is an able seaman as preachers go. At the age of
70, he is a hearty man with a voice that never
tires. This voice breathing o’er Israel has builded
a Synagogue worth 1130,000.
At the age of 25 the Rabbi suffered chronically
from sore throat. He consulted many physicians
without being healed. At last an 80-year-old doctor
of the ancient school, a doctor in periwig and top-
boots, spoke the fateful words, “Young man,
grow a beard—and smoke.” The Rabbi obeyed.
In the morning haze, in the evening chill, in fog,
in rain, in wintry wind—the
Rabbi smoked. And he has not
had sore throat for 45 years.
He got a touch of earthquake
when in Jamaica, and at once
sent in his resignation. Earth-
quakes upset him ; they made
him “ low and dispirited.” He
returned to London, his birth-
place, and then came on to Syd-
ney. Here, 36 years ago, he
found the Jews huddled toge-
ther “like one large family,
mostly in York-street and Wyn-
yard Square, with the Syna-
gogue in the midst. Every-
body knew everybody, and
everybody visited everybody.
I gave offence by comparing the
Synagogue to an Egyptian
mummy-house. Ours deemed
it one. of the ornaments of Syd-
ney. In those days, William-
street was ‘ the Bush,’ and my
sons used to gather five-corners
there ; there was no Surry Hills
—it was sandhills.”
On the 16th inst., one day
after his 70th birthday, the
Rabbi sat in his library, be-
tween two magnificent birth-
day cakes, and displayed his
presents. A pile of congratu-
latory letters and telegrams;
handsome pipes, in case; a
“God bless you,” from ttie
Rev. Geo. Walters, Unitarian
Rabbi ; an address ; a sanctifi-
cation cap ; and—a cheque.
Only he didn’t show the
cheque.
He said: “Our people on
earth number B,ooo,ooo—more
than they have ever done, even
in the days of David or Solo-
mon. There is the Zionist
movement, on the Continent,
to get theJ ws back to Pales-
tine, but they will not go back until the Mes-
siah comes, which may be to-day, to-morrow,
thousands upon thousands of years hence. it
would be a most difficult matter to get our people
out of England. When I was in London, they
numbered 30,000 in that city, now they number
110,000, and their strength and influence, and posi-
tion—as bankers, financiers, and commercial men
generally—are something to marvel at. I here is
very little caste among our people, but the better
class will not allow a money-lender in their
houses; and the 5000 Jewish population of Sydney
is averse to money-lenders. Our people, who
were originally an agricultural people, became
money-lenders in the 14th or 15th century, merely
because all other employment was shut against
them.
‘‘ We never seek to make proselytes. We never
accept proselytes until they knock at the door
again and again for some three or four years.
This is because we regard a sincere revolution in
ideas to be the exception. We hold that the ideas
which an individual has learned to lisp at his
mother’s knee will be fixtures in his mind
through life. This attitude of ours towards prose-
lytes is one chief reason why we are averse to
marriage with other religions. # Of course if a
young fellow takes a fancy to a girl, nothing will
stop him marrying her. But we know that
natures trained in differing ideas can never live in
harmony. A liberal-minded man like Rosebery
marries Miss Rothschild on the condition that
the sons follow the father’s religion and the girls
the mother’s. Concerning proselytes, I am forci-
bly reminded of Napoleon going to decorate a
soldier with the Legion of Honor. Napoleon
learned that the soldier was a convert from •Juda-
ism to Christianity. He thereupon refused the
honor with the words, 4 A man who would tor-
sake his God would forsake his Emperor.
“The vitality of our people, like the virtue of
our women, is a subject which has been much
considered, and for which one can take every
credit. Wars upon wars have been waged upon
us; but, while other nations perish in such wars,
we remain more numerous than ever. I da.re.say
that one of the causes of our vitality is the inten-
sity of our past struggles for existence, but the
chief cause is our dietary system, and the next
the happiness of our homes.
“ All the meat we eat is examined by the Shou-
chat (the killer): one of our own, a competent in
detection of disease. He critically examines each
carcase throughout—takes the lungs, for instance,
blows them out, and searches them, ohould he
find a symptom of disease, he condemns the car-
case. Tuberculosis is much more prevalent than
is generally supposed, and our care in meat-
examination greatly explains our vitality and
longevity. Our meat, too, being drained of
the blood, is more healthful. The Jewish
housewife also is an excellent cook ; no matter
how rich her father may be, she has been
trained in the business of the kitchen. She is also
the best of wives in other respects ; her husband
can always rest assured that everything is as right
in his home as it should be. The happiness of
their domestic surroundings causes our people to
stay at home ; they don’t go to clubs and so on.
If they go out at night they take their wives with
them. The success, of our men in business is very
much due to their good home-life. People of other
religions have often spoken to me about the per-
petual industry of our men, and wondered how
they could sustain it. I have told them that the
reason is that our men are cared for in a good,
comfortable home by a good, comfortable w 1 fe.
Zangwill, among others, has glowingly set forth
the happiness of the Jewish home.’
S. A. Mills.

As illustrating the vagaries of an Australian
sub-tropical climate : Twice within the last two
months small flakes of snow have been noticed
falling in the city of Brisbane. The last occurrence
took place during the recent heavy snow-storm in
Toowoomba. Abundance of evidence is forth-
coming on this matter, and it might be added that
17 years ago snow fell, in very minute quantities,
in Brisbane Botanical Gardens.
“ Timi ” on human hybrids
Dear Bulletin.— lie supposed degeneration from
hybridisation, it is noteworthy that the Norfolk Islanders,
though four or five generations removed from the Original
“ cross,” show no sign of physical deterioration. One
would not look hopefully for a high mental standard in
the progeny of ignorant sailors of the last century, such
progeny cut off for generations from civilisation ; and
yet some of these hybrids have shown themselves capable
of considerable culture ; for instance, Macy Quintal, an
Auckland solicitor, was a descendant of a Bounty man.
Perhaps some M.L. Bulletin reader may be abl,e to
supply data regarding t he Maori quadroons and octoroons.
If this mixed-race-degeneracy really exists may it not
more likely be due to climatic than to racial influences
be the inheritance, that is, of the hybrid descendants of
nations whose race-cradles are not climatically similar t
It may be that the admixture of dissimilar races coming
from similar climatic zones—even though separated by
the equator—does not result in degenerate offspring.
The Tartar-Russian mixture, for instance, can hardly be
considered an example of degeneracy. Note the degene-
racy of the purest white races when transplanted to tne
tropics ! I understand that the India-born English grand-
child of India-born English grand parents does not exist.
If this be so, surely the complete loss of propagatory
power is the extreme symptom of degeneracy, and
even the timorous and inert Eurasian is higher m tne
physiological gamut than the non-procreative India-born
Englishman.
“A London blunder was obliterated this
week,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent, “ and the distance between the ‘ Bank and
Waterloo Station reduced from 30 to three minutes
by the new electric underground railway. Sixty-
five feet beneath the pavement and passing under
the Thames, the two tunnels—' up ’ and ‘ down
have taken four years to complete for running
purposes. One shaft only was sunk, and that m
the river, and borings made from there to each
terminus. There are no ‘ classes ’; one fare for all,
either single or return, no intermediate station m
the total mile and half, and for the first time in
England American carriages have been mtro-
duced. Each car has an aisle throughout, both
cross and side seats, holds 54 comfortably, and
seven oars make up a full tram, having
a motor at each end to save reversing. lhe
motor picks the current up with a metallic
shoe from a centre rail of channel iron insulated
by porcelain cups, and the circuit is completed by
the two ordinary rails with copper strips under
the fish plates. 'The fare is expected to be 2d.
or 3d. return. “ Several other electno under-
"Tounds,” continues our correspondent, are m
progress, and the District Co. has become so
alarmed at the loss threatened their suffocating
brick sewers that they intend building an electric
line underneath the present tunnel from Earl-
s to Mansion House, with a break at Charing
Cross only.”
TO V. J. DALEY.
You sing in fluting verse more sweet
Than tender evensong of birds,
And sigh o’er melodies too fleet
And thoughts too subtle strange for words.
Ay, carve in gold with all the strong,
Fair daintiness and pride of art;
But better have you done with song
Deep carven in the human heart.
E.D.
Peggy Pryde.
Ten o’clock at Sydney Tivoli, and the audience is
wearying. People slouch in their seats with
dulled eyes and jaded brains, and have only
languid smiles for the actors who a little ago
won boisterous laughter. The pit exhales strange
odours to the dress-circle ; the dress-circle adds its
own aroma and passes to the stifling gallery.
There is a noticeable sense of expectation, winch
heightens to eagerness as a turn ends and the
stage is empty. Backs straighten ; eyes brighten,
programmes Ire lifted ; the orchestra mutters pre-
liminary bars . . . and there hurries to the foot
lights a girl—she looks only a girl—suoh as M 1 ■,
End road on Saturday night sees by
plump, coquettish, audacious, with plain jacket
and skirt, and a feather in her bat. which chal
lenges and invites. She nods to the audience
and instantly the whole theatre seems alive with
her—she is so recklessly, magnificently vital.
Never still an instant, she advances, retires, bends
with hands on hips to vent a low, rich
chuckle from a gurgling diaphragm- a real
Oarrollian chortle, talks with the ease of a
bon camarade, the aplomb of a stage vet-
eran. She sings in husky contralto of hus
band who digs in the backyard for K 1 on dyke
gold ; but her song is nothing her voice is httle,
it is herself that fascinates and nnpresses-less a
girl, a woman, than a force, an impetus, a jo ly
good fellow who flings herself at you and over-
powers you into admiration—the restless embodi-
ment of splendid animal energy. Bravo, Leggy
Pryde ! ” „ , ...

“ minute. I have been twenty-one
years on the stage. No, it’s very good of
you, but I’m sure I look it; I began when 1
was only eight years old at Hull. I’ve been
all through England and America, and in
Africa. Do I like Australian audiences?
Well, I onlv know’Sydney; but they’re
good. It was a little hard at first. They
gave me a great welcome, clapped hands
and all that, and I thought I was going
to have a soft thing. Then they settled
back quiet, just as if they were saying,
‘ Well, we’ve heard a lot about you, and we’ve
given you a good clap to encourage you.
Now let us see what you can do.’ Then, of
course, I had to go in and win, and I did.
I’ve been here fifteen weeks now. You know
eight weeks is considered a good Tivoli
season. The Tivs. are very impatient. I
think I’ve trained them a bit, though.
You know we can’t change all in a moment;
and at first when I used to come off breath-
less, and have my dresser throwing things
on me as fast as she could, I could hear
them saying in front, ‘Oh, hur-ree up!
Oh, hur-ree up!’ So one night I went out
and talked to them. ‘ Just keep cool,’ I
said. ‘l’m going as fast as ever I can. If
you think you can go faster, just you come
and do the part yourself.’ Since then
they’ve been a little more considerate. I
must say they don’t want telling twice. See
you again ”
She is a woman’s-rights advocate in

it to your sorrow.’’ Poor stuff in print ;

ceases to gape when it roars. Ihe
audience understands perfectly : it
has not come to hear Peggy Pryde’s

oome to see and hear Peggy Pryde.*

were like him. What is my favorite part ?
I have so many. But I think I like ‘ Sally’s
Wedding ’as well as any. No, not exactly coster ; but just

course you keep it on. That was where my mother shone
bo She was never at a loss. It did not matter what the
part was. She could always invent some little gag that
came natural to it and pleased the audience. Would I
like to try another class of parts? Well, not tragedy.
Not Lady Macbeth, you know. But I think I could play
comedy. I played Ruth in ‘The Gay Parisienne,' at
a London theatre, and didn’t get on so badly.
Somehow, I think when I get back to London 111
have another try at comedy. No, I don’t see the
audience as a rule : I sing to a gas-jet. Sometimes when
I see a man laughing very much I single his face out and
sing to him—it does one so much good, a jolly face 1 But
a cold, fishy face—oh, how that does depress you ! It
seems to take all the go out of you. No, I never feel

A dramatic monologue—“ The lodging-house
slavey.” The quaint, dumpy little figure takes
possession of the stage in a rush and a stride. It
is Dickens’s “Willing Sophy ” come to life. Her
arms are elbow-bare : little ringlets fall beneath
her little cap. Her black bodice contrasts with the
cheap, light skirt ; the coarse canvas apron is
tucked round her waist. _ In her hand a broom
which Bhe brandishes : with which she appeals,
menaces, implores. There is music for a line or
two: the rest is spoken —emitted in little ex-
plosive spasms like the bombs of a firework flower-
pot. “ Oh, the missus ! Such a trouble as I ave
with ’er! And Gawd knows I’m as faithful as the
dawg, lam ! ” Her arm dabs her eyes : her voice
fades on a pathetic cadence. “ And the lodgers !
Oh, they are a mouldy lot ! Such a s’picious
lot! And such airs! There’s that Italian
dook! ’E buys a scrag-end of mutton, and
’as a chop cooked off it every day! And
by the time you get to the twelfth chop ”
—she is holding her nose with all her
body—“oh, lor! it does hum! Why, I was
takin’ one of them chops up the other day, and
holding a regular conversation with it a-goin’ up
the stairs. Then there’s the new-married couple
in the front parlor —you should just see them, you
should! ‘Will my little lovey-dovey ’ave a
hegg?’ ‘Yes, if my darling little tootsy-wootsy
will’a ve’arf ! ’ —you know the style. W’y, even
our old tom ’ad to get up and walk out, and ’e
ain’t no chicken l—’e knows a thing or two ! ”
And so on with the good gray jokes beloved
of the people, punctuated by bawling laughter
and applause. And the quaint little woman
never halts or hesitates, flings out spasmodic sen-
tence after sentence, uses voice, limbs, features,
with a vehement emphasis that silhouettes her
figure on the memory —the small round clamor-
ous figure in the tucked apron, with the magnetic
broom, and the round earnest face thrust forward,
tossing perpetually in a fever of ferocious gesticu-
lation.
On receiving news from N.S.W. of Justice
Manning’s death, Otago Daily Times (M.L.)
fished out an obituary notice, to the effect that
“Sir W. Manning, LL.D., M.L.C.” (nearly four
years dead !) “was born in 1811,” &c. Still, the
mistake does not beat that of the great Austra-
lian dailies who killed Academician John Gilbert
twice over.
Lipton, the millionaire, friend to the
London poor—he has given <£700,000 to found a
cheap-dinner system —is an Irishman. He went
to America when 14, returned to Glasgow at 19,
and started in a small way in the butter-and -cheese
line. It is to the Scotch city he owes the be-
ginning of his fortune, but he has not yet con-
sidered its poor. It is London that is the royal
road to a peerage.

“ 8.,” on the butchered passenger :
The slaughter-bill of the Victorian railways is probably
one of the reasons why they don’t pay. Since they first
started the iron horse in Victoria, it has laid out 94/
persons and more-or-less smashed 5840. This must have
affected the traffic considerably, not only by withdrawing
the dead and damaged from circulation, out also by
making lots of other people conclude that it wasn t good
enough to take the risk. . TO w
A.n interesting theory, but it won t pass. JN.». W.
and Maoriland railways kill or maim a larger pro-
portion of passengers than the Vic. lines, and they
pay better—perhaps because they get the freight
on the corpses, or perhaps because, when people
hear of a big smash, they at once send their
mothers-in-law on long railway-journeys. The
amazing feature of the . record, however
(“Wealth and Progress of N.S.W., page 567) is
that S.A. seems to kill four times larger a propor-
tion of passengers than either Vic. or N.S.W.,
while it injures only a quarter as many as Victoria
and not much over a fifth as many as N.S.W.
Do the S.A. railway officials go round after an
accident and knock the wounded on the head to
prevent them giving evidence—or what ?

The grand-uncle of President M‘Kinley
(U.S.), a North of Ireland Presbyterian farmer,
was hanged at Coleraine for high treason in 1797.
The police have contrived to import a good
deal of the mock-tragedy element into the pursuit
and capture of Peisley, who, at Cabramatta
(Sydney) recently, wounded a policeman with his
own pistol. And since, as that shooting was as
likely as not accidental, Peisley’s only claim to be
regarded as a desperado is that while running
away with great skill he discharged two shots
with very little—while his pursuers were them-
selves distributing much casual lead over the sur-
rounding scenery—it looked somewhat puerile to
bring the famished and broken-down captive be-
fore the beak—gripped hard on either side by a
constable big enough to eat him. No trade with-
out its little tricks.

A certain very bald candidate, rejected at
a recent general election in favor of a wild gintle-
man from the ould sod, was told by a sympathising
friend that the “ absence of hair from his head
had prejudiced him.” “ No,"replied the defeated
one, “ it was the absence of hair from my teeth.”

“ R.A.D.” : Be use of photography in de-
tecting forgery (Bulletin, 30/7/98). I think the
practice originated with the Parnell Forgery Com-
mission, as follows: The Judges, on taking their
seats one morning, were surprised to see a large
sheet occupying a portion of the gallery, while an
operator with two magic-lanterns sat in the body
of the Court. When Parnell was called and asked
if a letter, handed to him, was in his writing, he
replied in the affirmative, whereupon the letter
was handed to the operator, who placed it in one
of the lanterns ; and another letter, which Parnell
had previously sworn to as a forgery, was placed
in the other lantern. The court was then dark-
ened, and when the two reflections, very much en-
larged, were thrown on the sheet, it was at once
evident that in the forged document each letter
was distinct by itself, whilst in the genuine one
the letters ran into one another. This was the
beginning of the break-down of the Times case,
which ended in Pigott’s confession and suicide.
The idea was said to be due to Tim Healey, who
hadn’t then turned dog on Parnell.
“Hermes” sends The Bulletin this queer
item :—
Consider the tops and how they spin. A scientific
friend recently broke me all up with the wonders of
rotary motion. Set spinning a number of tops (made of
cut-down cotton-reels and little cylinders of tinfoil pointed
at one end), which, when struck quite a heavy blow,
simply leant over, gracefully described a wider circle, and
recovered their upright position, in joyful defiance of
gravity. When they collided they hurled each other all
over the room with wonderful force. Then another set,
bristling with needle-points round their equators, were
sent spinning against a sheet of paper hanging loosely.
They tore it to pieces, and also tore a sheet stretched
across their track. A large circular sheet of paper was
fastened to a small block on a turning-lathe and made to
revolve at a high rate, and when the armed tops were
sent against it, it hurled them away without receiving a
scratch. I then struck the revolving paper with a knife,
but though it threw off the blow and resounded like a
drum, it never bent. A steel watchchain was then tied
round another block which was made to revolve at the
same rate as the paper. The chain flew off, rolled across
the table rigid, like a wheel, and dented a piece of tin.
But the same chain, hurled by the same force against the
revolving paper, made no impression whatever. Said my
friend : “ If those Spaniards had fought behind rapidly
revolving disc-shields not a Yankee shot would have got
home. Next year I hope to have perfected a method —
with a ring of poisonous gas—of turning any hostile ship
into a mortuary long before she could get within effective
range, and without any flash and roar to betray my
position. The gang that tackles me will have to sail in
a hermetically-sealed ship, use compressed air, and run
15 miles out to sea every time they want to load up ! ”

POLITICAL
POINTS
For sale or to-let—the Q. Opposition.
It seems possible that events will compel Q.
( TOvt. to organise a service of State mail-steamers
tor its own North. State Socialism !
Maoriland is still on the move. In
Dunedin lately, for the first time in the history of
«he world, a man was fined for employing a non-
unionist.
Q. Government’s Parliamentary strength
is made up of five items—expenditure of public
money, billet patronage, dissolution threats, elec-
sorate promises, members “ on the make.”
“The Russian press, ” says the cable, “has
reoeived the British naval scheme with remarkable
-quammity.” Why “remarkable”? Russia’s
navy is a luxury—England’s is a life-and-death
necessity.
“The children of the white settlers are
being continually followed by Hindoos and Japs.,
who make indecent overtures to them.”—Mary-
borough (Q.) Patriot. Leperland wallows in
the mire it loves.
The great Westralian financial drunk—and
afterwards ! The W.A. civil -servants are now
being officially slaughtered like rabbits ; 400 are
to be discharged within the next few months, 200
from the Works Dept, alone.
Mr. Alfred Allen to be a member of
N.S.W. Upper House ! What is evidently needed
is a third House, composed of the played-out and
ejected pink-eyed rabbits and Italian greyhounds
>f rough-and-tumble politics.
r Sixty-one members have retired from
N r .S.W. Legislative Council during the past 12
vears—49 by death and 12 by resignation. Can
Held s proposed rotary system improve on these
natural methods of clearance ?
A N.S.W. country member, first time in,
posted 16 letters, one day last week, all encased in
large blue Parliamentary envelopes : Supporters
receiving a note of thanks on Govt.-supplied note-
paper and enclosed in Govt, envelopes.
Both Houses of S.A. Parliament pay their
members .£2OO a year. And there is much
grumbling in both. The sum is too much for
drinks and too little for screw ; and Labor Ms.P.
begin to desperately say that something must be
done.
The Bulletin doesn’t think much of Mr.
Joe Chamberlain in connection with the great
Papuan land-steal. The reasons why will be very
apparent when full details as to the credentials
given by Mr. Joe to Somers Vine become public
property.
The peaceful re-election of J. P. Abbott
“ jhe N.S.W. Speakership was chronicled by
Melb. Erald as a rather astonishing event. In
Victoria it is customary to regard the Speaker as
a regrettable circumstance who ought to be changed
when the opportunity offers.
Another stone on the cairn for the Pacific
eable. Let us insist upon this nerve of communi-
cation with America. Of course the ’Frisco-
Hawaii cable goes without saying. Then only
the hitch-on to Maoriland. The ocean bed was
satisfactorily surveyed 25 years ago.
The labor bosses in every Q. constituency
should use the machinery of a labor plebiscite
elect candidates, then select the candidate and
barrack and vote for that one as one man, before
and at the next Q. parliamentary elections. You
must fight machinery with machinery.
Jenkinson, the Q. Labor-member who
annexed the Wide Bay seat vacated by Agent-
General Tozer, believes that newspapers should be
carried free through the post. The writer thinks
shat newspaper-writers—and rum'—should Vie
carried free. Isn’t it enough, however, to curry
she Jenkinsons free ?
When Black Rod, lme a Jack-out-of-the-
box, completed the last trick of his fantastic
capers in N.S.W. Parliament last week, one of
t he oldest members whispered to his neighbor •
“There goes the last Black Rod. This Parlia-
ment will extinguish him! ” If common-sense
rule, the words should be prophetic.
Another English syndicat nibbling at
British Papua—this time a syndikitten. Liver-
pool promoters, capital AlO,OOO ; local boss, one
M'Kenzie, said to have been connected with
Papua since ’9l, and to be on his way to Samarai,
nailed with “.Cingalese experts ” in tropical cul-
ture. What color is Australia to be ?
W. H. Groom, M.L.A., completed the
•'6th year of his continuous parliamentary life in
Q. Assembly on 11th Aug. Some say that the
Speakership trap is being again baited for him ;
others that the Home Secretary’s portfolio is being
dropped in the track that he is likely to take.
Groom seems to be growing younger.
When the final return of the cost of the
M'Sharry (railway - contract - arbitration) case
reaches N.S.W. Parliament, it will be about the

lative veto to a House drawn from a small class in
a small community is being again illustrated in

Vic. Insurance Bill is receiving from the teeth of

S.A.’s returned Agent-General Playford is
credited with being indifferent about re-entering
polities, but it is believed the Govt, will persuade
him to contest Onkaparinga next April against
poet Caldwell, secretary to the Opposition. The
constituency, which contains a big producing
population, would vote solid for Playford, who
has always belonged to that class, and still runs a
potato-patch.
A brighter season in the N.S.W. back-
country shows up the danger of attaching resumed
areas to leaseholds under Carruthers’s ’95 Act.
The alert squatter plugged in his application for
attachment in the dry years, when nobody wanted
to select, and there was consequently no opposi-
tion, and now, when, with better prospects, men
would select, they find much good country shut up
against them.
Ed. Bulletin.— He assertion that Irish
Catholics always place the religious question first.
Candidates for Burrowa (N.S.W.) electorate:
Mackay, Scotch descent, Presbyterian; and
Toomey, old Irish family. Catholic voting strength
to Protestant, 7 to 2. Result : majority for
Mackay, 308. This, in the most Hibernian elec-
torate in N.S.W., and in the month of the Yellow
Pup’s loudest yelping.—Liberal.
While Lyne, Barton, Reid, O’Connor,
Wise, Bruce Smith and others are pilloried for
alleged discrepancies between their comments on
the original Convention Bill, leading up to the
Referendum, and their comments now, one man,
d. H. Carruthers, escapes with hardly a bad word.
Yet Carruthers was a glaring apostate. When
licked at the Referendum he turned right round
and sat down with Reid —and is there still.
According to Premier Reid, ex-Minis-
ters Gould, Garrard, and Smith did not
take part in commuting that death-sentence
after ceasing to hold seats in Parliament—
they merely confirmed or announced a previous
Ministerial decision. All the same, they had
no right to interfere even to that extent with
Executive business. When they were thrown
right out they should have stayed right out—not
on the mat or just inside the door.
The English news printing-paper makers
have been clean whipped out of the Australian
market; some of them are withdrawing their local
representatives. All the big Australian journals’
contracts have gone to Protectionist America.
Even Sydney D.T. itself is printed on Yankee
paper. Protection makes things dear, and that’s
why the Freetrade-mad Sydney D.T. uses Ameri-
can paper. The Bulletin is now about the only
well-circulated Australian paper printed on Bull-
made material.
VVeak as is esprit de corps in N.S.W. Civil
Service, the Public Service Board’s “marks”
project galvanised a little concerted action into
the service rank and file. A rumored block anti-
Ministerial vote scared the Board into withdraw-
ing, for the time at any rate, the certificate project
which so excited the wrath of their usually weak-
spirited minions ; it is a fact that the departmental
heads, who were under instructions to furnish
their returns by Aug. 1, were told to temporarily
hold them over.

“T.P.” : From intermarriage of Chinese
and Australians what improvement can be ex-
pected? Both races .suffer terribly from. V that
tired feeling.’’ Australia is too tired to care to
become a nation, and China is too tired to care to

Moore, successor to Nevermind Nelson as
M.P. for Murilla (Q.), has moved to have the
numbers eliminated from electioneering ballot-
papers. Good. The numbers are not necessary
in order to detect impersonation, because each
returning or presiding officer is required by law to
mark off the name of every voter to whom a ballot-
paper is supplied ; but the numbers do enable in-
formation to be made available nefariously, after
the election, as to how any voter voted. Old
Nevermind is finding out the fidelity of political
proteges. First, his best whiteheaded boy,
Byrnes, countermarches in his policy, and now
the successor to his pocket electorate essays to do
a democratic deed.
“ The man who confers a favor makes one
friend and a thousand enemies.” Premier Reid
dares not fill the portfolios vacant in his Ministry
until lie shall have got his professed followers to
back up the Federal resolutions he intends sub-
mitting to the Assembly. There are at least 10
Ministers-expectant, and if the Premier lowered
the tantalising carrots into any three of the water-
ing mouths, about seven Reidites would at once
see incurable defects in whatever the man with
the eyeglass chose to propose with respect to
Federation.
Some Clarence River sugar-planters have
the impudence to say that black-labor is essential
to the N.S.W. sugar-industry. In that case, the
sooner the N.S.W. sugar-industry dies the better:
the land can be put to better use than in growing
Hindoos. A recent letter from the Clarence to
The Bulletin says : “ The country round here is
simply black with Hindoos ; and on the Richmond
it is worse. The very worst foe of the white
laborer is the laborer jumped-up to farmer. For
scrooging and driving no ‘fat’ capitalist can
oompare with him.” And it looks like it.
Tas. Assembly has just passed the “Natal
Act ” for the exclusion of aliens. During the
debate members complained that even a Britisher
might be excluded on the educational test (50
words to be written in some European language),
but Braddon silenced the objectors by explaining
that the clause referred to was to be construed
with a diplomatic wink. Chamberlain’s “states-
manship ” was then loudly praised by Parliament
and the press. Possible Asiatic cavillers at the
double-dealing of the highly-moral Bull are, of
course, expected to be effectually outwitted by
these “ diplomatic ” proceedings !
Brisbane (Q.) Observer flaunts an editorial
in the attempt to show that 100 Japs were recently
landed at Port Douglas (N.Q.), for the Mosinan
Central Sugar-Mill— there being already available
in that district 500 Chinamen and 300 kanakas !
because “ labor is lacking.'’ Labor is not lacking.
Thousands of white men, looking for work, infest
the towns, the populous centres, and the droughty
tracks of Queensland. Wages are lacking, in
spite of the fact that Govt, has granted to sugar-
mills loans, many of which can never, and will
never, be repaid. The Observer is the Courier’s
bantling. The Courier is Premier Byrnes’s politi-
cal guardian and trustee. What chance, then, for
rotting Leperland ?
“Fiat Justitia” re the Bill to simplify
Supreme Court practice introduced by N.S.W.
Attorney-General :
The Bill contains many clerical errors, caused chiefly
through a too-close copy of enactments in foroe in Eng-
land and the other Australias. It attempts to amalga-
mate law and equity procedure, as has been done in Eng-
land under the Judicatuie Act, but retains, with slight
alterations, the present system of pleading, which has
been in force here since the fifties, and was abolished in
England some 25 years ago. Vagueness is undesirable in
an Act of Parliament. What, however, can be more
vague than section 21 : “ From and after the commence-
ment of this Act all enactments inconsistent therewith
shall be repealed.” This will be a nice harvest for the
lawyers, and the already over-burdened Supreme Court
will have tough work in deciding what Acts are repealed.
Truly a new way to simplify the law.
Crick, M.P., remarks that the remission
of the death-sentence in the Cootamundra rape
case means the abolition of capital punishment for
that class of offence in N.S.W. The law is quite
at variance with public opinion— i.e., cool and
deliberate public opinion ; but it is apt to be
carried into effect when two or three penny news-
papers raise a scare, and start the public yelling
for blood. The Reid Govt, has never yet hanged
a man whose life it could possibly spare ; but there
have been Ministries in N.S.W. which never
seemed to spare a man whom they could possibly
hang. There is no reason—except the taint of the
old convict leaven in the law—why N.S.W. should
have seven or eight times as many capital offences
as (say) England.
Give even the Boodleland Nevermind
Government its due. In the matter of the Agri-
cultural Lands Re-purchase Act, no matter
though pecuniary los-es may have been made by
the State, and special advantages reaped by squat-
ters, or by their mortgagees, benefit has accrued
to the country as a whole. E.y., Rosewood estate
was re-bought, divided into 56 areas, and opened
to selectors. Forty-nine areas have been selected,
and there is not yet a fraction of arrears of rent.
Meaning that 50 settlers now populate an area
formerly held by one grazier.
“A NEW AND ORIGINAL SCARECROW
DUET."
S.A. contemplates building 263 miles of
railway to Innamincka on the Q. border, at a
cost of <£1,200,000. The line would give far S.W.
Queensland access to a port that is 250 nearer
than Brisbane and would save 2000 miles of sea-
freight on its wool as well. In other words, S.A.
would provide the railway and stand any loss,
and the S.W. Queenslander would be enriched by
cheaper freights, and more population would come
to the country because it was made accessible, and
the value of land would go up in consequence—in
fact, Queensland would profit all round. But
probably the mutton-headed Queensland provin-
cialist would simply call it an attempt to rob
“ us ” of “ our ” trade, and put an export duty on
wool to prevent its own people being enriched by
lower railway-rates, and other facilities.
Sydney Telegraph :
We may take it for granted that the Government will
submit no [Federal] amendments beyond those for which
they received the country’s endorsement at the elections.
For what amendments did the Govt, receive the
country’s endorsement at the elections ? The
Federal issue was complicated with a hundred
other issues, and over all the local roads-and-
bridges question was paramount. When “ the
country ” had to vote on one straight-out issue, it
gave a majority for the Bill —and no amendments.
And, in any case, the Telegraph’s own figures of
votes polled at the elections triumphantly show
that the Reid majority in the Assembly—if there
is a majority—represents a minority of voters ;
and the real majority, if any Federal deduction
can be drawn, is still on the side of the original
Bill.
Ryott-Maughan, Opposition M.P., could
get no member but Labor-leader Glassey to usher
and sponsor him at the book-kissing ceremony in
Q. Assembly, and referred to that fact in his
maiden speech. “Committees” Annear also
alluded to the incident, and the different treat-
ment accorded Glassey on his entrance as a par-
liamentary sizar was variously pictured. Then
Labor-man Andy Dawson (summarised): —
When Glassey first became a member of this House, he
was very badly treated. If he hung up his hat on a peg,
all the other members removed their hats. If he put his
umbrella in a stand, all the other umbrellas in that
stand were taken out of it. I believe the only reason
violent hands were not laid on him was—fear of the Law.
Whipper Hamilton declared that he had never
heard that yarn before. Several whippees veiled:
“ Pure fiction.”
Bale-of-blankets ” Brimker, member for
Mulbring, is the latest title of the N.S.W.
Colonial Secretary. The saintly old gentleman
just scraped back into Parliament by a majority
of 45. On an average all the large centres gave
his opponent just as many votes as himself ; but
Mulbring division (timber getters and bone-,
gatherers) came to the rescue with : Brunker, 59 ;
T’otherman, 10. If a bale of blankets had also
been distributed on behalf of the other man
among the Mulbring innocents, well the Colonial
Secretary would have to go cow-selling once
again. After all, where is the essential difference
between the blanket-distributing operations of
the Reid Govt, and a certain old-time N.S.W.
electioneering occurrence long ago related in The
Bulletin? Before the days of single constitu-
encies two candidates running together for an
electorate visited, in the course of their canvassing
tour, the mining corner of the district. When
they “paid their respects” at the first miner’s
cottage they came to and kissed the baby and
asked the miner’s wife to tell her husband to vote
for them, she plumply enquired what she was
going to profit by it ? “I haven't had a new dress
for two years,” she ingenuously remarked. “ But,
my good woman,” said the richer of the two can-
didates, a big storekeeper, “surely you don’t ex-
pect us to give you and all your neighbors new
dresses ! ” “Yes, indeed,” she replied, “if you
don’t do something for me I’ll take no interest in
you.” The pair got so many replies of the same
sort that they agreed that “something ” must be
done. Next day 230 pieces of dress-material went
to Flat, and at the election the benevolent
candidates were triumphantly returned.

Four Solomons—Emanuel Solomon, Judah
Moss Solomon, Saul Solomon and Vabien Louis
Solomon—have sat at different times in the S.A.
Parliament. And the reputation for wisdom
which used to attach to the name of Solomon
hasn’t been much improved by any of them.
James Crotty’s sister, re his mean be-
quest to his widow :
He knew her best, and, if he thought much money was
best, no doubt she would have had it. But, knowing she
was once a Protestant, he, as her truest friend, wished to
guard her from sinking back into that slough of heresy ;
hence the limitations of his will, for he, as I, or any
instructed Catholic, know that the gift of true faith is
worth more than a million worlds, and without faith it is
impossible to please God.
Orotty’s method of encouraging his widow not to
sink back into the slough was, at least, peculiar.
Some specially fine horrors are being
brought to light in the departmental investigation
into the affairs of Mt. Eliza Infirmary, the Perth
(W. A.) Poorhouse. Evidence has been given that
the inmates after washing the night-utensils used
them as wash-basins; that scores of men washed
their plates, knives, forks, &c., in one kerosene-
tin of water; that blankets on bed-ridden men
were left unchanged for long periods with most
hideous results. Also, that a paralysed patient
was repeatedly flogged with a wet towel—and
many minor horrors, amongst which the fact that
vermin reigned supreme at the institution was a
kind of trifle.
Grandfather Brunker v N.S.W. Colonial
Secretary,_ defending himself against the charge
that he is a stupid person who is of no
earthly use in politics, boasts that at his
instance, or because of his pleadings, £147,169 of
Govt, money has been spent in E. Maitland
electorate during 18 years. If E. Maitland was
entitled to the expenditure, it should have got it
without Brunker’s solicitations ; if uneutitled,
Brunker has been a party to cheating some other
constituency of its due—and boasts of it. The
road-and-bridges member is the curse of Parlia-
ment, and all Brunker can find to say in self-
defence is that in his time he has been a
phenomenally big curse.
Government and municipal authorities in
Perth (W.A.) object to the periodical publication
of the startling local tuberculosis horrors, and the
stock department now has instructions to keep
dark anything of the kind. The Perth municipal
authorities, after a particularly alarming discovery
of tuberculous cows in a local dairy recently,
actually persuaded the looal daily press to keep
the thing quiet on the plea that publication would
defeat the efforts of the municipal officers to dis-
cover more tuberculous cows. Nothing would
probably have been heard of the matter, but a
rascally Sunday paper published the thing, with
special comment on the action of the dailies,
which eventually had to print the belated news
and lamely explain their previous silence.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8., in N.S.W. only. To all the other colonies
1/8, 2/8, 3/8. Beware of imitations. Only address:
Senior, chemist, 246 George-street Sydney. *
“ Scientifically, cautiously and solidly is
Scotland Yard sparring for time, and plenty of it,
in the encounter v. Monson (the Ardlamont * not-
proven ’ man) and c 0.,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “ The issue is to be to
the death, or at least public obliteration, of the
dangerous conspirators who have plied their trade
even more openly than the blackbirders did in the
South Seas not many years ago. Trading in
reversionary interests has been brought to a fine
art in this wise: If Tom will come into £lO,OOO
on surviving his elder brother Bill, Tom has an in-
surable interest to the full extent of that amount.
Tom is profligate, prodigal and poverty-stricken
finally, so he sells his reversionary interest to an
enterprising syndicate for, say, £5OO. They,
having an insurable interest in his life for their
£lO,OOO, guarantee themselves by insuring him
fully. Unless such an insurable interest be shown
no company would or could accept the risk. The
policy once effected, the simple question bursts
into being, why pay premiums and wait for Tom
or even Bill’s death to occur at Nature’s own
date? Give poor old over-worked Nature a hand
and expedite Tom’s worldly departure, as he is a
dram or drug-drinker and easily getatable. So
one day Tom is found dead in his rooms—over-
dose of morphia—disappears one night during an
ocean voyage, or falls out of the Night Owls’ Club
top-storey window whilst very drunk, and a com-
plaisant coroner, to screen such a swell institution,
hushes the inquest, or, as has been done several
times in the past few years, the routine reporters
are bribed. Why work when a moment of mur-
der pays tens of thousands? It takes a big city
to birth, breed, and nurture such villainy, and if
Scotland Yard can envelop this covey of birds of
prey after whom it is straining every D., London
will gasp so that all the world will hear.”
The only thing the Scotch miss in Aus-
tralia—the Scotch mist.

E. E. Williams, arch-blasphemer of the
axiomatic truth, ’ hit the Cobden Club so hard
with his book, “ Made in Germany,” that Courte-
nay Boyle was commissioned by Government to
inquire whether the assertion that “ the industrial
glory of Britain is departing ” is really true,
f mauig the commission’s report and statistics a
‘complete verification” of his “Made in Ger-
many assertions, Williams is now out with an-
other book, “ Marching Backwards.” Some
figures: In ’95, as compared with ’93, the export
of manufactures from France increased £2,000,000 ;
Germany, increase, £11,000,000 ; U.S., increase*
£10,000,000; Great Britain, decrease, £19,000,000.
In the first nine months of ’95 Britain exported
£15,180,718 worth of woollen manufactures ; in
'96 it fell away to £14,736,170; in ’97 it dropped
to £13,141,501. On the other hand, the export of
raw wool to be made up in foreign factories goes
up fast, and the export of coal (the end of the
supply of which is within measureable distance)
increases by leaps of millions. The most recent
Board of Trade returns show that Britain bought
from the tI.S. £118,000,000, and sold to the U.S.
£17,000,000, during the year.

Education minister Butler, in S.A., in-
stitutes a “ parents’ visiting day ” —designed to
let more light in upon local pedagogic methods.
And in other respects the newly-elevated minister,
whose failure was confidently predicted, surprises
onlookers —and illustrates once more Kingston’s
superiority as a judge of men.
Labor journals are urging a boycott of all
matches the manufacture of which tends to pro-
duce that horrible necrosis of the lower jawbone,
known as “phossy jaw.” Matches which only
strike on the box and are made from “ red ”
phosphorus do not produce the disease, and are
accordingly advocated.
“ Dead wood Dick ” literature was respon-
sible, up Broken Hill way, the other day, for a
number of lads burgling a grocery and stealing
cigars, cigarettes, lollies and a tin-whistle. Seven
of the boys were taken before the court, though
17 were under apprehension at one time—the odd
10 were “receivers.” The ringleader had
“ turned dog ” on his followers and become a
myrmidon of the police. Several of the lads
who were fined have sworn by the awful symbol
of the Six -fingered Hand of Red to scalp the one
who “ peached ” —ha, ha !
Westralian Justice Hensman is a veritable
type of the slavishly-judicial Judge. He once
affected the utmost ignorance upon hearing a wit-
ness use the expression “ straighthair,” and would
not enter it upon his notes until it had been
clearly interpreted as meaning a Westralian
native. Such pretence on the part of an old sand-
groping lawyer and Judge was greeted in court
with a wink of the other eye, as the term
“ straighthair ” always touches a tender spot
amongst those Westralian families whose descent
from early ‘ ‘ settlers ” is notorious, and also
amongst those, such as that to which the learned
Judge belongs, whose Westralian residence, though
honorable, is old enough to gain for them the
application of the term. Which recalls that there
was no “gammon” of this sort in the recently-
deceased Mr. (N.S.W.) Justice Manning. Sitting
in Full Court on an appeal against a conviction
for attempted blackmail, a day or two before he
died, His Honor in reply to the argument that
there could be no blackmail when there was direct
mention of money, remarked : “Do you seriously
contend, Mr. , that the application for
money would have been any plainer had prisoner
written‘you’d better part the ooftish,’or ‘hand
over the stuff ? ’ ”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Frank Madden took the trouble to assure Vie.
Assembly last week that he is not “ the champion of the
bookmaker,” as some people assert. If Madden doesn’t
want to be mistaken for Ikey Mo’s barracker he had bet-
ter show himself an honest debater when he makes
speeches on Ikey’s behalf. He quotes the programme for
the latest meeting of the S.A.J.C. as evidence that
“ stakes generally have been decreased 15 per cent.,” and
further alleges, for the misinformation of the ignorant,
that stakes in S.A. have a tendency to grow smaller and
smaller every year. As a matter of fact, they have grown
bigger and bigger during the ten years of the tote’s exist-
ence. The recent—very recent—decline in the S.A.J.O.
receipts is attributable, no doubt, to the rapid advance of
the Adelaide Racing Club, which now gives thousands of
pounds more during the season than it gave in ’96. When
Madden was interrupted in his Ikey Mo-ish oration with a
request to tell the House what the A.R.C. had done, he
got out of the difficulty by saying that he had taken the
■‘principal club.”
He disingenuously instanced a temporary check
in the prosperity of one club, and ignored the rapid ad-
vance made by another. Madden, in this matter, is nob
the Champion of Truth, anyhow. As a student of racing
figures, he is palpably using his knowdedge to misguide
the public. The book which doesn’t lie knocks all the
stuffing out of Frank Madden’s argument, for it shows
that a lot of entirely new racing stakes, up to the value
of £3OO and £5OO apiece, have been established by the
A.R.O. within the past 18 months.
As for Ikey Mo’s friend, Hancock, who
fritters away his time and money (so he says) in
punting, there’s no disguise about his blatant incon-
sistency. In the first place, Hancock said “the
totalisator would extend the area of gambling, though it
might decrease its volume.” Now, fully 75 per cent, of
the betting, mostly small betting, that occurs in Mel-
bourne is in connection with the little proprietary-club
meetings of which Hancock is a habituC. Good. “ The
present bill,” says Hancock, “contemplates an injustice
to the proprietary racing clubs round Melbourne.”
Therefore Hancock opposes the Bill. He knows that it
would settle the hash of these accursed every-day-in-the-
week gambling places, and yet he has the impudence to
declare that it would “ extend the area of gambling.”
The glaring absurdity of such anti-Tote vaporings by
legislators who are personally acquainted with Ikey Mo is
indeed edifying.
One thing is certain. If the Turner Govt, has
the interests of Young Victoria at heart it will appoint a
fair and square Commission of Enquiry into the Betting
Evil, and the result will surely be a Govt. Totalisator
Bill. The Turner policy has been to stand aside and
wink at the annual failure of Jack Murray’s measure,
which this year was discussed one night in August, and
couldn’t be discussed again for the next three months
because it was merely a private member’s bit of business.
Some of the anti-tote allegations put forward
by Frank Madden and John Hancock for the preservation
of their friend Ikey Mo in Victoria are worth quoting and
commenting on. Said Frank Madden: “The totalisator
would increase the evil of gambling, as it had done every-
where. . . . According to the annual report of the
S. A. J.C., there was a continuous shrinkage in its revenue.
The decrease was attributed to the falling off in the
receipts for the totalisator.” Gambling evil increases as
tote receipts decline. Rats 1 But said Hancock, flatly
contradicting his Tory ally in this bad cause : “ Without
the totalisator Adelaide racing was at a low ebb, now it
was booming, and Morphetville was second only to
Flemington as a racecourse.” The one man opposes the
tote on the ground that it is detrimental to big
racing clubs and the cultivation of high-class horseflesh.
The other man affirms that it has brought prosperity to
the Adelaide racecourses. Again, Madden said : “ Under
the totalisator, racing clubs would spring up mushroom-
fashion all over the country, as they had done in South
Australia, but the stakes would grow smaller and
smaller.” What are the facts re racing in Victoria, where
the tote isn’t, and in S.A., where the tote is? The Argus,
which issues a racing calendar from its office, states that
“ the number of racing clubs in Victoria is about 65, in-
cluding clubs which would not be licensed to use the
machine.” The object of the Tote Bill is to kill these
clubs which would not be licensed. A mushroom
growth of new' clubs is an impossibility, as Madden
must know. And now, what says Miller’s carefully
compiled “ Racing Chronicle ” for last year ? In Victoria
there were race-meetings at 145 different places, as many
as 12 meetings in the year being held at some of these
“ pitches.” In South Australia the number of racing
“pitches” was 42 only, and the greatest number of
meetings held at any one course was four. The two
principal clubs—the S.A.J.C. and the Adelaide R.C.—
were responsible for four meetings apiece, and of these
four meetings one in each ease was a tw’o-days’ fixture,
and one other extended over three days. Each club
compressed five out of seven days’ racing per annum into
two important meetings. The S.A.J.C., whose stakes,
according to Madden, “grow smaller and smaller"
(whereas Hancock states that the club has gradually
arrived at a “ booming ” condition since the tote was in-
troduced some ten years ago), supplied 30 races in its five
principal days last year. The total amount given in
stakes was £6480. Average value of the stakes, £216.
And The Bulletin herewith challenges Frank Madden to
prove that he isn’t a mischievous misleader of the public.
The S.A.J.C. at its principal meetings, last year, actually
gave more stakes : money than in 1896. He can refer to
his “ Racing Chronicle,” and see for himself.
* » * •
If the tote were legalised in Victoria—“ The
bookmaker might be removed, but he would not be
killed. He would become the owner,” roars John Han-
cock, M.L.A. Whereas Frank Madden vows that no man
will be able to support a stud of racehorses when there
are no bookies to back them with. Owners must get the
cream of the betting-market, or perish. Therefore Ikey
Mo, having become an owner (as Hancock predicts) will
slowly and surely perish. What a fascinating argument
in favor of the Tote 1
» * • *
The progress of racing in South Australia with
the totalisator : S.A.J.C., in ’93-’94, paid in stakes £6744 ;
in successive years since, £7242, £7717, £8162, £8165
compare, for the same period, the Vic. figures—and the
club’s net profits have allowed £21,000 to be spent on im-
provements—against which, by the way, owners have
something to say. Mr. C. H, T. Hart, growling on their
behalf at the annual meeting, urged, but apparently
without much result, that the public and members should
be given a rest, and the owners sweetened with a little
more prize-money.
• * • •
Whilst Frank Madden was talking balderdash
to the effect that the tote in Victoria would be the ruin
of first-class horse-racing, Walter Hickenbotham (the
trainer of Mentor and Carbine) and Alec Taylor, who
trains for more than one “bloated capitalist,” were
busily engaged in spreading the gospel of the tote.
* * * *
Frank Madden, in Vic. Assembly, opposed the
Tote Bill because “ the machine would militate against
the production of thoroughbred horses in Australia ” :
That sentiment is all “ mv eye.”
The “ books ” who bleed the masses
Believe ’twill stop the grand supply
Of thoroughgoing asses.
* * * *
A great number of parsons and bookmakers are
arguing that the Totalisator has produced increased
racing in S.A. Considering that before it was re-enacted
a few years ago racing was practically dead in S.A. this
statement is true. The machine is now, however, oper-
ated under rigid regulations and police supervision, and
only clubs with a certain membership are allowed to work
it. In the old days it was run by private speculators for
their own benefit, hence its abolition. Nowadays tote
profits are devoted to improvements on courses and in-
creased stakes, and S.A. owners have time and again pro-
THE LUKE OF MANCHESTER.
nounced against a return to the old bookmaker regime.
Never was racing so decent in S.A. as to-day.
* * * «
“ Though a punter of 40 years’ standing, I
strongly oppose this Bill, because I believe it will increase
gambling. My boys do not go to the racecourse, for they
say father can lose enough for the whole family.”—(Han-
cock, M.L.A., in Tic. Assembly.)
Not for the first time The Bulletin asks who is
Rev. Joseph Nicholson, of Geelong, and where does he
get his wildly exaggerated statistics of tote receipts?
Rev. Joseph shows such remarkable enthusiasm in pub-
lishing tommy-rotten arguments against the Totalisator
Bill every time it is brought forward in Victoria, as to
excite a public interest in him and his flock, and all that
appertaineth unto the good man. Any parson, or other
professional pietist who conspicuously attacks the tote,
lays himself open to the suspicion of having been con-
verted to the wrong faith by Ikey Mo. Is Geelongeezer
Nicholson one of these unhappy converts, and why doesn’t
somebody teach him that the tote which he condemns is
good in the sight of the Lord ? The Bulletin has begun
to tremble for the soul of all kerristians who shut their
eyes to terewths concerning vicious Ikey Mo and the
virtuous machine, and this journal trembles particularly
for the immortal future of “Rev. Joseph Nicholson,
Parsonage, Geelong.”
• • * •
“ Temperance ” Vale, of the Victorian Alliance,
had his foolish say in opposition to the Tote, and the cold-
water clique passed a resolution alleging “that State
sanction of a new form of betting would extend the evil,
and not reduce it.” Argument is wasted on these arro-
gant saints. They simply refuse to learn that the Tote
is Ikey Mo minus Ikey’s blasphemy, bad habits, and ex-
tortionate profits. That it is a means of substituting oc-
casional harmless gambling (as far as the g.p. are con-
cerned) for habitual pernicious punting. The saints
persist in their obstinate ignorance of these facts. They
deny any Truth which doesn’t lend itself to snuffle, so to
speak. In their vindictively moral desire to damn the sins
they’re not inclined to, they are not likely to favor a
machine that would discount the force of an old-estab-
lished curse.
* * » •
“ Not once or twice, but nearly always so,
The machine’s ‘ divvy ’ beats the price of Mo.”
V.R.C. meeting at Flemington (Vic.), last Saturday ; start-
ing prices of winners were : 7-4, 7-2, 5-4, 4-1, 2-1, 3-1;
whilst, same day, at A.R.C. meeting, Adelaide (S.A.), the
dividends were : £5 195., £2 11s., £ll 155., £7 155., £3 25.,
£26 17s.—which pan-out muchly in favor of the machine.
* * ' • »
Two noteworthy winners at the V.R.C. meeting
last Saturday were Caledonia and Dreamland. The three-
year-old had been whispered as a “dead-bird” for a race
at Caulfield two or three weeks previously, but was
scratched at the last moment. Saturday’s performance
bore out the prediction that Caledonia would certainly
win the next time he wasn’t scratched.
Dreamland didn’t score a straight-out win, bub
the handsome old deceiver ran a dead-heat with La Cara-
bine in quite brilliant style. This coming after dozens of
failures—which were always attributed to a weakness of
constitution rather than a want of gameness—suggests
that Dreamland may even now do something worthy of
his long-ago reputation. But The Bulletin will not be-
lieve it possible until it Is actually accomplished.
• • • • • •
After all, it is a waste of time to talk about the
Cup chances of the Victorian horses until some of them
have had their “form” tested at Randwick Spring
meeting. Last year the Sydney horses did as they liked
when they reached Melbourne.
* * * •
One of the best wins on Pat’s Grand National
was that of a cherubic priest, who takes his wins and
losses with the same easy good humor. In which he
differs from a sporting brother of the cloth, who was a
client of an erstwhile tipster, and who, after parting
with about £6OO, got restive, and wrote to the sharp
one, threatening law. The tip at once prepared a letter
to the shepherd’s ecclesiastical superior, stating the facts
of the case, and sent a copy thereof to the irate sky-pilot,
who, on condition the original was not forwarded, con-
sented to settle the matter out of court.
* * * *
Whatever the premises, the vicarious backer
will never deduce an inference likely to act as a deterrent,
Kelly Maitland is generally credited with all the wisdom
of the Evil One, yet Vindictive wins three times in sue-
cession unbacked by his owner. And, instead of realising
from this that horses are not machines readily adapted to
the working of the betting market, punters wink the
other eye and ask one another knowingly what “ R.K.”
has up his sleeve. Once he has enthroned an ovvner on
the altar of cleverness, the backer never admits the
possibility of mistakes on the part of his idol. And this
is just why the backer “falls in ”so frequently. Starting
with the assumption that all men—racing men, of course
—are full of guile, his calculations are upset by the few
honest owners, and the mistakes of others, who are
“ honest ” but “ clever."

llie cable news that Albert irott had put up a
good, but not singularly brilliant, batting and bowling
performiuicc for Middlesex v. Leicestershire was an un-

* * • *
Printed that an English pro. bowler had stated
that, in his opinion, Albert Trott was the worst bowler
who ever made a reputation. Shortly after he had to
face Trott, and was handed a paper which he was asked
Mil his innings. Trott’s first ball
hulled him in the binjie, the second he struck wildly at
and missed, the third rattled his stumps. Returned to
the pavilion, he read, “ Trott wins £5 if he hits you with
the first ball, makes you miss the second, and bowls you
in first over. What about T.’s reputation, now ? ”
• * * *
“ It will gladden the heart,” writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent, “ of many an Australian
barracker to hear that ‘ Mr.’ Storer, dogged bat and
dodgy wicket-keep, brought back such a reputation from
his trip ‘down under’ that his rival ‘pro.,’ Lilley, is
getting all the chances of making for himself a better
one. Much to the disgust of the Derbyshire man, Lilley
has been picked to take the sticks for the Players v.
Gentlemen, the North v. South, and Sloddart’s Team v.
England-left. Storer is just such a ‘ pro.’ as ‘ W.G.’
would have been had he not been hampered by the desig-
nation of ‘amateur,’ but lacks the big man’s portly
cunning to conceal his savageness when umpires are
‘ agin him.’ Than this ultra-sharp stumper there is no
more serviceable bat in the two cricket continents, and
he holds the fifth position in the average-list with 4570
for 20 attempts—Lilley has 38-46 for 13 innings, but
sportsmanlike temper must be served.”
• • a •
Clearly our old friend, Storer, didn’t pick up
any bowling wrinkles during his Australian trip. The
cable reports a prodigious score by little Brown and big
Tunnicliffe against Derbyshire, which is Storer’s county.
We can almost assume that a good many of Brown’s 400
runs were got by boundary-smitings of the pugnacious
wicket-keeper’s slows. When he made his big, decisive
innings in the final test-match of Stoddart’s '94 team, he
was absurdly rough on Giffen, who was bowling “ tripe ”
on that particular morning ; sometimes with intent, but
toujours “ tripe,” anyhow. Brown is a thorough little
bull-dog for “ tripe.”
* * • •
The Golden Gate athletic club, Sydney, brought
off a successful set-to on Saturday night when Tweedie
met Ritchie. Ritchie’s gameness proved of no avail
against Tweedie’s cleverness, and the latter won in a
little over two rounds.
• • * •
Perhaps, out of rough and raw Kanowna, there
there isn’t in Australia any town about which this could
be printed : —“ The well-known prize-fighters, W. Doherty
and the two Duggans, arrived yesterday. They were met
at the station and driven in a drag to Soanso’s hotel,
where they were welcomed to the town. Over a glass of
wine the Mayor made some appropriate remarks.” Yet
how soon these towns get out of that way ! Already
Coolgardie, which only a year or two ago would have
done quite the same thing itself, except that there might
have been a drag apiece for the visitors, now lampoons
Kanowna’s Mayor unmercifully.
* * * *
Percy Cavill has done well in England since
joining the professionals. On Friday last he won the
850yds. professional championship at St. Ives, S. W.
Greaseley again finishing behind him.
• * • •
Dawson-Mannock billiard exhibitions fell flat
in Melbourne at the Athenaeum Hall, and again at
Menzies’ Hotel. Dawson, whose general resemblance to
Dot Boucicault is noticeable, plays a fine, all-round game,
but, after all said and done, the little ’un is a tame per-
former in comparison with John Roberts. He hasn’t the
same mastery over the balls, doesn’t bring them together
for nursery cannoning with Roberts’s unerring ease and
delicacy, and when he has them well in hand he certainly
fails to persuade the experienced public that he could
equal Roberts with the “push ” barred. The champion,
last time he visited Australia, made some wonderful
sequences of legitimate cannon touches, free from any
suspicion of push—and made them faster than Dawson’s
top rate of scoring. Every way he was the more inter-
esting showman. He held his audience. The game, as
Dawson and Mannock play it, doesn’t impress unskilled
spectators, and men stroll out of the room to get a drink
after waiting for half-an-hour to see Dawson score
about half as many points as Roberts would put up in the
same time. Also, these wearied ones sometimes forget to
come back again. The entertainment is not sufficiently
exciting. Dawson, taking one instance, will concede 225
to 750 to Mannock, and the biggest breaks will be 101 by
Dawson and 107 by Mannock —good billiards, hot not
astounding.
* * * *
Lawyer Hutchison’s Restraint of Wagering Bill,
to be considered by M.L. Parliament, is a drastic measure,
likely to be altogether knocked out or utterly emascu-
lated. Under it, anyone sending letter, telegram, or
advt. to another re quotations, and thereby “ inciting to
wager,” is liable to £lOO fine or three months. The
street-penciller who “ loiters ” for a living is to be simi-
larly treated ; also, any “ loiterer” may be run-in with-
out warrant. There are other consequential provisions
which infringe the liberty of the subject, and one con-
cerning stakeholders, who, though they pay over stakes
to winners, must return deposits to losers, if the latter
demand it.
• • • •
Another attempt is to be made this session to
legalise consultations in M.L. Heaps of money drift
across to “ Tatt.,” and Lawry, M.H.R., thinks that M.L.
might just as well have its own horse-lottery. He pro-
poses that sweep-promoters should be licensed (cost £25);
Hancock’s offspring do not bet
With Ikey Mo on ’osses,
They view with undisguised regret
Their punting parent’s losses.
“ Alas 1 ” perchance, they groan aloud,
“ He’s backed another duffer,
He drops the stuff for all the crowd,
And we have got to suffer.
“ His pants are quite a shocking fib,
His hat is very seedy,
Through ‘ doing in his little bit,’
The old ’un’s always needy.
He might afford a smarter coat
(Because he’d save some money)
If he would vote for Murray’s Tote,
And yet he don’t. It’s funny 1
“A spectacle for men and gods,
At pony-meets he poses—
The patron of starvation odds,
The friend of Ikey Moses.
His policy seems rough on us ;
At first it was bewild’ring,
But Dad explains the matter thus—
He wants to warn his children.
“ We never venture on a course ;
The Turf we’d scorn to trample :
We hate the very name of Horse—
The fruits of Dad’s example.
And w’hen we mention the machine,
It makes him awful 1 shirty,’
For racing with the Tote is clean,
And he prefers it dirty.”

It has been discovered in M.L. —more particu-
larly in Wellington districts (writes “ Steffi Cassander ”
to Tub Bulletin) —that a wholesale forgery of totalisator
tickets has been going on during the present year, with
very aggravating loss to those concerned. At a recent
Masterton race-meeting £6O was paid away in divs. on
forged tickets ; and at Hawera, Wellington, and also in
Auckland, divs. have been paid out considerably over the
sum allotted for same. The forgeries have been so per-
fect that the tote, or racing authorities, have never yet
been able to drop on to a holder. A “ protest ” div. was
purposely delayed at, the last Wellington winter meeting
till next day so that the clerks could examine tickets more
carefully, as they feared a protest would give the forgers
ample time to put in their work, but there were none
submitted. At present it is absurdly easy to forge tote-
tickets (they are all of one design in plain black-and-
white); the only difficulty is in the rubber stamp. The
tickets bear a differently-designed rubber stamp for each
horse and race, and it is asserted that the operators must
carry round with them a very complete and up-to-date
rubber-stamp plant, and that their operations are
probably conducted in an enclosed cab. Of course,
they must have trouble in getting hold of a
winning ticket, but they get one all the same-
perhaps they invest on every horse. At all events,
they have never yet been caught, and as they are only
working for comparatively small sums, they are not
likely to be. It has also been discovered that tote people
have been hitherto very careless re stocks of blank
tickets (tickets before the rubber-stamp impression has
been made), and it is possible the forgers may have stolen
these from some hotel or grand-stand before a race-
meeting, probably at some country place where hundreds
of them have been carelessly stacked, and the tote people
do not seem to knovv the exact quantities they start with,
as in spite of each ticket being numbered there is always
a lot to spare, go far, this matter has been kept out of
the papers.

The Irishman, W. J. M. Newburn, who at pre-
sent holds the world’s record at broad jumping, stands
6ft. 6in. In three successive jumps he cleared well over
23ft.
