There are evidently a few good athletic parsons
yet at’Ome. Recently Rev. W. G. Lutyens ran 900 yds.
in 2min. 0 2-ssec. (beating W. G. George’s amateur record
by 1 l-ssec.), and in doing the 1000yds. in 2min. 14 5-ssec.
he beat Rev. W. Pollock Hill’s record by the small margin
of lsec.
* * * *
The great Rugby football game has sadly de-
generated in M.L., bashing exhibitions being a conspicu-
ous feature of most contests this season (writes a Bulletin
correspondent). The papers now publish a list of wounded
after “scientific” expositions, just as they do after a
railway disaster or a Yanko-Spankee naval battle. In one
interprovincial leather-matinee lately two were knocked
right out, one skipper had his nose broken, and two
others were stunned and ambulanced for repairs. The
best back of another travelling combination heavily
st.oushed an opponent, and was passed out for a year. A
f#w others have been similarly treated in other centres;
one bold bad basher boastsof a two years’ sentence, and an-
other is disqualified from ever entering a football ground.
Three brothers were lately disabled—one eye, one arm,
one leg in bandages. At meals two had to sit together :
one held the fork, the other steered the knife, the father
lectured on the brutality of football, whilst brother No.
3 winked the other eye.

in running would come as a relief after a nauseating
course of cycle-fake.

“ Punter ” to The Bulletin :—“ Apropos your
recent article. The Vic. Tote Bill is intended as a turf-
reform bill, and not as an Act for the Encouragement of
Gambling. Consequently the Bill proposes to boycott
proprietary clubs, for several reasons. In the first, place,
proprietary racing clubs are run for the benefit of one
man, or, perhaps, half-a-dozen men, instead of for the
benefit of the property and the sports who race horses
thereon. Flemington would not have become the most
perfectly appointed show-place in the world if the
V.R.O. were a private concern conducted by two or
three men for their own profit. To grant the Tote
to proprietary clubs would be to swell the incomes of
the men who run them, and encourage the kind of racing
which good sportsmen would gladly suppress altogether.
Rotten little meetings would be run on the tote. To per-
mit the use of the tote for more than four days at anyone
meeting would be to promote race meetings where the
same horses would, in many cases, be raced on six succes-
sive days. Seeing that no Australian meeting up to date
has ever been extended over more than four days, and
that everybody, except the professional gamblers, reckons
that a four days’ meeting is one day too many,
there can be no objection to the limit clause in
the Tote Bill. The Bill, if passed, will wipe out
proprietary clubs, and thereby confer a great boon upon
the misguided shop-boy who is lured into backing
the eronk gee-gees which run at those pestilent gather-
ings. It will make the horse-game a cleaner and better
game and reduce the gambling spirit in Victoria. If Tub
Bulletin admires the gambling spirit and is in favor of
sticking totes all over the place to be worked by rogues
in the interests of villainy—or even to be worked by the
Govt, in the interests of charity at the expense of a
hugely-increased Gambling Spirit—then the Tote Bill is
bad from a Bulletin point of view. Racing men consider
it good in all its clauses. It isn't being introduced
‘ humbly and apologetically, and on a small scale,’ but
quietly and confidently, and on a scale calculated to serve
the very best interests of the public in general. There
now 1 ”
* • • *
Politicians nowadays make a great bid for the
“Young Australia” vote, and though it is considered
that this is a purely federal vote, it is just as well to be
on the safe side. A second-class Sydney Eastern suburbs
cricket club has, for the coming season, “ elected ” 20
vice-presidents at (say) one guinea each— including not
only the six surrounding M’sP., but most of the recently-
defeated candidates as well.
* * * *
“Tic-tacking,” or telegraphing the odds from
the paddock to the Leger, which was ukased out of
existence at Randwick, is not interfered with by the
English racing authorities, and the “ silver bookies at
Newmarket or any leading English racecourse gladly pay
an expert tic-tacker £6 a week. “ Directly the numbers
go up for a race,” says an English authority,, and the
betting on the event opens, the duties of the tic-tac man
commence. First, he hauls madly at his left ear, then
repeats the operation on his right, and finishes up by
tweaking his nose vigorously three times. Calming down
somewhat, he stands listening to the cries of the book-
makers around him, but suddenly, without warning,
begins once more to ill-use himself, by slapping his right
cheek, jabbing himself under the chin, and smacking his,
by this time, perspiring brow in the manner of a falsely -
accused hero of melodrama,”
* * * *
The apotheosis of the pug ! Four exponents of
' the noble art, not finding sufficient appreciation in Perth,
moved camp to Kanowna. They were met by the Mayor,
and invited to champagne in the council-chamber.
There his worship welcomed them on behalf of the citi-
zens of “ this prosperous and go-ahead city” in “a few
well-chosen phrases.” One of the four professors, with
an eloquence born of numerous newspaper defiances, re-
sponded to these complimentary attentions in “ suitable
terms.”

The first public speech made by a N.S.W.
cycling champion was at a dinner given in his honor by
Victorian wheelmen. Praise had been lavishly bestowed,
and every one agreed that the cyclist would be able to
best even Zimmerman, then a power in the world. When
he stood to reply, cheers and shouts of “ Good boy (say)
Billy,” greeted him on all sides. Billy glanced apprehen-
sively about him, and at last remarked, “Oh, nark it,
you’s blanky Victorians!" Brief, unique, and to the
point.

A Maitland (N.S.W.) man, moving a vote of
confidence:— “l have much pleasure in moving
that Mr. Blank, a man, as you know, of refine-
ment —er —and of—er—er —learning and of—er—
ability and a thorough gentleman, is a fit and
proper person to represent ” (getting annoyed at
the behaviour of the other man’s supporters)
“ this rotten, larrikin-ridden, loafing hole filled
with the worst gang of cut-throats under the
glorious canopy of Heaven.” But the man of
refinement, &c., won’t represent above hole in
this Parliament.
Within a few years Brisbane has given
birth to (and buried) four Democratic weeklies —
Boomerang, Chronicle, Tribune and Echo.
The first, by far the most brilliant, was murdered
by extravagant management—it paid too heavily
for its brilliancy; the second died of frequent
change of editorial control and organised adver-
tisers’boycott ; the third -run by Vosper, of W. A.,
—never took life seriously, and had only four thin
and eccentric issues ; and the last, although above
the average in patches, became so erratic in policy
and feeble in funds that its last issue was about
the most satisfactory event in its existence. Yet,
with these four dried specimens before him, the
writer, if he were a bloated capitalist, anxious to
increase his pile, would sooner sink his lucre in an
ably-conducted Brisbane radical weekly or daily
than in the best present show in the market.
“ VonKotze I read A. Meston’s opinion
of Lionel Johnson, Byron’s detractor, with much
relish, not to say admiration. I always admire the
Sacred Ibis. He provides that element of Rococo
humor that seems to have gone out of fashion in
the higher circles of society with the reformation
of Don Quixote, and is now to be found only
secreted away in the box of Matilda Jane, the
cook, and read surreptitiously by the light of the
slush-lamp to the weeping Mary Ann, maid of
all work. A sort of chaste and sentimental
mediaeval Deadwood Dick, and extremely rare.
Also extremely shallow and polysyllabic. Some
of Mr. Meston’s highflown and trancendentally
virginal love stories I cherish among my most
valued treasures even unto this day. And when I
feel low-spirited, I read them over again, and
courage returns. A community that lets Meston
live and write, cannot be utterly callous and
hardhearted, and impossible to exploit. But in
this instance, I fear me, Mr. Lionel Johnson will
derive much unholy credit out of the Great Croco-
dile’s sneers. If Lionel aforesaid had known the
Mighty Protector, he could have boiled down his
criticism to the remark, that Mr. Meston is an
admirer of Byron. Which classifies Byron at
once. On the other hand, it explains the poet’s
wonderful success, and promises immortality to
him while there are Mary Janes on this matter-of-
fact planet.

Westralia had 550 miles of State-owned
railway at June, ’95, while by May, 1898, it had
1269 miles completed and a good deal under
construction. Westralia has a mile of State-
owned railway for every 133 inhabitants, and, in
proportion to population, it is the most-railwayed
State in the world.

“F.P.”: Re moon-rainbows. On the
evening of 3rd Aug., from 7.30 to a little after
eight, Melbourne was treated to a pretty instance.
The moon was sunk in a golden sphere about
twice its own diameter, and round this was a
ring showing the usual rainbow colors. This ring
was about six moons dia., and, though a complete
circle, was somewhat ragged. The sky was clear
at the tiine, but ; a little after eight, clouds drifted
up from the North, and, though the moon was
still visible, both rainbow-ring and golden sphere
disappeared.
He couldn’t be safely left alone with a
ten-ton anchor in the middle of Sahara.” — Politi-
cian’s epitaph.
“ Victor ” : The Bourgogne owners lost
another gigantic steamer, the Ville de Havre, in
much the same way, over 20 years ago, with a
parallel frightful loss of life. No Cunard ship-
wreck has ever cost a life, because—according to
Bache Cunard, “ we never employ cheap men.”
What price the Bourgogne crew ! Probably, how-
ever, the owners are gainers, except in injury to
prestige.

HEBE BASMUSSEN’S
Mission in the Herbal Itforld.
Herr Rasmussen has devoted his whole life to
the study of medical herbs and manufacture of
Herbal remedies, and his great mission has been
most wonderfully successful.
The great virtues of the Herr Rasmussen s Alfa-
line Herbal Remedies are so well known that it is
quite unnecessary to say much in regard to them ;
the very fact of their having been before the pub-
lic for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and maybe seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented in
the History of Herbal Remedies.
Herbal Remedies are gradually pushing their
way forward, entirely taking the place of those
remedies which contain mercury, arsenic or strych-
nine. . ,
The Australian press has fully recognised the
noble mission of Herr Rassmussen, and praise
bestowed upon him from this important source has
not only been most whole-hearted, but also very
generous. Space will not permit to repeat one-
tenth of his best press notices, but few will suffice
to show the public the estimation in which Herr
Rassmussen is held by the Australian press.-
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common?” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of testimonials to that
effect.

When President M‘Kinley was examining
some of the armour-plates of the burst-up Maine,
he found an unbroken human jawbone embedded,
immovable, in the solid chilled steel. That in-
vincible Yankee jaw !
Zangwill, the litterateur and ex-school-
teacher, has been telling a Jewish interviewer
that he attributes all his success to “ sheer indus-
try.” But what is success? As the result of
overwork, Zangwill suffers from depression of
spirits, and is always seeking health in change of
scene.
Prof. Marshall-Hall is to finish his aca-
demic year ; then he gets a year’s leave of
absence; then he resigns his Melb. University
chair of music. It is his own way out of the
difficulty, whatever influences made him take it;
and probably his Ego will be all the better for the
separation.
Cigarette smokers and their name is
legion choose the “Three Castles’’ before any
other. Why ?. Because of their fragrance and
purity. This is abundantly proved by the large
sales of this favorite brand.*
When Marshall-Hall first took charge of
Melb. music he astounded all his pupils by setting
as a question at the initial exam. —“ 1. What is
the difference between the sublime and the beau-
tiful?” “2. State the theories that have from
time to time been promulgated differentiating the
sublime and the beautiful.” The students sat
dumbfounded ; but, reckoning to make up lost
marks in the practical exam.', they trooped into
the next room. “Strike E Flat,” demanded the
Prof, of the first candidate. The trembling youth
obeyed. “No-o-o-o-o-o,” yelled the irate musi-
cian, “ I want a deep orange, not a pale yellow.”
The Bulletin hardly needs to apologise
for directing attention in another column to
Hoyle’s prints advertisement. Every—or nearly
every—lady purchaser of print goods knows that
Hoyle’s is one of the largest firms of calico
printers in the world. It has been established
over 100 years. Hoyle’s prints, so long famed for
their quality and guaranteed fast colors, can be
procured at all drapers in the colonies.*
“M‘Gr.”: Asked whether he did not
consider a certain novel immoral Oscar Wilde
replied, “It is worse—it is inartistic,” and
those are my sentiment's in the Marshall-Hall con-
troversy. A large number of the Prof.’s sup-
porters instance lines from “ Queen Mab,” “Songs
before Sunrise,” “Romeo and Juliet,” the Bible,
and other works of poetical fiction, as far more
wicked than the doggerel of their idol, and seem
to think that this clinches the argument. But
how fallacious is such a comparison ! A nude
picture by the great Lefebvre is indecent to no-
body but the professional prurients of the
W.C.T.U., but a nude picture drawn on the back
wall by a precocious twelve-year State-schoolboy
is quite another thing.
A PROSPEROUS INDUSTRY.
The yearly output of Ogden’s Guinea Gold
Cigarettes, if added lengthwise, would exceed a
distance of 5000 miles.*
A Sydney hair-artist, with a foreign name,
publicly complains that he has been called in the
press by the “barbarous expression” of barber —
“ which is an insult to myself and children.” The
gentleman wishes to be known as “hairdresser,
music-seller, or tobacconist.” Seems quaint; yet
Dickens, in “ Master Humphrey’s Clock,” lets a
character voice precisely the same objection—“l
would suggest that barbers is not exactly the kind
of language which is agreeable and soothing to our
feelings. ... I believe there is such a word in the
dictionary as hairdressers .” How has the mighty
barber-surgeon fallen !
Hannibal Rowe, the hero of a recent
Bulletin yarn, died at his native town of Castle-
maine last week, owing to a peach-stone sticking
in his throat. Rowe it was who, being offered
several thousands for his share in a Fryers-creek
claim, refused to deal on the ground that he had
no trade, and as he had a “ stiddy job ” in carting
mullock away from the property he wasn’t going
to take the risk of being stranded. In vain his
three brothers—partners in the mine—pointed out
that his share would yield more in Govt, deben-
tures than he ever made out of carting mullock ;
Hannibal was obdurate, and the sale fell through.
That was 30 years ago, and poor Rowe has re-
mained to work his property ever since, changing
at last by means of a peach-stone to even a steadier
job than that of mullock-carting.

“Although Messrs. Gould, Sydney Smith
and .Garrard have practically retired from the
Ministry, it is not intended to gazette their
resignations till Thursday, in order that they may
share with their old colleagues in the honor of
having broken the record of N.S.W. Ministers for
the length of term of office.”—(S.M. Herald,
Aug. 16). Compare this with the dismissal of
civil-servants who had but a few months to serve
to become entitled to pensions ! Should Premier
Reid be beaten on a direct vote of want of confi-
dence in the present session of Parliament, he will
probably retain office till next year in order to
break the record for the greatest number of Xmas-
days in the life of any Ministry.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
The N.S.W. police superannuation-fund
should receive instant attention at the hands of
Parliament. Every day the subject stands where
it does adds a fresh injustice. Constables have
paid 2\ per cent, of their salary till they became
sergeants, and sergeants till they became inspec-
tors, under the official bond that on attaining 60
years of age they would be able to retire on a
pension equal to their last salary. Every man
over 60—and there are 120 on the roll—willing to
retire is justly entitled to this pension, but, willing
or not, he must stay at his post for the reason that
the pension-fund is bankrupt. This is not only
unjust to the men primarily concerned, but to the
rank and file, who might reasonably look for pro-
motion on the retirement of their seniors.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*
When Premier Reid passed incog, (but
very much cog.) through Narandera ; the other
day, the maddest man in the district was the
Mayor, who wa,s away on his selection, and missed
the great occasion. He made up for it, however,
by calling a meeting at the council-chambers for
the purpose of banqueting the already-banqueted.
His Worship faithfully attended the meeting, and
dismissed a “representative attendance,” consist-
ing of himself and the lamplighter. Five shillings
isn’t so easily raised in Beerandera.
A. Gr. Downer, S.A.’s best-known squatter-
lawyer, lately told the local Pastoral Commission
that he did not know half-a-dozen men who had
made a success of the province’s purely pastoral
country. Price Maurice, he declared, was “out ”
over .£lOO,OOO on Mt. Eba; W. J. Browne,
.£90,000 on Newcastle Waters ; T. Elder, between
<£40,000 and <£50,000 on Mt. Burrell and Owen
Springs ; Wooldridge and co., .£20.000 on Bar-
row’s Creek ; P. Levi and co. .£60,000, and W.
K. Simms <£30,000, on Moolooloo ; Scammell, over
£50,000 on Ediacara. The Elder estate, Peter
Waite, Mortlock, Andrew Tennant, and W. H.
Duncan were mentioned as about all who had
thrived on the way-back country.

There are but three Westralian Q.O.’s :
Sept. Burt, late Attorney-General; S. H. Parker,
now travelling in England, who was beaten while
trying to enter the Assembly from the Council, to
become leader of the Opposition against the
Forrest gang ; and Geo. Leake, the pantomimic
leader of the Opposition, who would have given
way to Parker, had the latter not been defeated.
The snub received by the Government in the
Council at the hands of R. S. Haynes, in the
shape of the latter’s amendment of the address in
reply, appears to have had its origin in the fact
that Haynes (a brother of John Haynes, of
Mudgee) was not “given silk.” When elected
Haynes was a qualified Government supporter,
but long: waiting for “silk,” or else office, has
turned him into the leader of the Opposition party
in the Council. The Government, however, is
unconcerned, since it “ does not hold office at the
pleasure of the Leg. Council.”

R. GILPIN, Esq. Melb., July 28, 1898.
Dear Sir,
At your request I visited the Birthday United
Company’s property, situated at Berringa, about
miles from Staffordshire Reef, and beg to sub-
mit the following report :
The property consists of a 30-acre Lease and
three shafts have been sunk on the property. The
present working shaft is sunk to a depth of 60
feet, and drives are being driven North and South
on the course of the Lode, the former a distance
of 12 feet, and the latter 11 feet, both in nice
laminated stone. Lode 12 inches in width but
making larger as it goes down.
North of the above, a shaft was put down 25
feet and a drive driven 40 feet with a lode 12 to
18 inches in width and nice gold showing. This
drive is connected with the present working shaft
and gives good ventilation.
140 feet North of the above, a shaft is sunk to a
depth of 80 feet, and close timbered ; it carried a
nice lode 18 inches in width. All the stone in this
shaft, I am informed, went 12 dwts. per ton, and
improves as it goes deeper. North of the above a
distance of 40 feet the lode is outcropping on the
surface 16 inches in width, and showing gold. Up
to the present date, 67 tons have been crushed for
a yield of 37 ounces 3 dwts., and the present pro-
prietors are putting through a 50 ton crushing
which they estimate will go 1 ounce per ton. This
is exclusive of pyrites, of which the stone carries a
large quantity. The gold is of good quality,
worth £i 2s. 6d. per ounce. Firewood can be had
on the Lease, and can be obtained and stacked on
the mine at 6s. per cord. There is also abund-
ance of mining timber, and 100 yards below the
shaft, a good site for a battery is available.
Taken into consideration the easy nature of the
ground on both sides of the lode, the stone can be
mined at a very small cost.
In conclusion, I am of the opinion that this pro-
perty is a second Birthday, and the stone being
similar to that Company’s, I think it is on the
same line of reef, and can confidently recommend
it as a good mining investment.
Yours respectfully,
JAMES DURSTON, Mine Manager.
REPORT ON BIRTHDAY UNITED MINE.
The mine is situated between the birthday and
Jubilee Mines, the two best mines in the district.
It is held in a 30-acre lease. There is one shaft
down 80ft., also a drive driven 40ft., from which all
stone taken has gone a little over 12dwt. to the
ton. There is another shaft 140 ft. further south,
which is down 60ft. and the stone is improving in
width and gold. The stone in this shaft prospects
between 15dwt. and an ounce to the ton. There
is also a drive in 50ft., it is driven north towards
the 80ft. shaft; there is good payable stone from
one end to the other, and the stone is still going
north. Between these two shafts there is another
down 25ft., and the stone is from 12 to 18in. wide,
the ground is of such an easy nature that so far all
work done has been done with pick and gad.
ALFRED SPARKS.
Kangaroo, 28th July, 1898.
The Directors notify that they have secured all
the stone at grass for the company, so that crush-
ing can be commenced forthwith.

Mr. Woolnough, chaplain to Tas. House
of Assembly, lives a bachelor-life at old Port
Arthur, where he owns some property. He has
no servant, and boils his billy and broils his chop
in heavenly isolation. He bought the old Model
Prison from Govt., and set up his drawing-room
in the chapel, where he gave some swagger
suppers. Also, he used to entertain lady visitors
by shutting them up in the dark cell, and alto-
gether made his guests enormously enjoy them-
selves. But he was burnt out, and now he hermits
at the other end of the town, in a cottage of his
own.
Mary Grant, who died at Manly, Sydney,
the other day, at the age of 86, welcomed
Victoria and Albert to Belfast, Ireland, in 1847,
by christening in public her twin children Albert
and Victoria. The Queen waved her handkerchief
over the celebration, a fact that Mrs. Grant glowed
over the rest of her life. She and her husband
came to N.S. Wales soon after. Mr. Grant has
been 08 years a Freemason, holding, probably, the
Australian record in that respect. At last count
they had 17 children, 87 grandchildren, 78 great-
grandchildren and 21 great-great-grandchildren.
' Dear Bulletin, — lie first attempt to ex-
plore ‘‘Papua.” In ’64 Capt. Mackay (founder
of the now-famous sugar-and-rum district) and
Lieut. Cotgrane, of the late Indian navy, acting
on the suggestion of the late Dr. Lang (then
visiting Rockhampton), made overtures to N.S. W.
Government with the view of obtaining some
assistance in fitting out an expedition to explore
and open up trading relations with that island.
The reply, though warmly applauding the project,
declined assistance and suggested an appeal to
Sir Roderick Murchison, who in England was
drawing attention to Papua's supposed vast auri-
ferous resources. Whether the appeal was made
I never learned ; but Cotgrane’s death shortly
afterwards was doubtless the cause of the scheme
being abandoned.— John S. St. Croix, Gympie.
During the recent N.S.W. election a cer-
tain aspirant was addressing a meeting from a
cart ; his site was just on the bank of a coastal
river 'which flows through the town where he
wasn't wanted. Every few minutes Young Aus-
tralia would come forward and get a mouldy egg
ready for shying by giving it a slight crack on the
wheel of the cart, consequently the eloquent
address was interrupted as many times by “ Hey !
now then just you throw that egg ! ” the press re-
presentatives putting it down as “cheers.” The
meeting was brought to an abrupt end by a large
army of the other man’s barrackers seizing the
cart and running it down the bank into the river,
candidate and chairman included.
| The election of labor-man Holman to
N.S.W. Parliament revives the memory of the
long and acrimonious epistolary duel he had
with Holy Joe Cook during the election of ’94.
The two slung mud at one another with great
vigor, but Joseph compounded with his conscience
by winding up each of his gall-soaked communi-
cations with “ Fraternally yours, Joseph Cook.”
Which called forth these verses in the National
Ass. organ, Plain Talk :
You’re a scoundrel, a traitor,
A disintegrator;
You’re as foul as the filth of the sewers ;
The offspring of Cain ;
And yet I remain,
Dear sir, most fraternally yours.

Mitchell on Women.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ All the same,” said Mitchell’s mate, continuing
an argument by the camp-fire. “ All the same, I
think that a woman can stand cold water better
than a man. Why, when I was staying in a
boarding-house in Dunedin, one very cold winter,
there was a lady lodger who went down to the
shower-bath first thing every morning ; never
missed one ; sometimes in freezing weather
when I wouldn’t go into a cold batli for a fiver;
and, sometimes she’d stay under the shower for
ten minutes at a time.”
“ How’d you know ? ”
“ Why, my room was near the bath-room, and
I could hear the shower and tap going, and her
floundering about.”
“ Hear yer grandmother ! ” exclaimed Mitchell,
contemptuously. “ You don’t know women yet.
Was this woman married ? Did she have a hus-
band there ? ”
“ No ; she was a young widow. ”
“ Ah ! well it would have been the same if she
was a young girl—or an old one. Was there some
passable men-boarders there?”
“/was there.”
“ Oh, yes ! But I mean was there any there
beside you ? ”
“ Oh, yes, there were three or four ; there was
a clerk and a ”
“Nevermind, as long as there was something
with trousers on. Did it ever strike you that she
never got into the bath at all ? ”
“ Why, no ! What would she want to go there
at all for, in that case ? ”
“ To make an impression on the men,” replied
Mitchell promptly. “She wanted to make out
she was nice, and wholesome, and well-washed,
and particular. Made an impression on you, it
seems, or you wouldn’t remember it.”
“Well, yes, I suppose so; and, now I come to
think of it, the bath didn’t seem to injure her
make-up or wet her hair ; but I suppose she held
her head from under the shower somehow.”
“ Did she make-up so early in the morning ? ”
asked Mitchell.
“Yes—l’m sure.”
“That’s unusual; but it might have been so
where there was a lot of boarders. And about the
hair—that didn’t count for nothing, because wash-
the-head ain’t supposed to be always included in a
lady’s bath ; it’s only supposed to be washed once
a fortnight, and some don’t do it once a month.
The hair takes so long to dry ; it don’t matter so
much if the woman’s got short, scraggy hair ; but
with a girl with hair down to her waist it would
take hours to dry.”
“ Well, how do they manage it without wetting
their heads? n
“ Oh, that’s easy, enough. They have a little
oilskin ca,p that fits tight over the forehead, and
they put it on, and bunch their hair up in it when
they go under the shower. Did you ever see a
woman sit in a sunny place with her hair down
after having a wash ? ”
“ Yes, I used to see one do that regular where I
was staying; but I thought she only did it to
show off.”
“ Not at all—she was drying her hair though
perhaps she was showing off at the same time, for
she wouldn’t sit where you—or even a Chinaman
—could see her, if she didn’t think she had a good
head of hair. Now, I'll tell you a yarn about a
woman’s bath. I was stopping at a sliabby-gen
teel boarding-house in Melbourne once, and one
very cold winter, too ; and there was a rather
good-looking woman there, looking for a’ husband.
She used to go down to the bath every morning,
no matter how cold it was, and flounder and splash
about as if she enjoyed it, till you’d feel as though
you’d like to go and catch hold of her and wrap
her in a rug and carry her in to the fire and nurse
her till she was warm again.”
Mitchell’s mate seemed greatly interested.
“ But she never went into the water at all!’
continued Mitchell. “ As soon as one or two of
the men was up in the morning she’d come down
from her room in a dressing-gown. It was a toney
dressing-gown, too, and set her off properly. She
knew how to dress, anyway ; most of those sort
of women do The gown was a kind of green
color with pink and white flowers all over it, and
red lining, and a lot of coffee-colored lace round
the neck and down the front. Well, she’d come
tripping downstairs and along the passage, hold-
ing up one side of the gown to show her little bare
white foot in a slipper, and in the other hand she
carried her tooth-brush, and bath-brush, and soap
—like this —so’s we all could see ’em ; trying to
make out she was too particular to use soap after
anyone else. She could afford to buy her own
soap, anyhow ; it was hardly eyer wet.
“ Well, she’d go into the bathroom, turn on the
tap and shower, and, when she got about three
inches of water in the bath, she’d step in, holding
up her gown out of the water, and go slithering
and kicking up and down the bath, like this, making
a tremendous splashing. Oh, yes, of course, she’d
turn off the shower first, and screw it off very
tight—wouldn’t do to let that leak, you know; she
might get wet; but she’d leave the other tap on,
so as to make all the more noise.”
“ But how did you come to know all about
this?”
“ Oh, the servant-girl told me. One morning
she twigged her through a corner of the bathroom-
window, that the curtain didn’t cover.”
“ You seem to have been pretty thick with ser-
vant-girls ? ”
“ So do you with landladies ! But, never mind-
let me finish the yarn. When she thought she’d
splashed enough, she’d get out, wipe her feet, wash
her face and hands, and carefully unbutton the
two top buttons of her gown, throw a towel over
her head and shoulders, and listen at the door till
she thought she heard some of the men moving
about. Then she’d start for her room, and, if
she met one of the men-boarders in the passage or
on the stairs, she’d drop her eyes, and pretend to
see for the first time that the top of her dressing-
gown wasn’t buttoned, and then she’d give a little
start and grab the gown, and scurry off to her
room buttonin’ it up.
“And sometimes she’d come skipping into the
breakfast-room late, looking awfully sweet in her
dressing-gown ; and if she saw any of us there,
she’d pretend to be much startled, and say that
she thought all the men had gone out, and make
as though she was going to clear ; and then some-
one ’d jump up and give her a chair, while some-
one else said, ‘ Come in, Miss Brown ! come in !
Don’t let us frighten you. Come right in, and
have your breakfast before it gets cold. ’ So she’d
flutter a bit in pretty confusion, and then make a
sweet little girly-girly dive for her chair, and tuck
her feet away under the table ; and she’d blush,
too, but I don’t know how she managed that.
“ I know another trick that women have; it’s
mostly played by private barmaids. That is, to
leave a stocking by accident in the bathroom for
the gentlemen to find. If the barmaid’s got a
nice foot and ankle, she uses one of her own
stockings ; but if she hasn’t, she gets holt of a
stocking that belongs to a girl that has. Any-
way, she’ll have one readied-up somehow. The
stocking must be worn and nicely darned ; one
that’s been worn will keep the shape of the leg
and foot—at least till it’s washed again. Well,
the barmaid generally knows what time the
gentlemen go to bath, and she’ll make it a point
of going down just as a gentleman’s going. Of
course he’ll give her the preference—let her go
first, yer know ; and she’ll go in and accident’ly
leave the stocking there in a place where he’s sure
to see it, and when she comes out he’ll go in and
find it; and very likely he’ll be a jolly sort of
feller, and when they’re all sitting down to break-
fast he’ll come in and ask them to guess what he’s
found, and then he’ll hold up the stocking. The
barmaid likes this sort of thing, but she’ll hold
down her head, and pretend to be confused, and
keep her eyes on her plate, and there’ll be much
blushing and all that sort of thing, and perhaps
she’ll gammon to be mad at him, and the landlady
’ll say, ‘Oh, Mr. Brown ! how can yer? At the
breakfast-table too!’ and they’ll all laugh and
look at the barmaid, and she’ll get more embar-
rassed than ever, and spill her tea, and make out
as though the stocking didn’t belong to her.”
HENRY LAWSON.
LAND OF THE CROCODILE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On his recent visit to the old country T.
Macdonald Paterson, who, strange to say, is a
humorous Scotchman, had with him a walking-
stick made of the timber, known as “ringed
gidya,” by reason of its brown and gold concentric
rings. A certain noble lord greatly admired the
stick and asked, haw, the “meaning of those
wings, dontcherknow ! ” And Paterson gravely
explained to the astonished aristocrat that this
extraordinary timber comes from the great
Queensland desert, that it has a jet-black bark and
snow-white leaves, that the roots go straight down
hundreds of feet to artesian water, that the yellow
rings denote dry seasons, and the brown rings
wet seasons; and that a jet of water, nightly
projected from the centre of the top, sprays all the
foliage like a fountain ! Also, that the tree bears a
plum which is red at night and blue in the day-
time, and that a woman who eats one of these
plums will never afterwards bear children ! The
noble lord has since written to ask if there is
“ any chance of obtaining some of these plums at
any price.”

Be the Crotty will-case. Nobody seems
to have cited the return of the Hartmann estate
to the family by Colonel Olcott on behalf of the
Theosophical Society, to which Hartmann had
willed it. Why not have Government “Execu-
tors of Wills,” who would {see to the equitable
distribution of all estates and guard against what
Carlyle would have called the malodorous phos-
phorescence of pre-mortem terrorism ? Some 20
years ago an irresponsible person named Neild left
<£600,000 to the Queen, who (it is said) handed it
all over to the heirs, discovered after careful
investigation. But let any Church get an
absolutely firm, unchallengeable grip of a bequest,
and a dynamite bomb would not blow a three-
penny-bit out of it.

The following, re Julian Thomas, is from a
Brisbane resident: —“There was a boom among
Victorian shanties in ’6O, and sly-grog detectives
were employed by the Government. I was then
in charge of a police-station in the Avoca district,
and one day a young man, who said his name was
‘Sydney James,’ reported to me that he and
another man had been sent to ‘ stick up ’ the
shanties in my sub-district. They performed that
duty in first-class style. James told me he had
been in the R.N. and the American War. He
went to St. Arnaud, stayed there some years, and
contributed to St: Arnaud Mercury. I recog-
nised his style of writing in the first of the ‘ Vaga-
bond Papers.’ I met Julian Thomas afterwards
in Brisbane, and, after some hesitation, he ad-
mitted being ‘Sydney James.’ C. F. Lewis, of
St. Arnaud Mercury, could say if the con-
tributor who broke his leg and was treated in the
St. Arnaud Hospital was ‘ Sydney James ’ and
the ‘Vagabond.’ ”
* * « *
When Jack Hamilton, M.L.A. (Q.) — as a
member of the Mines Commission of last year—
was examining a Charters Towers doctor
about the effects of carbonic oxide on respiration,
the doctor’s evidence got so tangled up about the
mouse-test that Hamilton sarcastically remarked,
“ A miner, therefore, when going down a shaft
should have a candle in one hand, a mouse in the
other, and a bottle of lime-water under his arm,
and on arriving at the bottom he must keep one
eye on the bottle and the other on the mouse, and
if excess of carbonic acid does not render him un-
conscious, and the light doesn’t go out through
want of oxygen, and he can see the mouse looking
ill through carbonic oxide, he may have time to
escape before that gas overpowers him.”
* * * *
I recently met an old fellow who was one
of Horrocks’ exploring party of 1846. J. A.
Horrocks used the first camel introduced to Aus-
tralia, and that camel cost him his life. He started
to go from Adelaide by the head of Spencer’s
Gulf and north-west from Lake Torrens. When
a month out he was pulling his gun from the pack
of the camel, and the charge exploded, fatally
wounding him.
• * • »
On the 20th of June, while a mob of
150 blacks were camped on the Warrego River,
two men, “King Billy” and “ Cockatoo,” had a
fight to a finish over a dead gin. They fought
with the sharp, heavy gidyea boomerangs of the
west. In a couple of minutes both were down
with terrible cuts in the groin and thighs and in
five minutes more they had bled to death. The
constable who went out found both bodies wrapped
in bark, ready for burial, and had to threaten
with his revolver before he was allowed to examine
their wounds.

HtALTH IS PRICELESS.
Bile Beans for Biliousness

AN ADELAIDE LADY SPEAKS IN HIGH PRAISE OE
BILE BEANS.
COMPLETELY CURED OE BILIOUSNESS AND INDIGESTION.
MEDICAL SKILL INEFFICIENT.
Mrs. Margaret Nelson, who lives in Phi Hip-street, Wolseley (near the Croydon Railway Station),
Adelaide, is one of the latest beneficiaries through the curative properties of Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness. Hearing of the cure, a reporter from the Adelaide Advertiser went to Croydon on Friday
afternoon to inquire personally into the case. Mrs. Nelson at once expressed perfect willingness to
tell her story of suffering, which continued for thirteen long and weary years, until arrested by the
use of only three boxes of the boon to thousands in Australia—Bile Beans.
%
m
"Q
“Tell me,” said the reporter, “the facts connected with
your illness.”
“ Certainly,” said Mrs. Nelson, “ I will do so with pleasure.
Bile Beans have wrought a wonderful change in me. Thirteen
years ago I took ill with biliousness and indigestion. I had
pains in the head, a bad taste in the mouth ; a sickly feeling
would come over me, and if I did not pick my food I would
suffer great pain after eating. The doctors prescribed all sorts
of physic, but it did me no good.”
“ When did you first hear of Bile Beans ? ”
‘ ‘ About ten weeks ago. Although at first, like many others,
sceptical, I decided to give them a trial.”
“ And with what result ? ”
“ Well, look at me. I look healthy enough, don’t I ? And
I assure you I never felt better in my life than Ido now. My
husband knows how ill I was before I took Bile Beans.”
Mr. Nelson, who was listening to his wife’s narration,
humorously assured the reporter that he “just did.”
“My temper,” continued Mrs. Nelson, “ was short, and I
worried a great deal over trifles, and was also very irritable.
You know,” she said confidentially to the interviewer, “that
the ordinary housewife has a lot to worry her, and when her
health is bad it does not make matters any the better.”
“ Bile Beans have been a blessing to you, then? ”
“ Aye, that they have, and I shall always sing their praises.
I now go about my work with better spirit. I never knew during my long illness what it was to be
without pain, but during the past ten weeks I have been a new woman. The doctors told me that I
would never be cured, and naturally I believed them. My appetite used to be wretched before I
commenced using the Beans, but now I can eat almost anything with relish, and do not dread to take
my food.”
Mrs. Nelson has resided in the Croydon district for 12 years, and is very widely known. Her
testimony was given with perfect candor and spontaneity, and her marvellous cure is only a typical
case of the merits of Bile Beans for Biliousness.
BILIOUSNESS.
Biliousness is an ailment caused by a surchase of bile becoming stagnant in the stomach. It is
generally accompanied by indigestion and constipation, vomiting, and a yellow appearance of the
complexion. Dizziness and violent headaches are usual symptoms. The bile is the natural purgative
of the body, and is secreted by the liver ; if it fails to flow in its usual channels, or to perform its
usual duties, the liver is responsible.
A more annoying complaint than biliousness it is hard to find. It prohibits the sufferer from
doing work of any kind, holding a conversation, or even resting quietly ; not only this, but the nausea
is most disagreeable, and the taste and breath are exceedingly offensive. A remedy should be applied
at once that will act in an efficient manner, and the remedy to take is Bile Beans for Biliousness,
which are compounded expressly for ailments of this sort. They are so prepared that they act directly
on the liver, helping it to help itself, and enabling it to secrete enough bile to flush the bowels
thoroughly, and thus clear off all waste and offensive matter from the system. This remedy is well-
known, and is conceded the most effective purgative medicine of the age. It does not afford mere
temporary relief, but an absolute and lasting cure.
+
INFLUENZA AND COLDS.
Influenza is not generally understood by the greater part of those who suffer from it. It is in
reality an aggravated form of cold, supplemented by a general weakness of the whole system. Ordi-
nary colds, it is well-known, are caused by the congestion of the blood, which, as a rule, is most
prominent in the head or throat. Influenza can be traced to the same cause, and irregular or insuf-
ficient liver action is, in most cases, responsible for both. When the liver, through any derangement
of the digestive organism, ceases to act in the proper manner, a certain amount of bile finds its way
into the blood, and, at the same time, the blood created from food consumed is of a decidedly poor
quality. In this manner, the blood becomes congested, the circulation sluggish, and a cold results.
The circulation of the blood now being much slower than usual, the entire system is affected. The
nervous organism becomes unstrung, the appetite poor, and the rest disturbed. The body is extremely
susceptible to chills, and the slightest draught causes a violent attack of coughing or sneezing. Many
so-called cures for influenza fail to get at the root of the trouble, and only afford temporary relief. A
medicine that will keep the liver in thorough and regular working order, and the digestion right, will,
without doubt, prove the most effective preventive and cure for influenza and colds. This is what
Bile Beans for Biliousness will do, and we maintain that no better remedy can be found for these
ailments.

The Molecules.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They made him out of mystery, they filled him
full of life,
They fitted him with intellect to know he didn't
know,
They built him for quiescence, and they fitted him
for strife,
They geared him for rapidity, and braked him
to go slow.
They took a billion ancestors of forty million sorts,
They lumped up all their wishes and their ten-
dencies and creeds,
They soaked him, every atom, in the essence of
all thoughts,
And jammed him full of hunger in his hopes,
and in his needs.
The Hunger of the molecules that teas not satisfied,
The Hunger of the molecules that would not he
denied,
The Hunger of the molecules that plagued him till
he died,
And so found peace —molecular dispersion.
There was Hunger, bitter Hunger, for the good
things of the earth,
All the foods that thrilled the palate, all the
wine that fired the brain,
And the feeding of desire meant the never-ceasing
birth
Of the Hunger of the molecules that clamored
yet again.
And some grew strong and lusty—all the atoms
fiercely fought;
They wrestled, died, or triumphed, as the swift
sensations ran;
And keener was the summons of the Hunger that
they wrought,
And ever more impatient grew the Voices in
the man.
There was Hunger, bitter Hunger, for the joys
and woes of Love,
For the eyes of light unfathomed and the hair
that shines like gold,
For the mystery and magic that excels all else
above,
For the witchery and wisdom that the paths of
passion hold.
And to and fro it tossed him as a leaf before the
wind,
The Hunger, keenest monitor, that needs must
be obeyed :
It triumphed o’er all rivals in the fortress of his
mind,
And laughed at laggard wisdom and the laws
that men had made.
There was Hunger for illusions. For the Mystery
around
Cried out for explanation, and could never be
explained;
And so he made religions and philosophies
abound,
And he lumped into a symbol all the wonder
that remained.
But still they never pleased him overlong, for
doubts would come
And spur to speculation till another creed arose,
For Faith cannot for ever make the molecules be
dumb,
And, evermore resurgent, the Eternal Hunger
grows.
They showered on him Bibles and they smothered
him with tracts,
While hymns and sermons all his life kept up
their awful din ;
And when the moral precepts didn’t square with
solid facts
They put it down to Satan as the father of all
Sin.
They preached to him, they punished him, they
did their level best
To compass his salvation, but they might have
saved their breath,
For he never showed repentance till his Masters
gave him rest,
And that was when the molecules were slowing
down for death.
The Hunger of the molecules, it spins all living
things,
The reasons, hopes, and fancies, and the works
and ways of men.
The tree that spreads its branches, and the bird
that in it sings,
The grass beside the pathway, and the hand
that guides the pen.
It brings man to life’s threshold, and it drives
him on till death,
It leads the blood within him, and it reigns in
brain and nerve,
It moves the sullen muscles, and it ushers every
breath ;
It is the mighty despot that the organisms
serve.
The Hunger of the molecules that is not satisfied /.
The Hunger of the molecules that will not he denied I
The Hunger of the molecules that 'plagues till we
have died,
And found our peace—molecular dispersion!
N.S.W.

The manager of the most fashionable
Bourke-street, Melb., drapery was the other day
the recipient of a lovely present of oranges and
bananas from his tailoresses. Cutting the delicious
fruit, he found the oranges full of buttons and the
bananas stuffed with cotton-wool.
Down to date one “ Atherstone,” working
through Melb.’s old-established legal firm of
Blake and Riggall, has about put up a record
for anonymous charitable gifts. Within the
last six months many Vic. charities and almost
everything of the sort in every Westralian mining
centre and in Broken Hill has had the legal firm’s
cheque for ,£lOO upwards.
Launceston (Tas.) Parliamentary Debating
Society, a recognised training-ground for legis-
lators in the Isle of Bad Dreams, lately resolved—
“ That the House appoint a committee to draw
up an address of condolence to the Prince of
Wales, deeply sympathising with him on the
recent accident to his knee, and congratulating
him on the progress he now appears to be making
towards recovery.”
For the Laughter of the Gods.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The man had met the woman a few times at
friends’ houses, and one evening, the woman’s
husband being absent, he had offered to escort
her home. The night was chilly, and, on the
way, her cloak blew off. He carefully replaced
it, and let his hand rest on her shoulder —for as
long as a thought might pass through the mind.
The woman laughed. “ I don’t think my husband
ever did that, as long as I have known him.”
The man quickly withdrew his hand. They
reached her home, and he opened the gate.
“Shall I knock? or have you the key?” “No,
never mind, thanks. Goodnight.” The woman
held out her hand—and put up her face, and he,
being a man and young, stooped down and kissed
her, then raised his hat and was gone. Upstairs,
the gods sniggered, and made a note of it.
Later on, the man progressed, and the woman
helped him. Later still, the man arranged a
partnership with the woman’s husband, whose
business was large and increasing. Then the
woman grew jealous and suspicious, for the man
came to the house but seldom now, and, when he
did, seemed to give all his attention to the husband
—his partner. Also —though this the woman did
not yet know —there was one other who was in
the man’s thoughts most of all.
Now, the gods, who see all things, most of all
hate a farce, which was why they arranged the
tragedy. For one night, when the man called to
see his partner, the woman saw that a dispute was
impending, and that high words had passed. Late
in the evening she left the room to order supper,
and before she returned she heard the men dis-
puting angrily at the front-door, and one said he
would see his solicitor about it, and the other
replied tauntingly ; then a blow was struck, and
returned; and she reached the door to see her
husband fall heavily, striking his head on the iron
fence. The man carried his adversary inside and
sent for a doctor. And while the unconscious
husband waited the man and the woman looked
at each other, but spoke no word. Only the gods
looked down into the woman’s soul, and were
pleased ; ’twas a soul worth looking into.
The doctor came—and went away ; he could be
of no use now : the woman’s husband was dead.
Then, the dead husband lying there, the woman
went over to where the man stood, and placed her
hands on his shoulders and looked. And the man
looked into the eyes of the woman, and he saw
deep down into her soul—but not to the bottom
—only a woman can ever hope to reach that depth.
What he saw there he told no man, but his face
turned deathly white, and lie flung the woman
from him back to her dead husband, and turned
and left the house.
The next day the man was arrested, and
after the inquest the coroner committed him for
trial for manslaughter. The woman did not
appear before the coroner, she was too ill—which
was not to be wondered at, but her evidence would
be required at the higher court. What that evi-
dence would be depended—as the man knew—
upon himself. So, when the woman sent word
for him to come and see her (he being on bail), he
knew what the message meant, and straight and
true he sent back his answer in an envelope. The
woman opened the envelope and looked at the
card enclosed. Then her face grew grey, as the
face of a defeated devil, and she ground her teeth
in rage as she tore the missive to pieces. For the
card was a small photo, of a girl, a pretty girl
with deep, dark eyes and a happy, joyous look.
And underneath was written, “The girl I am
going to marry.” It was a complete answer. So
at the trial the woman got into the box, and, in a
gentle, grief-stricken voice, told the jury how her
dear husband had been threatened and abused and
finally struck by the prisoner, and that, though
she bore him no malice, the occurrence was little,
if at all. short of wilful murder.
And the gods, who know that a woman will tell
the loveliest lies with a smiling face, were amused,
and wagered among themselves as to whether the
jury would believe ; for even the gods cannot tell
what a jury may do. And the man in the dock
stood quietly, calmly listening. Since that night
when he had looked into the woman’s soul, he
knew what would come, and had braced himself
to meet it. The jury found him guilty, but some
there were who mistrusted the woman, so they
added a recommendation to mercy. And the
Judge, without comment, passed a sentence of two
years’ hard labor.
And when the sentence was pronounced the man
in the dock looked across at the girl—the girl of
the photo ; and the woman watched them both.
The girl arose and walked across and stood in front
of the dock. Then she lifted her veil, and, stand-
ing on tip-toe, raised her face to his.
And the prisoner stooped down, and, before all
the court, took the girl in his arms and kissed her
on the lips. Then, with a happy smile on her
pale face, she passed out of the court, and the
prisoner went below. But the face of the woman
was not good to see, and as she entered her lonely
home she muttered—“ Two years! Two years!
Ah ! I’d give ten years of my life for that one
kiss.”
The gods have not yet been able to agree as to
whether the man or the woman was the greater
fool.
F. CROSON.
Years ago, when the Hare system of voting
was agitatingthepoliticalquidnuncs of Maoriland,
the then Premier (Whitaker), by way of experiment,
invited the Govt, printing-office comps, to decide
by the Hare system the relative popularity of a
number of English poets. The test was con-
sidered unsatisfactory, as the result placed some
minor poets above the immortals.
The other day a Northern mill employd,
who was trying to fix a belt on a wheel with a
piece of wood, had the timber driven deep into his
chest. Received a terrible, ghastly wound, but
recovered. Now for the obverse. Within the
last month a well-known Bundaberg man lost his
life over—chasing a fowl in his yard, when his foot
caught and he fell on a sharp stick which pierced
his stomach and lacerated an intestine. Who
doesn’t believe in fatalism ?
